The Determining Time for Shares: Horseman vs. Foot Soldier
Establishes the Hanafi ruling and rebuts Shafi'i view on ghanimah shares.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for determining a fighter's share in ghanimah (crossing into enemy territory), explain the Hanafi rational proof, and compare it to the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Entitlement to a horseman's share is fixed at crossing, not battle—practicality over precision.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a key rule in the distribution of booty: whether a fighter is counted as a horseman or foot soldier is determined at the moment they cross into enemy territory, not at the time of battle. This rule has practical consequences and is defended with rational proofs against Imam al-Shafi'i's view. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the laws of ghanimah.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Understand that 'al-madiq' is the entrance to dar al-harb, and 'al-mujawazah' means crossing that barrier. The horseman gets two shares (for himself and his horse), the foot soldier gets one.
These terms are central to the ruling.
2
Grasp the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school holds that a fighter's status as horseman or foot soldier is determined at the moment of crossing (mujawazah), regardless of later changes. This is based on rational proof: crossing is a clear, objective cause leading to fighting, whereas actual fighting is difficult to verify.
This contrasts with Shafi'i who considers the time of battle.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the cause of entitlement is actual fighting (qahr wa qital), so status should be based on battle time. The Hanafi response: fighting is difficult to ascertain for every fighter, so crossing is set as a practical substitute. The hadith of Salamah is explained away as a special grant from the Prophet's personal fifth.
This shows the Hanafi emphasis on practicality (ra'y).
4
Apply the rule to scenarios
The text gives two scenarios: (1) A fighter crosses as a horseman but his horse dies before battle—he still gets two shares. (2) A fighter crosses as a foot soldier but later buys a horse—he gets one share. Apply this to other similar cases.
The rule is consistently applied based on crossing status.

كذا في «النهر»، وقيل: هو البرج الحاجز بين الدارين، بحيث لو جاوزه أهل الحرب دخلوا دار الإسلام وبالعكس.

فإن قلت : القتالُ أمر خفي لا يوقف عليه، فينبغي أن يقام السبب مقامه. قلت : لا تُسلم أنه لا يوقف عليه، فإنّ الشارع رئب كثيراً من الأحكام عليه، كإعطاء الرضخ للصبي والعبد إذا قاتل ، وهذا يدلّ على أنه مما يوقف عليه ، فإنّه لو لم يوقف عليه لم يتعلّق به . حكم. ولو سلمنا أنّ الوقوف على القتال متعدّر أو متعسر، فنقول : الحكم يتعلّق بشهودِ
الوقعة، ونحن نقول : المجاوزة نفسها قتال حكماً ؛ لأنه يلحقهم الخوف بها والحال بعدها حالة الدوام فلا يعتبر بها. وأيضاً الوقوف على حقيقة القتال في حق الكلّ متعسر ؛ فإنّ الإمام لا يمكنه أن يراقب بنفسه حالَ كلّ أحدٍ أنه قاتل أو لم يقاتل، ولا يعتبر إخبار الجند للتهمة، وكذا الاطلاع على شهود الوقعة متعسّر، فتقام المجاوزة مقامه ؛ لأنه السبب المفضي إليه ظاهراً، كذا في «الهداية»(۲) وحواشيها.
(1) ينظر: «الأم» (۷) : (٣٥٤) ، والبهجة المرضية (٥) : (١٢٦) ، ونهاية المحتاج»(٦ : ١٤٨)،
وغيرها.
(۲) «فتح القدير» و«الهداية»(٥ : ٤٤ - ٤٩٥).

كتاب الجهاد / باب المغنم وقسمته
٣٩٥
فمَن دخل دارهم فارساً فنفَقَ ،فرسه فلُهُ سهمان : سهم فارس، ومن دخلها
راجلا فشری فرساً، فله سهم راجل
[1]
[۲]-
(فمن دخل دارَهم فارساً فنفَقَ فرسه : أي مات فشهد الواقعة راجلاً ، فله سهمان : سهم فارس ، ومَن دخلَها راجلاً فشرى فرساً، فله سهم راجل)،
هذا عندنا
[١]قوله : فمن ؛ تفريع على ما مهده من اعتبارِ وقت المجاوزة لاستحقاق سهم الفارس والراجل ، مشيراً إلى مقدار سهم الفارس، وهو أنه ضعف سهم الراجل، سهم له وسهم لفرسه. [٢]قوله : أي مات ؛ ومثله ما لو أخذه العدوّ ، كما في «شرح السير الكبير»، ومثله ما لو قتل فرسَه رجل فأخذ منه القيمة، كذا في «البحر». ولو باعه قبل القتال استحق
سهم راجل.
[٣] قوله : سهم ؛ التنوين فيه للوحدة، وأشار به إلى مقدار نصيب الراجل، وهو أنه يسهم له سهماً واحداً لا أزيد ، وهذا يكاد أن يكون مجمعاً عليه، وكثرت فيه الأخبار، وجاء ما يخالفه، أخرجه مسلم في «باب بيعة الحديبية»: عن سلمة بن الأكوع في حديث طويل، قال سلمة : فلما أصبحنا قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «خیر فرساننا اليوم أبو قتادة، وخير رجالنا سلمة، ثمّ أعطاني سهمين : سهم الفارس، وسهم الراجل، فجمعهما لي جميعاً)، وأخرجه ابن حبان في «صحيحه». وأجاب عن ما يستفاد بظاهره بقوله : كان سلمة الله في تلك الغزاة راجلاً فأعطاه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سهم الراجل لما يستحقه، وإنّما أعطاه سهم الفارس أيضاً من خمس خمسه رسول الله له دون أن يكون أعطاه من سهام المسلمين. انتهى.
وأخرجه أبو عبيد القاسم بن سلام في كتاب «الأموال»، وزاد في آخره: «كان سلمة له قد استنفذ نفاح النبي ، قال عبد الرحمن بن مهدي : فحدثت أحد رواته : به سفیان، فقال : خاص برسول الله له ، قال أبو عبيد : وهذا عندي أولى من حمله على أنه أعطاه من سهمه الذي كان خاصاً به. انتهى.
(۱) في صحيح مسلم (۳) : ١٤٣٣) ، و(صحيح ابن حبان (١٦ : ١٤٠)، وغيرها.
And the 'madiq' is from the narrow passes of the Romans, and what is meant here is the entrance to the abode of war. And according to al-Shafi'i, the time of witnessing the battle is considered. Likewise in 'al-Nahr', and it is said: it is the fortress barrier between the two abodes, such that if the people of war cross it they enter the abode of Islam and vice versa. (1) [1] His saying: 'the time of witnessing the battle is considered' meaning the battle of fighting, and in a narration from him: the state at the conclusion of the fighting and its completion is considered, based on the fact that the cause of entitlement to booty is overpowering and fighting, so a person's state at that time is considered and he is given his share according to his state, whether he is a horseman or foot soldier. And crossing the barrier is a means to the cause, like leaving the house for the purpose of fighting to the abode of war, for it is a means to the cause, and the state of the warrior at that time is not considered by consensus, so likewise at the time of crossing. If you say: fighting is a hidden matter not easily known, so the cause should stand in its place. I say: we do not concede that it is not known, for the Lawgiver has built many rulings upon it, such as giving a small share (radkh) to a boy or slave if they fight, and this proves that it is something known, for if it were not known no ruling would be attached to it. And if we concede that knowledge of fighting is difficult or impossible, we say: the ruling is attached to witnessing the battle. And we say: crossing itself is considered fighting in effect, because fear attaches to them by it, and the state after it is a state of continuity, so it is not considered by it. Also, knowledge of the reality of fighting regarding everyone is difficult; for the Imam cannot himself monitor the state of every single person whether he fought or did not fight, and the soldiers' reports are not considered due to suspicion. Similarly, ascertaining witnessing of the battle is difficult, so crossing is set up in its place because it is the cause leading to it outwardly. As in 'al-Hidayah' (2) and its glossaries. (1) See: 'al-Umm' (7): (354), 'al-Bahjah al-Mardiyyah' (5): (126), 'Nihayat al-Muhtaj' (6:148), etc. (2) 'Fath al-Qadir' and 'al-Hidayah' (5:44-495). Then: Whoever enters their abode as a horseman and his horse dies, he gets two shares: a horseman's share. And whoever enters it as a foot soldier and then buys a horse, he gets a foot soldier's share. [1] [2]- (Whoever enters their abode as a horseman and his horse dies meaning it dies, so he witnesses the battle as a foot soldier, he gets two shares: a horseman's share. And whoever enters it as a foot soldier and then buys a horse, he gets a foot soldier's share.) This is our view. [1] His saying: 'Whoever'; a branching off from what was prepared concerning considering the time of crossing for entitlement to the horseman's and foot soldier's share, indicating the amount of the horseman's share, which is double the foot soldier's share: a share for him and a share for his horse. [2] His saying: 'dies'; and similarly if the enemy takes it, as in 'Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir', and similarly if a man kills his horse and takes the value from him, as in 'al-Bahr'. And if he sold it before the battle he deserves a foot soldier's share. [3] His saying: 'share'; the nunation indicates singularity, and by it he indicates the amount of the foot soldier's share, which is that he is given a single share and no more. And this is nearly consensus upon, and many reports confirm it, and something contrary came, recorded by Muslim in 'Bab Bay'at al-Hudaybiyyah' from Salamah ibn al-Akwa' in a long hadith: Salamah said: When morning came the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The best of our horsemen today is Abu Qatadah, and the best of our foot soldiers is Salamah.' Then he gave me two shares: the horseman's share and the foot soldier's share, and combined them both for me. And it was recorded by Ibn Hibban in his 'Sahih'. And he answered what is understood from its apparent meaning by saying: Salamah, may Allah be pleased with him, was a foot soldier in that campaign, so the Messenger of Allah gave him the foot soldier's share for what he deserved. He only gave him the horseman's share as well from the fifth of the fifth of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), not from the shares of the Muslims. End. And it was recorded by Abu 'Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam in 'al-Amwal', and added at its end: 'Salamah had saved the life of the Prophet.' 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi said: Then I narrated it to one of its transmitters: to Sufyan, who said: It was specific to the Messenger of Allah. Abu 'Ubayd said: This is in my view more appropriate than taking it to mean he gave him from his own share. End. (1) In Sahih Muslim (3:1433), and Sahih Ibn Hibban (16:40), etc.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal ruling and proof regarding the distribution of war booty (ghanimah) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically focusing on the shares due to horsemen (فارس) and foot soldiers (راجل). The text establishes that the determining factor for entitlement to a horseman's share (two shares: one for the fighter and one for his horse) or a foot soldier's share (one share) is the time of crossing into enemy territory (المجاوزة or المضيق), not the actual time of battle as held by Imam al-Shafi'i. 

The passage begins by defining 'المضيق' as the entrance to the abode of war, a barrier fort separating the two territories. It then contrasts the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i view: the latter considers the time of witnessing the battle or its conclusion. The Hanafi argument is that the cause (سبب) of entitlement is overpowering and fighting, and crossing is a means (وسيلة) to that cause. Just as leaving one's house for jihad is a means not considered for determining shares, so too is crossing; what matters is the state at the time of engaging the cause. 

The text then refutes potential objections. First, it argues that fighting is not entirely hidden because the Lawgiver has attached rulings to it (e.g., giving a small share to a boy or slave who fights). Second, even if knowing the reality of fighting is difficult, the ruling is attached to witnessing the battle, and crossing is deemed to be fighting in legal effect (حكما) because it induces fear. Moreover, ascertaining who actually fought is impractical for the Imam, so crossing serves as a clear, objective indicator. 

The second part of the passage applies this principle to two scenarios: (1) If a person enters as a horseman but his horse dies before the battle, he still gets two shares because his status was fixed at crossing. (2) If a person enters as a foot soldier but later acquires a horse, he still gets only one share. This is the Hanafi position (عندنا). The commentary further explains that the horseman's share is double the foot soldier's, and that the foot soldier's share is singular (no more than one). It also notes that if the horse is taken by the enemy or its value is taken, the ruling is the same; but if the horse is sold before battle, the person becomes a foot soldier retroactively? Actually, the text says 'if he sold it before the battle he deserves a foot soldier's share' – meaning the sale nullifies his claim to the horseman's share? The commentary clarifies that the ruling is based on the time of crossing, so if he crosses with a horse, he is a horseman; but if he sells it before the battle, does that change? The text says he gets a foot soldier's share, which seems to be an exception? Actually, careful: The passage says: 'ولو باعه قبل القتال استحق سهم راجل' – 'if he sold it before the battle, he deserves a foot soldier's share.' This is consistent because the time of crossing is the criterion, but selling before battle might indicate he is no longer a horseman? However, the principle seems to be that if he crosses as a horseman but later sells his horse, he becomes a foot soldier? But the text earlier said crossing determines it. There is a nuance: The ruling applies to the time of crossing; but if he no longer has the horse at the time of distribution, he may not get the additional share? The commentary is not explicit, but the author is deriving from the base text. The main point is that the Hanafi school adheres to the crossing criterion, while the Shafi'i school uses the time of battle. The passage also addresses a potential conflicting hadith where Salamah ibn al-Akwa' was given two shares despite being a foot soldier. The Hanafi response is that this was a special grant from the Prophet's personal fifth, not from the general booty, so it does not contradict the established rule.

Key Terms
المضيق (al-madiq): The narrow pass or barrier fort separating the territory of Islam from the territory of war; the point of crossing into enemy land.
سهم الفارس (sahm al-faris): The share of a horseman in the booty, which is two shares: one for the fighter and one for his horse.
سهم الراجل (sahm al-rajil): The share of a foot soldier, which is one share.
المجاوزة (al-mujawazah): The act of crossing the barrier (المضيق) into enemy territory; the determining factor for share entitlement.
شهود الوقعة (shuhud al-waq'ah): Witnessing the battle; the time at which the fighter's share is determined according to Imam al-Shafi'i.
الرضخ (al-radkh): A small, discretionary share given to fighters who are not entitled to a full share, such as minors or slaves, if they participate in battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what determines whether a fighter receives a horseman's share or a foot soldier's share of the booty?
His status at the moment of crossing into enemy territory (al-mujawazah).
His status at the actual time of battle.
His status at the conclusion of the fighting.
His status at the time of distribution of the booty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school fixates share entitlement at crossing (al-mujawazah) for practicality, while Shafi'i considers the time of battle; the rule is consistently applied even if the fighter's status later changes.

Cavalry Shares in Ghanima: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cavalry shares with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on the cavalryman's share in ghanima, identify the hadith evidence for each, and explain why Abu Yusuf's three-share view is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The faris receives three shares: one for himself and two for his horse.

The division of war booty (ghanima) is a detailed area of Hanafi fiqh. While all schools agree a cavalryman gets more than a foot soldier, the exact number of shares is debated within the Hanafi school itself. This passage presents the evidence from Imam Abu Hanifa (two shares total) and Imam Abu Yusuf (three shares total), with a clear preference for the latter based on stronger hadiths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The dispute is about how many shares a cavalryman (faris) deserves in ghanima. Both agree the foot soldier gets one share. Abu Hanifa says the faris gets two (one for himself, one for the horse). Abu Yusuf says he gets three (one for himself, two for the horse).
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's evidence
Abu Hanifa relies on hadiths stating 'the cavalryman gets two shares and the foot soldier one'. These include narrations from Mujamma', al-Miqdad, al-Zubayr, A'isha, and Ibn 'Umar. However, many of these hadiths are criticized as weak or defective.
Ibn al-Qattan declared one chain defective due to an unknown narrator.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's evidence
Abu Yusuf cites stronger hadiths from al-Bukhari and Muslim that state 'the horse gets two shares and its owner one'. These give the faris three shares. Narrations from al-Daraqutni, al-Bazzar, and Ibn 'Abbas also support this view.
Al-Zayla'i notes that the two-share hadiths are mostly weak and contradict the stronger narrations.
4
Evaluate the strength of evidence
The hadiths for the three-share view are from the most authentic sources and are more numerous. Abu Hanifa's argument that 'an animal should not be preferred over a man' is a qiyas-based objection, but it does not outweigh the naqli evidence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical difference
Abu Yusuf's view is the relied-upon position because his evidence is stronger. The practical difference is one share per cavalryman: under Abu Hanifa a faris gets 2 shares, under Abu Yusuf he gets 3.
Later Hanafi authorities like al-Zayla'i support the three-share view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفارس فاختلف الأخبار في سهمه : فمنها ما يدل على أنه ضعف سهم الراجل ، فسهم له وسهم لفرسه، فأخرج أبو داود عن مجمع في حديث طويل ، قال : قسمت خيبر على أهل الحديبية، فقسمها رسولُ الله على ثمانية عشر سهماً، وكان الجيش ألفاً وخمس مئة ، فيهم ثلاثمئة فارس، وأعطى الفارس سهمين، وأعطى الراجل سهما. قال أبو داود : وهذا وهم، إنما كانوا مئتي فارس، فأعطى الفرس سهمين، وأعطى صاحبه سهماً. انتهى. وكذلك رواه أحمد والطبراني وابن أبي شيبَةَ والدارقطني والبيهقي والحاكم، وأعله ابن القطان بجهل حال أحد رواته، يعقوب بن مجمع. وأخرج الطبراني عن المقداد رضي الله عنه : «أنه أسهم له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يوم بدر وكان فارساً سهمين لفرسه سهم وله سهم». وأخرج الواقدي في «كتاب المغازي عن الزبير رضي الله عنه قال : «شهدت بني قريظة فارساً، فضرب لي بسهم، ولفرسي بسهم». وأخرج ابن مردويه في «تفسيره» : عن عائشة رضي الله عنهما : «إنّ رسول الله أعطى الفارس سهمين والراجل سهما في غزوة بني المصطلق». وأخرج ابن أبي شيبة رضي الله عنه عن أبي أسامة وابن نمير عن عبيد الله عن نافع عن ابن عمر : «إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل للفارس سهمين وللراجل سهماً». ومن طريقه أخرجه الدارقطني في «سننه» وقال : قال أبو بكر النيسابوري هذا عندي، وهم من ابن أبي شيبة ؛ لأنّ أحمد بن حنبل وعبد الرحمن بن بشر وغيرهما رووه عن ابن نمير خلافه. فهذه الأخبار استند بها أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه في قوله : إنَّ للفارس سهمين، وقال : لا أفضل بهيمة على رجل، لكن قال أبو يوسف في كتاب «الخراج»: «ما ورد من الآثار والأحاديث : إنّ للفرس سهمين وللرجل سهماً أكثر من ذلك وأوثق، والعامة عليه». انتهى. وذكر الزيلعي وغيره : إن أحاديث كون السهمين للفارس أكثرها معلولة وضعيفة، ومخالفة لما هو أقوى منها، وهو ما أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وأصحاب السننِ إلا النسائي : عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما: «إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل للفرس سهمين، ولصاحبه سهما». وفي رواية للبخاري : «إنه قسم يوم خيبر للفرس سهمين وللرجل سهما». قال نافع أحد رواته : إذا كان مع الرجل فرس فله ثلاثة أسهم، فإن لم يكن له فرس فله سهم. وأخرج أبو داود عن ابن أبي عمرة عن أبيه قال: «أتينا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أربعة نفر ومعنا فرس، فأعطى كلّ إنسان منها سهما وأعطى الفرس سهمين». وأخرج الطبراني والدارقطني عن أبي رهم قال : شهدت أنا وأخي خيبر ومعنا فرسان، فقسم لنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ستة أسهم ، للفرسين أربعة ، ولنا سهمين. وفي سنده ضعف، ذكره الزيلعي. وأخرج البزار والدارقطني عن المقداد : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطى الفرس سهمين، ولصاحبه سهما». وأخرج إسحاق بن راهويه عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما قال : «أسهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للفارس ثلاثة أسهم وللراجل سهماً».
As for the cavalryman, the reports differ regarding his share: some indicate that it is double the share of the foot soldier, so he gets a share and his horse gets a share. Abu Dawud narrated from Mujamma' in a long hadith: 'Khaybar was divided among the people of al-Hudaybiyya, and the Messenger of Allah divided it into eighteen shares. The army numbered one thousand five hundred, including three hundred cavalry. He gave the cavalryman two shares and the foot soldier one share.' Abu Dawud said: 'This is a mistake; they were only two hundred cavalry. He gave the horse two shares and its owner one share.' End. Similarly, Ahmad, al-Tabarani, Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Hakim narrated it, and Ibn al-Qattan declared it defective due to ignorance of one of its narrators, Ya'qub ibn Mujamma'. Al-Tabarani narrated from al-Miqdad (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Messenger of Allah allotted him on the day of Badr, while he was a cavalryman, two shares: a share for his horse and a share for him.' Al-Waqidi narrated in Kitab al-Maghazi from al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'I witnessed [the battle of] Banu Qurayza as a cavalryman, and he assigned a share for me and a share for my horse.' Ibn Mardawayh narrated in his Tafsir from A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The Messenger of Allah gave the cavalryman two shares and the foot soldier one share in the expedition of Banu al-Mustaliq.' Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from Abu Usama and Ibn Numayr from Ubaydullah from Nafi' from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah made for the cavalryman two shares and for the foot soldier one share.' From this chain, al-Daraqutni narrated it in his Sunan and said: Abu Bakr al-Naysaburi said: In my view, this is a mistake from Ibn Abi Shayba because Ahmad ibn Hanbal, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Bishr, and others narrated it from Ibn Numayr differently. Abu Hanifa relied on these reports in his statement that the cavalryman has two shares, and he said: 'I do not prefer an animal over a man.' However, Abu Yusuf said in his book al-Kharaj: 'The reports and hadiths that state the horse has two shares and the man has one share are more numerous and reliable, and the common people act upon it.' End. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned that the hadiths stating two shares for the cavalryman are mostly defective and weak, and contradict what is stronger, namely what al-Bukhari, Muslim, and the authors of the Sunan except al-Nasa'i narrated from Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Messenger of Allah made for the horse two shares and for its owner one share.' In a narration by al-Bukhari: 'He divided on the day of Khaybar, giving the horse two shares and the man one share.' Nafi', one of its narrators, said: 'If a man has a horse, he gets three shares; if he has no horse, he gets one share.' Abu Dawud narrated from Ibn Abi 'Amra from his father: 'We came to the Messenger of Allah, four men with a horse, and he gave each man a share and gave the horse two shares.' Al-Tabarani and al-Daraqutni narrated from Abu Ruhm: 'I and my brother witnessed Khaybar with two horses, and the Messenger of Allah divided for us six shares: four for the two horses and two for us.' Its chain has weakness, mentioned by al-Zayla'i. Al-Bazzar and al-Daraqutni narrated from al-Miqdad: 'The Prophet gave the horse two shares and its owner one share.' Ishaq ibn Rahawayh narrated from Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them): 'The Messenger of Allah allotted for the cavalryman three shares and for the foot soldier one share.'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage discusses the share of the cavalryman (faris) in the division of war booty (ghanima), presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. The author first cites several hadiths indicating that the faris receives two shares (one for himself and one for his horse) and the foot soldier (rajil) one share. These hadiths are narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, al-Waqidi, Ibn Mardawayh, and Ibn Abi Shayba, though some have been criticized for weakness or confusion in the number of cavalry. Abu Hanifa relies on these reports to support his position that the faris gets two shares total, arguing that an animal should not be preferred over a man. However, Abu Yusuf, in his work al-Kharaj, counters that the hadiths giving the horse two shares and the man one share (so the faris gets three shares) are more abundant and authentic, and that this is the practice of the general public. To support Abu Yusuf, the author then cites stronger hadiths from al-Bukhari, Muslim, and others that give the horse two shares and the rider one, making the total three shares for a cavalryman with a horse. Al-Zayla'i notes that the hadiths giving only two shares to the faris are weak and contradict these more reliable narrations. The passage ends with a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas that explicitly gives the faris three shares and the foot soldier one, appearing to favor Abu Yusuf's position. The text does not explicitly declare which view is mufta bihi, but the weight of evidence and later commentary leans toward three shares for the faris.

Key Terms
فارس (fāris): A cavalry soldier who fights on horseback.
سهم (sahm): A share or portion of the spoils of war (ghanima) allotted to a soldier.
راجل (rājil): A foot soldier who fights on foot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, how many shares does a cavalryman (faris) receive in the division of ghanima?
A) One share
B) Two shares
C) Three shares
D) Four shares

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a cavalryman receives three shares in ghanima: one for himself and two for his horse.

The Horseman's Shares in Booty: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute over horseman's shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the horseman's shares in ghanimah and evaluate the evidence for each view.

Core Idea
The horseman's share in booty is either two or three, depending on which hadiths are deemed stronger.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: how many shares does a horseman receive in the division of ghanimah? Abu Hanifa held two shares (one for self, one for horse), while his two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argued for three shares. We will explore the hadith evidence and the rational arguments, and see which position became the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the views of Abu Hanifa (two shares) and the Sahibayn (three shares).
Abu Hanifa's view: one share for the rider, one for the horse. Sahibayn: one share for rider, two for horse.
2
Examine the hadith evidence for each
List the key hadiths cited: Abu Hanifa relies on reports giving the horse two shares and rider one; Sahibayn rely on reports giving the horseman three shares (e.g., Ibn Umar, practice at Khaybar).
Pay attention to the narrators and strength of chains.
3
Analyze the rational arguments
Understand Abu Hanifa's principle of not preferring an animal over a man, and the Sahibayn's argument that the horse's combat effectiveness is double.
The Sahibayn use a qiyas-like reasoning based on benefit.
4
Understand the tarjih methodology
See how later authorities weigh the conflicting evidence: the three-share hadiths are stronger (from Bukhari and Muslim), and the two-share hadiths are weak or defective.
Al-Zayla'i and the author of Al-Hidayah discuss the weakness of Abu Hanifa's hadiths.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi school follows Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, giving three shares to the horseman.
This is now the operative position for fatwa.

They supported it by reasoning that entitlement to a share is through sufficiency and effectiveness, and in the horse it is double that of the man, since the man is only for stability, while the horse also benefits in attack and flight when needed, so its share is double the share of its rider. It is mentioned in Al-Hidayah and others for preferring the school of Abu Hanifa, the gist of which: since the actions of the Prophet are contradictory, one resorts to his statement: his saying 'for the horseman two shares and for the foot soldier one share.' Also that the principle is: when two proofs conflict and reconciliation is impossible, one turns to what is after (the stronger?), not to what is above; and speech is stronger than action. And that resorting to another evidence besides the two conflicting ones is only when there is no way to combine them and no means of preference. In our case, the narrations of three shares for the horseman are preponderant in ways not hidden to the skilled, so one should act upon them without need to go to another evidence. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the number of shares a horseman receives in the division of booty (ghanimah). Abu Hanifa holds that the horseman gets two shares: one for himself and one for his horse, based on hadiths that give the horse two shares and the rider one. He also argues against preferring an animal over a man. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i hold that the horseman gets three shares: two for the horse and one for the rider, supported by hadiths that give three shares to the horseman and rational argument that the horse's effectiveness is double that of a man. The passage presents the evidence for both sides. Abu Hanifa's view is supported by some hadiths, but the commentator notes that many of those hadiths are weak. The opposing view is supported by stronger hadiths from Bukhari and Muslim, and by the reasoning that the horse contributes more to combat. The text then discusses the methodology of overcoming contradictory proofs. Some argue that when actions of the Prophet conflict, one turns to his statements, and there is a statement giving two shares. However, that statement is also weak. The stronger narrations favor three shares. The commentary from Al-Hidayah attempts to prefer Abu Hanifa's view but is rebutted. Finally, a note about al-Shafi'i's position: his ruling depends on the state of the fighter at the time of battle, not on ownership of a horse. This passage shows the complexity of hadith interpretation and the use of rational arguments (istihsan) in evaluating proofs. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school today follows Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, i.e., three shares for the horseman, but the text leaves room for differing views.

Key Terms
فارس (fāris): A warrior who fights on horseback in jihad.
راجل (rājil): A foot soldier, one who fights on foot.
سهم (sahm): A share or portion of the spoils of war.

The later Hanafi school, following Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, holds that a horseman receives three shares in ghanimah: two for the horse and one for himself, due to the horse's enhanced combat effectiveness.

The Horseman's Share in Spoils: Abu Hanifa vs. the Majority
Dispute over horseman's shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the horseman's share, explain the opposing view's proof, and evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal using hadith criticism.

Core Idea
The horseman's share is disputed: Abu Hanifa says two, the majority says three.

The division of spoils (ghanimah) among participants in jihad is governed by prophetic practice, but the exact shares for foot soldiers and horsemen have been debated. This lesson examines the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the majority of scholars (including his own students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, as well as al-Shafi'i) on how many shares a horseman deserves. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh conflicting hadith and apply usul al-fiqh principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's view: a horseman receives two shares and a foot soldier one share. Note the proof he relies on.
The proof is a reported statement of the Prophet ﷺ.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
The majority (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, and others) hold that a horseman gets three shares. Their hadith reports show the Prophet giving a horseman three shares and a horse two shares.
They also use rational argument: the horse provides double the benefit.
3
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
Abu Hanifa's supporters argue that when actions conflict, the Prophet's statement takes precedence. The majority rebut that the two-share statement is weak, and the three-share reports are stronger.
The text mentions al-Zayla'i detailing the weakness.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
The commentator leans toward the majority view, stating that the three-share reports are preponderant and need no resort to a secondary proof.
Consider why the majority view is considered stronger.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In practice, if Abu Hanifa's view is used, the horseman gets 2 shares (plus 1 for horse? Actually the text earlier: Abu Hanifa gives horse 2 shares, horseman 1? Wait, consistent: Abu Hanifa gives horseman 2, foot soldier 1, horse 2? The passage is about horseman shares. The thamarah is the total distribution differs.
The practical difference affects actual allocation in spoils.

As for according to al-Shafi'i (1) And Ahmad narrated from al-Zubayr: 'The Prophet ﷺ gave al-Zubayr a share and his horse two shares,' and similar was narrated by al-Daraqutni from Jabir, Abu Hurayra, Sahl ibn Abi Hathma, and al-Tabarani from Umar, and there are other reports on the topic. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the number of shares a horseman (فارس) and a foot soldier (راجل) receive in the division of spoils. The majority of scholars, including Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, al-Shafi'i, and others, hold that a horseman receives three shares and a foot soldier one share. They base this on several hadith reports indicating that the Prophet ﷺ gave a horseman three shares and a horse two shares. They also reason that a horse provides double the benefit of a man—both for steadfastness and for attack/retreat—so its share is double. Abu Hanifa, on the other hand, holds that a horseman gets two shares and a foot soldier one, as reported in some traditions. The commentator presents the argument for Abu Hanifa as found in al-Hidayah: when actions of the Prophet conflict, one should refer to his sayings; there is a saying that the horseman gets two shares. However, this is rebutted by noting that the opposing hadith is weak. The text then provides three reasons to prefer the majority view: (1) the statement (for two shares) is not authentically proven, (2) when proofs conflict, one should resort to the weaker (i.e., action over statement?) Actually the argument is that when two proofs conflict without reconciliation, one uses what is lower (action) not higher (statement), but here the majority says the statement is stronger? The text is complex. The commentator seems to side with the majority. Finally, the footnote clarifies a nuance about the ruling when the warrior's state changes.

Key Terms
فارس (fāris): A mounted warrior fighting on horseback in jihad.
راجل (rājil): A foot soldier in jihad.
سهم (sahm): A share of the spoils (ghanimah) given to a participant in battle.

The principal disagreement on the horseman's share is between two shares (Abu Hanifa) and three shares (majority), with later Hanafi preference leaning toward the majority.

Only One Horse Receives a Share in Spoils
Ruling with proof and internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the share of horses in spoils and evaluate the proof offered by Abu Yusuf for allowing two horses.

Core Idea
Only one horse per warrior gets a share in spoils; other animals and multiple horses are excluded.

In the division of war booty, horses are given a special share because they are prepared for combat. But what if a warrior brings more than one horse, or rides a mule? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on which animals receive a share, the internal dispute within the school, and the evidence from the prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling from the matn
Read the matn 'ولا يسهم إلا لفرس' (nothing gets a share except a horse). The commentator clarifies that this means one horse per warrior.
2
Understand the two implications
The statement excludes both non-horse animals (mules, camels) and multiple horses beyond one.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf argued for two horses based on a hadith from al-Daraqutni. Compare with the majority opinion of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
4
Consider cross-madhhab views
Review al-Shafi'i's reported view and Malik's agreement with the majority. Analyze the critique of the mursal hadith.
Al-Awza'i also accepted the hadith, but historians contradicted it.
5
Recognize the inclusion of horse types
The ruling applies to both Arabian (ʻatīq) and non-Arabian (bardhawn) horses equally.

Book of Jihad, Chapter on Spoils and Its Division: 'And nothing gets a share except a horse.' On the contrary, and the share of the horseman according to him is four shares. (And nothing gets a share except a horse: i.e., one horse. [1] His statement: 'Four shares'; thus in most copies, and it is an error without doubt, and the correct is what is in some copies: three shares, as detailed before. (2) His statement: 'Except a horse'; this statement indicates, as the commentator pointed out, two matters: One: if the warrior rides an animal other than a horse, no share is given for that animal, because what is prepared for fighting for them is the horse, not another animal. Second: if the warrior has two or more horses, only one horse gets a share. And Abu Yusuf said: two horses get shares, but not more, due to that being established from the Prophet ﷺ in a narration of al-Daraqutni from Bashir ibn 'Amr ibn Muhsin: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ gave four shares for my two horses.' And 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Makhul: 'Al-Zubayr attended Khaybar with two horses, so the Prophet ﷺ gave him five shares.' And Imam al-Shafi'i, as al-Bayhaqi narrated from him in the book 'al-Ma'rifah', that Makhul narrated: 'Al-Zubayr attended Khaybar, so he was given five shares.' Al-Awza'i inclined to accept this from Makhul, but it is mursal, and the historians did not report that he gave shares for two horses, and they did not differ that he attended Khaybar with three horses for himself, and he only took one horse. End. And Malik said in al-Muwatta': 'I have not heard of division except for one horse.' End. Then in the statement of the author, by the absolute term 'horse', there is an indication to a third matter: that the Arabian horse, called 'atiq, and the non-Arabian horse, called al-Bardhawn — with a kasrah on the ba.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the share allocated to horses in the division of spoils. The text begins with the matn 'ولا يسهم إلا لفرس' (nothing gets a share except a horse), meaning only one horse per warrior is entitled to a share. The commentator clarifies two points: (1) if a warrior rides a different animal (like a mule), that animal gets no share because only horses are prepared for fighting; (2) if the warrior has multiple horses, only one horse receives a share. There is a correction: the phrase 'four shares' in some copies is an error; the correct is three shares as previously detailed. The text then presents differing opinions: Abu Yusuf holds that two horses can get shares, based on a hadith where the Prophet gave four shares for two horses (thus each horse gets two shares). Al-Shafi'i is reported to have a view that might allow more, based on a hadith about al-Zubayr receiving five shares with two horses, but this is critiqued as mursal and contradicted by historical reports. Malik agreed with the position of only one horse. Finally, the author notes that the term 'horse' in the ruling applies to both Arabian (عتيق) and non-Arabian (برذون) horses, so both types are included.

Key Terms
فرس (faras): A horse used in jihad; only one per warrior gets a share in spoils.
برذون (bardhawn): A non-Arabian horse, a draft or common horse.
عتيق (ʻatīq): An Arabian horse of pure breed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) None
B) One
C) Two
D) As many as the warrior brings

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that only one horse per warrior receives a share in war spoils, based on established practice and the majority of the Companions.

Horse Shares in Ghanimah: One Horse Per Rider
Clarifies which horses qualify for a share in spoils and addresses disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the number of horses that receive a share in ghanimah and evaluate the opposing proofs from Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Only one horse per rider receives a share in ghanimah; all horse types are equal.

In this lesson, we examine a dispute within and outside the Hanafi school regarding how many horses a fighter can claim shares for from the spoils of war. The base text states that a share is given for only one horse, but Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i cite narrations suggesting two horses. We will follow the Hanafi reliance on established practice and the weight of transmission reliability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and Hanafi position
The base text states 'a share is not given except for (one) horse.' The commentator explains this means only one horse per rider, regardless of how many he owns.
This is the majority Hanafi view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Examine the opposing proof (Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i)
Abu Yusuf cites a hadith from al-Daraqutni that the Prophet gave four shares for two horses. Al-Shafi'i cites a report from Makhul that al-Zubayr received five shares for two horses at Khaybar.
These are ahad hadiths with zanni thubut.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that these reports are weak: the hadith is isolated, Makhul's report is mursal, and the consensus of maghazi experts affirms al-Zubayr only received share for one horse despite having three.
Established practice (sunnah 'amaliyyah) outweighs isolated reports.
4
Review positions of other imams
Malik said he knows of no division except for one horse. Al-Awza'i accepted the mursal report, but the majority of scholars including the Hanafis reject it.
The disagreement is across madhhabs as well.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon Hanafi view is one horse per rider. If a fighter has two horses, he still gets only one horse share. The practical difference is the distribution amount.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Second: if the fighter has two horses or more, a share is given only for one horse. And Abu Yusuf said: a share is given for two horses, not for more than two, due to the confirmation of that from the Prophet ﷺ in a narration of al-Daraqutni from Bashir ibn Amr ibn Muhsin, who said: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ gave four shares for my two horses.' Al-Awza'i went to accepting this from Makhul even though it is mursal, and the people of maghazi did not narrate that he gave shares for two horses, and they did not differ that he attended Khaybar with three horses for himself and only took one horse. End. And Malik said in al-Muwatta: I have not heard of division except for one horse. End. Then in the author's statement with the absolute term 'horse' there is an indication to a third matter: that the Arabian horse, also called 'atiq, and the non-Arabian horse, also called al-bardhawn—with kasra on the ba, then a silent ra, then dhal with fatha, then waw silent, then nun. And al-hajin: it is the horse whose father is Arabian and mother non-Arabian. And al-muqrif: it is the opposite of al-hajin. All are equal in deserving the share. Abu Yusuf said in the book 'al-Kharaj': Horses should not be preferred over each other due to His statement in His Book: 'And the horses, mules, and donkeys to ride and as adornment' and 'And prepare for them whatever you can of power and of tied horses to frighten thereby the enemy of Allah and your enemy.' And the Arabs say: 'these horses', and they do not mean thereby the Arabian horse to the exclusion of the bardhawn, and the strong horse is not preferred over the weak, and the brave man with complete weaponry is not preferred over the cowardly man who has no weapon except his sword. End. 11] His statement: and al-rahilah; the camels on which one travels and rides, male or female, and the ta in al-rahilah is for the singular or for transferring from adjective to noun, and the specific for male is 'jamal', just as the word 'naqah' is for female. [2] His statement: 'and they are given radhakh'; i.e., for these mentioned ones. The basis for this chapter is what Muslim narrated: 'That Najdah wrote to Ibn Abbas asking him about a woman and a slave attending the spoils: Did they have a definite share when they attended? So he wrote to him: They did not have a definite share except that they would be given something.' And in the narration of Abu Dawud: 'As for striking a share for them, no, but he used to give them (radhakh).' And in the book 'al-Ma'rifah' of al-Bayhaqi: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ sought the help of the Jews of Qaynuqa', so he gave them radhakh and did not give them a share.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the allocation of shares (suhm) from war booty (ghanimah) for horses. The base text states 'And a share is not given except for (one) horse.' The commentator clarifies two implications: first, if a fighter rides an animal other than a horse (e.g., mule or camel), no share is given for that animal because horses are specifically prepared for combat; second, if a fighter has two or more horses, he only receives a share for one horse. However, Abu Yusuf differs and holds that shares are given for two horses but not more, based on hadith reports. The passage then reviews opinions of other imams: al-Shafi'i accepts a report from Makhul that al-Zubayr got five shares for two horses at Khaybar; al-Awza'i accepts it as mursal; but the experts in military expeditions (ahl al-maghazi) maintain that al-Zubayr attended with three horses but only received share for one. Malik states he knows of no division except for one horse. The Hanafi position is that only one horse gets a share, and this is the relied-upon view. Additionally, the commentator notes that the term 'horse' (faras) is inclusive: Arabian (atiq), non-Arabian (bardhawn), hybrid (hajin), and its opposite (muqrif) are all equal in deserving the share. Abu Yusuf in his book 'al-Kharaj' argues that no preference is given among horses because the Quran mentions 'horses' generally and the Arabs use the word for all types. Similarly, quality of the horse or bravery of the rider does not affect the share. Thus, the share is standardized for one horse per rider.

Key Terms
فرس (faras): A horse, specifically the one used for riding in battle; includes Arabian (عتيق) and non-Arabian (برذون) types.
سهم (sahm): A share from the spoils of war (ghanimah); the horseman receives additional shares for his horse.
برذون (bardhawn): A non-Arabian horse, a pack-horse or workhorse, as opposed to the Arabian thoroughbred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how many horses receive a share in ghanimah for a fighter who owns multiple horses?
A) All horses he rides
B) Two horses
C) Only one horse
D) None, as horses do not receive shares

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on established practice and rejects isolated reports; only one horse per rider receives a share, and all horse breeds are equal.

Radhakh for Non-Combatants in Spoils Distribution
Explains that non-combatants do not receive a full share from spoils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on radhakh for non-combatants and explain the evidence from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
Radhakh is a discretionary token for non-combatants, not a fixed share.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies who receives a full share of the spoils and who receives only a token gift. The source establishes a key distinction: combatants are entitled to fixed shares, while non-combatants accompanying the army are not. Instead, the Imam may give them radhakh, a small discretionary gift. The proof comes from the practice of the Prophet ﷺ as reported by Ibn Abbas and others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the four groups that do not receive a full share: slaves, children, women, and dhimmis.
2
Understand radhakh
Define radhakh linguistically and legally: a small gift, less than a full share (sahm).
Radhakh is not a fixed entitlement.
3
Locate the dalil
Identify the hadith cited: Ibn Abbas’s response to Najdah, and the narration from Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi.
The hadith is found in Muslim, Abu Dawud, and al-Bayhaqi.
4
Analyze the proof
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling: the negation of a known share (sahm ma'lum) except for something given (yuhdha) shows radhakh is the only possibility.
The exception 'except that they would be given something' establishes the practice.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude that the Imam has discretion to give radhakh, but it is not wajib.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(And not for a slave, a child, a woman, and a dhimmi, and they are given radhakh). Radhakh: giving a little, and what is meant here is less than a share of the spoils. Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses the distribution of spoils to non-combatant participants or those who did not fight directly. The base text rules that no full share (sahm) is given to a slave, a child, a woman, or a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). Instead, they are given 'radhakh', which means a small gift or token, less than the standard share of a fighter. The commentator explains the linguistic meaning of radhakh as giving a little. The proof for this ruling comes from multiple hadith: In Sahih Muslim, Ibn Abbas responds to Najdah that women and slaves do not receive a definite share but are given something (yuhdha). In a variant from Abu Dawud, it is explicitly stated that no share is struck for them, but the Prophet would give them radhakh. Additionally, al-Bayhaqi records that the Prophet used the help of the Jews of Qaynuqa' and gave them radhakh, not a full share. This establishes that these categories are not entitled to a portion of the ghanimah as fighters are; rather, the Imam may give them a discretionary gift from the spoils as a reward or encouragement.

Key Terms
رضخ (radhakh): A small gift or token given to non-combatants from the spoils, less than a full share (sahm).
سهم (sahm): A full share of the spoils designated for a fighter (horseman or foot soldier).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what do women, children, slaves, and dhimmis receive from the spoils if they accompany the army?
A) A full share (sahm) like a foot soldier
B) A half share
C) A token gift (radhakh) less than a full share
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Non-combatants such as women, children, slaves, and dhimmis are not entitled to a fixed share of spoils but may receive a discretionary token (radhakh) from the Imam.

Recipients of the Khums: Orphans, Needy, and Wayfarer
Explanation of khums shares and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five recipients of khums according to the Hanafi school and explain the post-Prophet distribution of the Messenger's share.

Core Idea
The khums is divided into five shares; after the Prophet, his share is used for public welfare, and the relatives' share is for poor Banu Hashim.

This passage explains the distribution of the khums (one-fifth of war spoils). After detailing how the remaining four-fifths are divided among the fighters, the text turns to the fifth. The primary evidence is Quran 8:41, which lists six categories, but scholars agree that the share of Allah is a blessing and merges with the Messenger's, effectively creating five shares. The main dispute concerns the fate of the Messenger's share after his death and the precise recipients of the relatives' share. The passage also defines the terms 'orphan' and 'needy' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary textual evidence
Locate the Quranic basis for khums distribution. The passage cites Quran 8:41, which lists six categories. Understand how this verse is the foundation for the ruling.
Quran 8:41 is the key dalil for khums.
2
Note the agreed-upon consolidation into five shares
Recognize that scholars agree the 'share of Allah' is for blessing and is combined with the Messenger's share, resulting in five effective shares: Messenger, relatives, orphans, needy, and wayfarer.
This consolidation is universally accepted.
3
Identify the disputed shares and the Hanafi position
The dispute centers on the Messenger's share after his death and the relatives' share. The Hanafi position is that the Messenger's share goes to public welfare (masalih al-muslimin) and the relatives' share is exclusively for poor Banu Hashim.
Al-Tahawi's works are referenced for detailed discussion.
4
Understand the definitions of 'orphan' and 'needy'
The passage clarifies that 'miskin' (needy) includes 'faqir' (poor), and 'yatim' (orphan) is a child whose father died, but only if poor. Wealthy orphans do not receive khums.
Distinction between miskin and faqir is in the Zakat section.

And the fifth is for the needy and the orphan. He said in al-Hidaya: 'Then the slave...' [from previous paragraph]. His saying: 'And the fifth...' etc.; when he finished about how the four-fifths are divided among the fighters, he began to mention the expenditures of the fifth. The basis for it is His statement: 'And know that whatever you take as spoils, then indeed, for Allah is one-fifth of it and for the Messenger and for the near relative and the orphans and the needy and the wayfarer.' (Quran 8:41) The apparent meaning of the verse rules that the fifth of the spoils is divided into six shares: a share for Allah, a share for the Messenger, a share for the near relatives, a share for the orphans, a share for the needy, and a share for the wayfarer. They agreed that the mention of Allah in it is for blessing, and that the share of Allah and the share of the Messenger, peace be upon him, are one, so the fifth is divided into five portions. They also agreed on the continuance of the right of the orphans, needy, and wayfarers, and that they are recipients of the fifth without doubt. And they differed concerning the share of the Messenger and the share of the near relatives. Al-Tahawi has discussed this research in detail and mentioned their differences along with what they relied upon, and he refuted their statements in the chapter on the share of the near relatives from his book 'al-Siyar' and in the book 'Wujuh al-Fay' and 'Qism al-Ghana'im' from his book 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar.' Whoever wishes to review should refer to it. His saying: 'for the needy' includes the poor, and the difference between them has been mentioned in 'Bab al-Masarif' from the book of Zakat. His saying: 'and the orphan' is a child whose father has died, and he is also a recipient if he is poor, unless he is wealthy due to inheriting wealth that enriches him or other means of ownership.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage explains the recipients of the khums (one-fifth of the spoils). After discussing the distribution of four-fifths to the fighters, the text turns to the fifth. The primary evidence is Quran 8:41, which lists six categories: Allah, the Messenger, near relatives, orphans, needy, and wayfarers. Scholars agree that 'share of Allah' is a blessing and combined with the Messenger's share, effectively five shares. There is consensus that orphans, needy, and wayfarers remain recipients. The main disagreement concerns the share of the Messenger after his death and the share of the near relatives (Banu Hashim). Al-Tahawi is referenced for a detailed discussion of these disputes. The text clarifies that 'miskin' (needy) includes 'faqir' (poor), and the distinction is in the Zakat section. The orphan (yatim) is defined as a child whose father died, and he receives the khums only if poor; if he is wealthy, he is not entitled.

Key Terms
خمس (khums): One-fifth of the spoils of war, set aside for specific categories mentioned in the Quran (8:41).
يتيم (yatim): A child whose father has died; in this context, a recipient of khums only if poor.
مسكين (miskin): Needy person; includes the poor (faqir) in the context of khums.

Condition of Poverty for Dhawi al-Qurba in Khums
Debate on whether Dhawi al-Qurba inherit khums due to poverty or kinship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the entitlement of Dhawi al-Qurba to khums and explain why poverty is a necessary condition, using evidence from the Companions' practice.

Core Idea
After the Prophet, the khums share of Dhawi al-Qurba is only for the poor — kinship alone does not entitle a wealthy relative.

This lesson explores a key point of fiqh in the distribution of khums: are the Prophet's relatives (Dhawi al-Qurba) entitled to a share solely because of their kinship, or is poverty a condition? The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar, holds that after the Prophet's death, the share is reserved for the poor among them. The passage delves into the evidence from the caliphs and addresses objections raised by those who argue that kinship alone suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Determine what the Hanafi school rules regarding the khums share of Dhawi al-Qurba and what condition is attached.
The condition is poverty (al-faqr).
2
Examine the primary dalil
Analyze the narration from Kitab al-Kharaj by Abu Yusuf via Ibn Abbas showing the original five shares and how the caliphs reduced them after the Prophet's death.
The narration is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut but qati' dalalah.
3
Understand the objections and responses
Work through the three objections raised against the poverty condition and the Hanafi responses to each.
The third objection (giving to wealthy al-Abbas) is the strongest; rebuttal uses caliphs' ijtihad.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi, based on the near-consensus practice of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.
Though Ali opposed, the majority practice is relied upon.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference between the Hanafi and opposing views: whether a wealthy relative may receive khums.
In practice, Hanafis do not give to wealthy Dhawi al-Qurba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابن السبيل، وقُدم فقراء ذوى القربي عليهم وابن السبيل"، وقدم فقراء ذوى القربى عليهم المسكين. فإن قلت: لما كان اليتيم لا يستحق إلا بفقره، فأي فائدة في ذكره بعد ذكر المسكين؟ قلت: لدفع توهّم أنّ اليتيم لا حق له في الغنيمة؛ لأنّ استحقاقها بالجهاد، وهو صغير لا يجاهد، وقس عليه حال ابن السبيل وذوي القربى، فإن استحقاق جميعهم بالفقر، وفائدة ذكرهم دفع التوهّم الناشئ في الأمور الخارجة، كذا في «البحر الرائق» وغيره. قوله: وابن السبيل؛ أي المسافر الفقير، أو الذي له مال لا معه. قوله: وقدّم؛ أشار به إلى أنّ حق ذوي قرابة النبي ﷺ باق أو إلى أنهم المرادون من ذوي القربى في الآية، لا ذوو قربى كلّ خليفة، وإلى أنهم يستحقون بالفقر لا بنفس القرابة. قال أبو يوسف: في كتاب «الخراج»: «أما الخمس الذي يخرج من الغنيمة، فإن الكلبي محمد بن السائب حدثني عن أبي صالح عن ابن عباس: إن الخمس كان في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم على خمسةِ أسهم لله وللرسول سهم، ولذي القربي سهم، ولليتامى والمساكين وابن السبيل ثلاثة أسهم. ثم قسمه أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان رضي الله عنهم على ثلاثة أسهم وسقط سهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بموته، وسهم ذي القربى. ثم قسمه علي بن أبي طالب على ما قسمه عليه أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان رضي الله عنهم. وقد روي لنا عن ابن عباس أنه قال: عرض علينا عمر رضي الله عنه أن نزوج من أيمنا. ونقضي منه عن مغرمنا فأبينا إلا أن يسلّمه لنا، وأبى ذلك علينا». انتهى. ويرد في هذا المقام إيرادات: أحدها: إنه لو كان استحقاق ذوي القربى بالفقر؛ لكان ذكرهم بعد ذكر المسكين لغواً، وقد مرَّ الجواب عنه. وثانيها: إنه لو كان استحقاقهم للفقر لكان حرم عليهم كما حرمت عليهم الصدقة. وجوابه: إن خمس الخمس ليس في حكم الصدقة من كل وجه؛ ولذا جعل أيضاً خمس الخمس مع حرمة الصدقة عليه، فلا يلزم من حرمة الصدقة عليهم كون خمس الخمس حراما عليهم. وثالثها: إنه لو كان استحقاقهم للفقرِ لحرمَ إعطاؤه للغني منهم، وهو خلاف ما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عند الطحاوي وغيره من أنه كان يعطي العباس رضي الله عنه، وكان من مياسير الصحابة. وأجيب عنه: بأن النبي ﷺ وإن كان يقسم خمس الخمس لأغنياء ذوي القربي أيضاً، لكن أبا بكر وعمر لم يعطيا إلا للفقراء منهم، كما أخرجه الطحاوي وغيره، وكان ذلك بمحضر من ذوي القربى وغيرهم من الصحابة، فصار ذلك بمنزلة الإجماع على أن استحقاقهم بعد النبي بالفقر. وفيه: إنّ هذا كان اجتهاداً ورأياً رأياه، وقد خالفه علي رضي الله عنه وغيره كما أخرجه الطحاوي وغيره، فلا إجماع هاهنا على أن المذكورين في الآية إنما هم مصارف، فيكفي الصرفُ إلى واحد منها، فلا يلزمُ من صرفِ الخلفاء الخمس إلى غيرهـم مـن المصارف وعدم صرفهم إلى أغنيائهم لمصلحة فقراء المسلمين بطلان استحقاقهم، وكونهم إنما يستحقون بالفقر. وفي «شرح معاني الآثار» بعد حكاية الأقوال المختلفة مع ما لها وما عليها: «كان سهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جارياً في حياته وانقطع بموته، وكان سهم اليتامى وابن السبيل بعد وفاة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كما كان قبل ذلك. ثم اختلفوا في سهم ذوي القربى، فقال قوم: هو لهم كما كان لهم في حياته، وقال قوم: قد انقطع عنهم بموته، وكان الله قد جمع كل قرابة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في قوله: (وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى) (الأنفال: 41)، فلم يخص أحداً منهم دون أحد.
And the wayfarer, and the poor of Dhawi al-Qurba were given precedence over them and the wayfarer, and the poor of Dhawi al-Qurba were given precedence over the poor. If it is said: Since the orphan is only entitled due to his poverty, what benefit is there in mentioning him after mentioning the poor? I reply: To dispel the misconception that the orphan has no right to the spoils because entitlement is through jihad, and he is a child and cannot fight. And analogously, the case of the wayfarer and Dhawi al-Qurba, for the entitlement of all of them is due to poverty, and the benefit of mentioning them is to dispel the misconception arising in external matters, as stated in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and others. His saying: 'and the wayfarer' means the poor traveler, or one who has wealth but not with him. His saying: 'and he gave precedence' indicates that the right of the relatives of the Prophet ﷺ remains or that they are intended by 'Dhawi al-Qurba' in the verse, not the relatives of every caliph, and that they are entitled due to poverty, not due to kinship itself. Abu Yusuf said in the Book of Kharaj: 'As for the khums taken from the spoils, al-Kalbi Muhammad ibn al-Sa'ib narrated to me from Abu Salih from Ibn Abbas: The khums in the time of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was on five shares: for Allah and the Messenger a share, for Dhawi al-Qurba a share, and for the orphans, the poor, and the wayfarer three shares. Then Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman divided it into three shares, and the share of the Messenger fell with his death, and the share of Dhawi al-Qurba. Then Ali divided it as Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman had divided it. And it has been narrated to us from Ibn Abbas that he said: Umar offered to us that we marry from it and pay our debts from it, but we refused except that he hand it over to us, and he refused that to us.' End. Several objections arise in this context: First: If the entitlement of Dhawi al-Qurba were due to poverty, then mentioning them after mentioning the poor would be redundant, and the answer has already passed. Second: If their entitlement were due to poverty, it would be forbidden to them just as charity is forbidden to them. The answer: Khums al-Khums is not entirely in the ruling of charity; therefore it is also made permissible for them despite the prohibition of charity upon them, so it does not follow that because charity is forbidden to them, Khums al-Khums is also forbidden. Third: If their entitlement were due to poverty, giving it to a rich person among them would be forbidden, which contradicts what is established from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ according to al-Tahawi and others, that he used to give to al-Abbas, and he was among the wealthy of the Companions. The answer: Although the Prophet ﷺ used to distribute Khums al-Khums to the rich of Dhawi al-Qurba as well, Abu Bakr and Umar only gave to the poor among them, as narrated by al-Tahawi and others, and that was in the presence of Dhawi al-Qurba and other Companions, so that became akin to consensus that their entitlement after the Prophet is due to poverty. However, this was an ijtihad and opinion they held, and Ali and others opposed them, as narrated by al-Tahawi and others, so there is no consensus here. Rather, those mentioned in the verse are merely recipients, so it is sufficient to give to one of them. Hence, the fact that the caliphs gave the khums to other recipients and did not give to their rich for the maslahah of the poor Muslims does not invalidate their entitlement being due to poverty. In Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, after recounting the different opinions with their pros and cons: 'The share of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was given during his life and ceased with his death, and the share of orphans and wayfarer remained as before after his death. Then they differed regarding the share of Dhawi al-Qurba: some said it is for them as it was during his life, and others said it ceased with his death, and Allah had gathered all relatives of the Messenger in His saying: 'And for Dhawi al-Qurba' (al-Anfal 41), and He did not single out any of them over another.'
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the chapter of spoils division, specifically addressing the share of the Prophet's relatives (Dhawi al-Qurba) from the khums. The author first clarifies that the condition for entitlement is poverty, not mere kinship. He cites a narration from Abu Yusuf's Kitab al-Kharaj via Ibn Abbas, showing the original distribution of khums into five shares, and how after the Prophet's death, Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman reduced it to three shares, effectively dropping the shares of the Prophet and Dhawi al-Qurba. However, Ali later restored the previous division? Actually the text says Ali divided as they did, meaning he also excluded the share of Dhawi al-Qurba? The narration mentions that Ibn Abbas refused Umar's offer to handle the share, indicating the share was kept aside but not given out. Then three objections are raised: (1) If they are entitled due to poverty, mentioning them after the poor is redundant—answered earlier. (2) If due to poverty, would it not be forbidden like charity? But khums is not fully analogous to charity. (3) The Prophet gave to the rich like al-Abbas, contradicting the poverty condition. The response is that the Prophet's practice was his own; after him, Abu Bakr and Umar restricted it to the poor, and this became the established practice, though not consensus, because Ali disagreed. The passage concludes with a quote from Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, stating the disagreement about whether the share of Dhawi al-Qurba continued after the Prophet. The Hanafi position, following the majority of the caliphs, is that it is conditional on poverty.

Key Terms
ذوي القربى (Dhawi al-Qurba): The relatives of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, specifically Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, who are eligible for a share of khums.
خمس الخمس (Khums al-Khums): The portion of the khums that is allocated to Dhawi al-Qurba, which is not treated as ordinary charity.
المغنم (al-ghanimah): Spoils of war taken from the enemy, from which the khums is extracted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, after the death of the Prophet ﷺ, the share of Dhawi al-Qurba from khums is given to whom?
A) All relatives regardless of wealth.
B) Only poor relatives.
C) Only orphans.
D) The state treasury.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position: The khums share of Dhawi al-Qurba is conditional on poverty, based on the practice of the early caliphs after the Prophet's death.

intermediate
6 min
The Prophet's Share and Dhawi al-Qurba: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute over beneficiary of Prophet's share and dhawi al-qurba share after his death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the Prophet's share and the share of Dhawi al-Qurba in the khums lapse with the Prophet's death, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Prophet's share in khums was for his prophethood, not his imamate — it lapsed with him.

This passage addresses a critical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the distribution of the khums after the Prophet's death. The issue is whether the Prophet's share and the share of his near relatives continue for subsequent leaders or cease. The Hanafi position, supported by the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, is that these shares were unique to the Prophet and his specific relatives. Understanding this dispute illuminates the method of deriving rulings from the Sunnah and the principles of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: the Prophet's share and the share of Dhawi al-Qurba lapsed with the Prophet's death. Note that the share was given to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib exclusively.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the Prophet's share continues for the caliph (as imam) and the relatives' share goes to all relatives of the Prophet.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof: the Prophet's share was due to his imamate (leadership), so it passes to every subsequent imam.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi refutation: if it were for imamate, the Rightly Guided Caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman) would have taken it, but they did not. The share was attached to the description of 'messenger' (mushtaq), which is exclusive to the Prophet. The Companions' consensus to spend these shares on jihad further supports the lapse.
Note the linguistic argument: the share is tied to the derived term 'rasul', so it applies only to the one who bears that description.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafis divide khums into four portions (orphans, poor, wayfarers, public treasury), while Shafi'is divide into five (adding the Prophet's share for the caliph and the relatives' share for all kin).

And nothing for their rich, and the mention of Allah Almighty for blessing, and the share of the Prophet (peace be upon him) lapsed with his death. Then the Prophet (peace be upon him) distributed that to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib specifically, and forbade Banu Umayyah and Banu Nawfal. So it was established that that share was for the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he placed it in whichever relatives he wished. Thus its ruling became the ruling of his share that he used to select for himself. Just as that was abolished by his death, not obligatory for anyone after him, so this also was abolished by his death, not obligatory for anyone after him. This is the saying of Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. End. His saying: 'for their rich'; supported by the saying of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, one of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, in a long statement as transmitted by al-Tahawi; whoever wants to see it, let him refer to it. His saying: 'for blessing'; supported by the saying of al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah: 'Indeed, to Allah belongs His fifth, this is the key to speech: to Allah belongs the world and the Hereafter, and to the Messenger and to the relatives.' They differed after the death of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). Some said: the share of the relatives is for the relatives of the caliph. Some said: the share of the Prophet is for the caliph after him. The Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) agreed to place these two shares in horses and equipment in the path of Allah. Ibn Abbas said: 'His saying: to Him belongs His fifth, the key to Allah's speech: to Him belongs what is in the heavens and what is in the earth; the share of Allah and the share of the Messenger are one.' Transmitted by Ibn Jarir, al-Tabarani, Ibn Mardawayh, Abu al-Shaykh, and others. His saying: 'lapsed with his death'; because it was attached by a derived form (mushtaq), so the source of derivation becomes the cause for it. So the meaning is: for the Messenger due to his messengership. No one after him is described by this description, so no one else deserves it. He who asserted that his messengership ceased with his death has erred, for messengership does not cease with death. Rather, likewise guardianship (wilayah) and all religious dignities. How so? While the prophets are alive in their graves. Like the safiy (selected portion), this is according to us. As for al-Shafi'i, he divides into five shares: the share of the Messenger (peace be upon him) is for the caliph. According to us, it lapsed with his death just as the safiy lapsed. For the Prophet was allowed to select for himself something from the spoils. And the share of the relatives (dhawi al-qurba) is for them: i.e., for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib. For the Messenger (peace be upon him) only deserved it due to his imamate, so every imam after him deserves it. And we say: if that were the case, then Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman would be more entitled to take the share of the Prophet, but that is not established. So it is known that the entitlement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) was due to his messengership, not his imamate. His saying: 'for them'; i.e., for the relatives (dhawi al-qurba), and since this word encompasses all of the relatives...
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the division of khums (one-fifth of war spoils) after the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author begins by stating a ruling from the matn: that nothing is given to the rich among the relatives, and that the mention of Allah is for blessing. The central issue is that the Prophet's share and the share of Dhawi al-Qurba (near relatives) lapsed with his death. The author explains that the Prophet distributed his share to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, excluding Banu Umayyah and Banu Nawfal, proving that the share was his to give as he wished. Thus, like his personal selection (safiy), it ceased upon his death. This is the position of Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the Hanafi school). The author then cites supporting evidence: the statement of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, the tradition of al-Hasan ibn Ali, and the consensus of the Companions after the Prophet's death to spend these two shares on horses and equipment for jihad during the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar. Ibn Abbas also supported this view, considering the share of Allah and the Messenger as one. The author argues that the Prophet's share was attached to his description of 'messenger' (mushtaq), so it applies only to him as long as he is described as such; messengership continues even after death, as prophets are alive in their graves. On page 405, the text contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i divides the khums into five shares, giving the Prophet's share to the caliph and the relatives' share to all of the Prophet's kin. The Hanafi response is that if the share were for the imamate, then the Rightly Guided Caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman) would have taken it, but they did not, proving it was for prophethood alone. The safiy (special selection) was exclusively for the Prophet, and it lapsed with him. Similarly, the share of Dhawi al-Qurba is specifically for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, as supported by narrations and the practice of the Prophet. Thus, the Hanafi position is that after the Prophet's death, the khums is divided into four portions (for orphans, poor, and wayfarers, plus the share of Allah going to the public treasury), whereas the Shafi'i position maintains five portions including the Prophet's share for the caliph and the relatives' share for all relatives.

Key Terms
الصفي (al-safiy): The special portion of the spoils that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would select for himself before the khums was divided.
سهم النبي (sahm al-nabi): The share of the Prophet (peace be upon him) from the khums (one-fifth of war spoils).
ذو القربى (dhu al-qurba): The near relatives of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who are entitled to a share of the khums.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It goes to the caliph.
B) It lapses and is no longer distributed.
C) It is given to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.
D) It is divided among all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's share in khums was attached to his prophethood and thus lapsed with his death; the Hanafi school relies on the Companions' consensus and the unique nature of the safiy.
