The Prophet's Special Share (al-Safi) and Division of Khums: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on the continuation of the Prophet's khums shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the continuation of the Prophet's share (sahm al-rasul) and al-safi after his death, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and explain the Hanafi response using the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Core Idea
The Prophet's share of khums ceased with his death; it was tied to his prophethood, not his leadership.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute on how to divide the khums (one-fifth of war spoils). The Hanafi school holds that the Prophet's special share (sahm al-rasul) and his right to take a choice item (al-safi) before division were specific to his prophethood and ended with his death. The Shafi'i school argues that these entitlements transfer to the caliph because the Prophet deserved them as a leader. The Hanafi response relies on the clear precedent of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman, who did not claim the Prophet's share, proving it was not tied to the office of imamah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is whether the Prophet's share and al-safi continue for the caliph after his death. The Hanafi say no, Shafi'i say yes.
The issue is about the continuation of a specific entitlement.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Prophet's share and al-safi were specific to him; after his death, the khums is divided into four shares (for dhawi al-qurba, orphans, needy, travelers) and no separate share for the caliph.
The Hanafi basis includes hadith and practice of the caliphs.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof
They argue the Prophet's entitlement was due to his imamah (leadership), so every imam inherits it.
This is a qiyas (analogy) on the position of leadership.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
If it were due to imamah, Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman would have taken it, but they did not. This indicates it was due to prophethood (nubuwwa) alone.
The rebuttal uses the absence of practice (adam al-thubut) as proof.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Hanafis do not allocate a separate fifth share for the caliph; Shafi'is do. This affects how khums is distributed and the understanding of the Prophet's unique status.
The practical difference is in the allocation of khums shares.

Source Text

كتاب الجهاد / باب المغنم وقسمته

كالصفي، هذا عندنا.

أما عند الشَّافِعِي فيقسم على خمسة أسهم: سهم الرسول عليه السلام للخليفة.

وعندنا سقط بموته كما سقط الصفي، فإنه كان للنبي أن يصطفي لنفسه شيئاً من الغنيمة.

وسهم ذوي القربى لهم: أي لبني هاشم وبني المطلب.

[1] قوله: كالصفي؛ على وزن فعيل؛ قال الشعبي: كان سهم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يدعى الصفي إن شاء عبداً وإن شاء أمة وإن شاء فرساً، يختاره قبل الخمس. أخرجه ابن المنذر وابن أبي شيبة وعبد الرزاق وأبو داود. وقال محمد بن سيرين: كان يضرب له سهم مع المسلمين وإن لم يشهد، والصفي يؤخذ له من رأس الخمس قبل كل شيء. أخرجه أبو داود، ومثله عن قتادة والحسن وعائشة وغيرهم.

[2] قوله: للخليفة؛ أي السلطان والإمام بعده، فإن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم إنما كان يستحقه لإمامته، فيستحقه كل إمام بعده. ونحن نقول: لو كان كذلك لكان أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان أحقاء بأخذ سهم النبي ولم يثبت، فعلم أن استحقاق الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم كان لرسالته لا لإمامته.

[3] قوله: لهم؛ أي لذوي القربى، ولما كان هذا اللفظ شاملاً لجميع أرباب قرابة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وقد ثبت أنه لم يعط إلا لبني هاشم وبني المطلب، فسره الشارح بقوله: أي لبني هاشم وبني المطلب.
Book of Jihad / Chapter on Spoils and Its Division

Al-Safi; this is according to us.

As for according to al-Shafi'i, it is divided into five shares: the share of the Messenger, peace be upon him, for the caliph.

According to us, it ceased with his death just as al-safi ceased, for the Prophet had the right to select for himself something from the spoils.

And the share of dhawi al-qurba (relatives) is for them: i.e., for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.

[1] His statement: "al-safi" is on the pattern of fa'il. Al-Sha'bi said: The share of the Prophet, peace be upon him, was called al-safi; if he wished a male slave, or a female slave, or a horse, he would choose it before the khums. Narrated by Ibn al-Mundhir, Ibn Abi Shaybah, Abd al-Razzaq, and Abu Dawud. And Muhammad ibn Sirin said: A share was allocated for him with the Muslims even if he was not present, and al-safi was taken for him from the head of the khums before everything else. Narrated by Abu Dawud, and similar from Qatadah, al-Hasan, A'ishah, and others.

[2] His statement: "for the caliph" means the ruler and the imam after him, for the Messenger, peace be upon him, only deserved it due to his imamah, so every imam after him deserves it. But we say: If it were so, then Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman would be more deserving to take the Prophet's share, but this is not established. So it is known that the Prophet's entitlement was due to his messengership, not his imamah.

[3] His statement: "for them" means for dhawi al-qurba. And since this term included all relatives of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and it is established that it was only given to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, the commentator interpreted it by saying: i.e., for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the division of the khums (one-fifth of war spoils). The Hanafi position is that the Prophet's special share (sahm al-rasul) and the right to take al-safi (a choice item before division) were specific to the Prophet and ceased upon his death. The Shafi'i position holds that these entitlements continue for the caliph after the Prophet. The Hanafi proof includes the practice of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman, who did not take the Prophet's share, indicating it was tied to his prophethood, not his leadership. Additionally, the share of dhawi al-qurba (close relatives) is specified for Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib only, not all relatives, as established by historical practice. The commentary includes hadith evidence from al-Sha'bi and Muhammad ibn Sirin defining al-safi.

Key Terms
الصفي (al-safi): A choice item taken by the Prophet from the spoils before division, as his personal right.
الخمس (al-khums): One-fifth of war spoils set aside for Allah, the Prophet, relatives, orphans, needy, and travelers.
ذوي القربى (dhawi al-qurba): The Prophet's close relatives entitled to a share of khums, specifically Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is discontinued.
C) It is given to Banu Hashim.
D) It is added to the general treasury.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's share of khums and his right to al-safi were unique to his prophethood and ceased with his death; Hanafi fiqh maintains this based on the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

The Imam's Authority to Grant Tanfil During Battle
Explains the Hanafi ruling on tanfil with conditions and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanfil and identify when the Imam may grant extra rewards from spoils.

Core Idea
Tanfil is only allowed before the spoils are secured or from the khums — after seizure, the fighters' rights are fixed.

In the heat of battle, a commander may promise extra rewards to motivate troops beyond their regular share of spoils. This practice, called tanfil, has specific rules in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the source of the extra reward, and the evidence from hadith and Quran, ensuring you understand both the permissibility and the restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what tanfil is and its hukm level (obligatory, recommended, etc.). The text states that specific promises of extra reward are mandub (recommended), while the general duty to urge (hathth) is wajib.
Tanfil is a discretionary grant, not an obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence: the hadith 'Whoever kills a slain man, his belongings are for him' (man qatala qatilan fahuwa lahu) and the Quranic verse urging believers to fight (8:65).
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the permissibility of tanfil. The phrase 'fahuwa lahu' (it is for him) indicates a promise of extra reward, which the Imam can use to incentivize fighters.
The Hanafi school applies istihsan to uphold this practice despite the text's zanni status.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute: whether tanfil is taken from the khums or from the entire spoils. The well-known (mashhur) position is from the khums.
The ikhtilaf does not affect the primary condition of timing.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Understand why the mashhur position is preferred: it aligns with the practice of the Companions and the principle that the Imam's promise is a pledge from the public treasury (khums), not from the fighters' shares.
The mufta bihi is that tanfil after seizure is only from the khums.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وللإمام أن ينفل وقت القتال" حنا"، فيقول : من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه) : التنفيلُ إعطاء شيء زائد على سهم الغنيمة الإمام ففيه روايتان، والمشهور أنه يخمس ؛ لأنه لما أذن لهم الإمام فقد التزم نصرتهم بالإمداد ، فصار كالمنعة (١).
[١]قوله : أن ينفل ؛ مضارع من التنفيل ، يقال : نفله تنفيلا، ونفله بالتخفيف : إذا أعطاه الإمام فوق سهمه، وهو من النفل بمعنى الزائد، ومنه سُمِّي التطوع نفلاً ، والتنفيلُ نوع من قسمةِ الغنائم، إلا أنها بدون ضابط ؛ لأنه مفوّض إلى رأي الإمام. كذا في «الفتح» (٢).
[٢] قوله : وقت القتال ؛ قال في «الفتح» (۳) : قيّد به ؛ لأنّ التنفيل إنما يجوز عندنا قبل الإصابة سواءً كان بسلب المقتول أو غيره لا بعده. انتهى. وفي «الذخيرة»: لا خلاف في أنّ التنفيل قبل الإصابة وإحراز الغنيمة، وقبل أن تضع الحرب أوزارها جائز، ويوم الهزيمة ويوم الفتح لا يجوز؛ لأن القصد به التحريض على القتال، ولا حاجة إليه إذا انهزم العدوّ ، وأمّا بعد الإحراز فلا يجوز إلا من الخمس إذا كان محتاجاً. انتهى.
[٣] قوله : حقاً ؛ الحث - بفتح الحاء المهملة، وتشديد الثاء المثلثة – : التحريض والترغيب إلى القتال ومطلقه واجب ؛ لقوله : ( يَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ حَرْضِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ عَلَى الْقِتَالِ ، سواء كان بالموعظة أو بإعطاء المال من عنده أو بغير ذلك، وخصوص الوعد بإعطاء السلب ونحوه مندوب إليه، ولا تغتر بما في «الكفاية» و«العناية»(٥) و«البناية) من أنّ أمر حرّض في الآية مصروف إلى الاستحباب.
أو لسرية جعلت لكم الربع بعد الخمس، لا بعد الإحراز هاهنا، إلا من الخمس والتركيب" يدلُّ على الزيادة، قوله : مَن قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه ؛ سماه قتيلاً لقريه إلى القتل.
أو السرية جعلت لكم الربع بعد الخمس : أي بعدما رُفِعَ الخمس جعلْتُ لكم ربع الباقي، أو ثلثه ، أو نحو ذلك ، لا بعد الإحراز هاهنا ) : أي بدار الإسلام ؛ لأنه حينئذ صار ملكاً للغانمين (إلا) من الخمس )
[١] قوله : والتركيب ؛ أي تركيب حروفِ كلمة التنفيل يدلّ على الزيادة ؛ لأنه من النفل، وهو في الأصل التبرع والزيادة.
[٢] قوله : من قتل ... الخ ؛ دفعُ دخل مقدّر تقرير الدخل : إن قتل المقتول غير متصوّر شرعاً وعقلاً ، بل الرجلُ إنّما يكون قتيلاً بعد قتله لا قبله، فكيف يصح قوله : من قتل قتيلاً ، أو تقرير الدفع أنه قد يُسمّى الشيء باسم باعتبار ما يؤول إليه، كما في قوله حال : إني أردني أَعْصِرُ خَمْرًا ) ، فسمى العصير خمراً لقربه منه ، وأوله إليه. فكذلك سمّى مَن يقتله الغازي عند قتله قتيلا ؛ لقريه إليه ، وقيل : لا مجاز فيه، ولا حاجة إلى هذا التأويل، فإنّ اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال مجاز في الماضي والاستقبال حال التلبس بالفعل، لا حالَ النطق ، فالقتيل وإن لم يكن قتيلا حقيقة عند هذا القول ؛ أي من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه، لكنه قتيل حقيقة حال وقوع القتل عليه بلا شبهة.
[٣]قوله : لا بعد الإحراز ؛ أي لا يجوز للإمام أن ينفل بعد إحراز الغنيمة بدار الإسلام، وكذا قبله بعد الإصابة كما مرّ.
(٤) قوله : صار ملكاً ؛ أي مملوكاً للكلّ على سبيل الاشتراك، فلا يجوز إبطال حقهم.
[0]قوله : إلا من الخمس ؛ يعني يجوز أن ينفل بعد الإحراز من خمس الغنيمة ؛ لأنه لا حق فيها للغانمين ، فيتصرف الإمام فيه كيف شاء.
(1) أي لا ينفل الإمام بعد إحراز الغنيمة بدار الإسلام ؛ لأن حق الغير تأكد فيه بالاحراز، ويجوز التنفيل بعد الاحراز من الخمس إلا للغني لأن الخمس للمحتاج. ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر»
.(701)
(۲) يوسف : من الآية ٣٦.
("And the Imam may grant extra reward [tanfil] at the time of battle, rightfully, saying: Whoever kills a slain man, his belongings are for him.") Tanfil is giving something additional beyond the share of the spoils. The Imam has two narrations regarding it, and the well-known is that it is taken from the khums (one-fifth); because when the Imam permitted them, he has obligated himself to support them with reinforcements, so it becomes like a grant (1). [1] His statement: "an yunfila" is the imperfect verb from tanfil; it is said: nafalahu tanfilan, and nafalahu with tashdid: when the Imam gives him beyond his share, and it is from nafal meaning surplus, from which supererogatory worship is called nafal. Tanfil is a type of division of spoils, but without a fixed measure; because it is delegated to the Imam's opinion. Thus in al-Fath (2). [2] His statement: "at the time of battle"; it is said in al-Fath (3): He restricted it because tanfil is only permissible according to us before the injury (qabl al-isabah), whether by the belongings of the slain or other, not after. End quote. In al-Dhakhira: There is no disagreement that tanfil before injury and seizure of the spoils, and before war lays down its burdens, is permissible, but on the day of defeat and the day of victory it is not permissible; because the purpose is incitement to fight, and there is no need for it when the enemy is routed. As for after seizure (al-ihraz), it is not permissible except from the khums if there is need. End quote. [3] His statement: "haqqan" (rightfully); al-hathth (incitement) with fat-h on the ha' and tashdid on the tha': inciting and encouraging to fight; its absolute form is obligatory due to His saying: "O Prophet, urge the believers to fight" (Quran 8:65), whether by admonition or by giving wealth from himself or otherwise. Specific promises of giving the belongings (salab) and the like are recommended (mandub). Do not be deceived by what is in al-Kifayah, al-Inayah (5) and al-Binayah that the command "urge" in the verse is turned to recommendation (istihbab). Or sending a detachment: "I have made for you one-fourth after the khums, not after seizure here, except from the khums". And the construction indicates increase. His statement: "Whoever kills a slain man, his belongings are for him"; he called him "slain" (qatil) because of his proximity to being killed. Or sending a detachment: "I have made for you one-fourth after the khums": i.e., after the khums is taken, I have made for you one-fourth of the remainder, or one-third, or similar, "not after seizure here": i.e., in the abode of Islam; because then it has become property of the combatants (ghanimin), except from the khums. [1] His statement: "and the construction" (al-tarkib); i.e., the composition of the letters of the word tanfil indicates increase; because it is from nafal, which originally means donation and increase. [2] His statement: "Whoever kills..." etc.; repelling an anticipated objection: The objection: The killing of the slain is not conceivable legally and rationally; rather a man becomes slain only after his death, not before it, so how does the statement "Whoever kills a slain man" make sense? Or the defense: it is that a thing may be named by a name considering what it will become, as in His statement: "I see myself pressing wine" (Quran 12:36), so he called grape juice wine because of its proximity to it. So similarly, the one whom the fighter kills at the time of his killing is called "slain" because of his proximity to it. And it is said: There is no metaphor at all, and there is no need for this interpretation; for the active participle (ism al-fa'il) is reality in the present, and metaphorically in the past and future when engaged in the action, not at the time of utterance. So the "slain" (qatil) is not actually slain at the time of this statement, but he becomes slain at the moment of the killing falling upon him without doubt. [3] His statement: "not after seizure"; i.e., it is not permissible for the Imam to grant extra reward after the spoils are seized in the abode of Islam, and likewise before that after injury (isabah) as passed. (4) His statement: "it has become property"; i.e., owned by all collectively, so it is not permissible to invalidate their right. [0] His statement: "except from the khums"; i.e., it is permissible to grant extra reward after seizure from the khums of the spoils; because the combatants have no right in it, so the Imam may dispose of it as he wishes. (1) i.e., the Imam does not grant extra reward after seizure of the spoils in the abode of Islam; because the right of others is confirmed by seizure, and grant after seizure from the khums is permissible except for the rich, because the khums is for the needy. See: Majma' al-Anhur (701). (2) Yusuf: from verse 36.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the subject of tanfil (granting extra reward from spoils) by the Imam during jihad. It begins by stating the basic ruling: the Imam may lawfully grant extra rewards at the time of battle, rightfully (haqqan), by saying, for example, 'Whoever kills a slain man, his belongings (salab) are for him.' The commentator explains that tanfil is giving something beyond the regular share of the spoils. There are two narrations about how it is taken from the spoils, but the well-known (mashhur) position is that it is deducted from the khums (the one-fifth share of the spoils allocated to the state), because the Imam, by promising extra reward, has obligated himself to support the fighters with reinforcements, similar to a grant. The passage then breaks down the wording: 'an yunfila' is derived from nafal meaning increase, and tanfil is a type of division without a fixed measure, left to the Imam's discretion. The condition 'at the time of battle' is significant: according to the Hanafi school, tanfil is only permissible before the injury (qabl al-isabah) — i.e., before the enemy is hurt and the spoils are secured. It is not allowed after that, because the purpose is to encourage fighting, which is no longer needed. Al-Dhakhira confirms that there is no dispute that tanfil before injury and seizure is permissible, but on the day of defeat or victory it is not, and after seizure (ihraz) it is only permissible from the khums if there is need. The commentator clarifies that the absolute act of urging to fight (hathth) is obligatory based on the Quranic verse (8:65), but specific promises like giving the belongings of a slain enemy are recommended (mandub), not obligatory. The passage then discusses the phrase 'after the khums' in the context of the Imam's announcement to a detachment, explaining that the extra reward is only from the remainder after the khums is taken, and not after the spoils are secured in the abode of Islam (because then the fighters' rights are fixed). The commentator also addresses a linguistic issue: how can one say 'whoever kills a slain man' (man qatala qatilan) when the man is not yet slain? The answer is that something can be named by its future state (like wine for juice), or in a different view, the active participle is used in the context of the action being engaged in, so it means 'whoever is in the process of killing a man' — that man is called 'slain' in anticipation. Finally, the passage reiterates that after seizure (ihraz) in the abode of Islam, tanfil is not allowed except from the khums, because the fighters' shared ownership is confirmed. The Imam may give from the khums to the needy, not the rich.

Key Terms
التنفيل (al-tanfil): The act of the Imam granting extra reward (beyond the regular share) to fighters from the spoils, before the spoils are secured.
السلب (al-salab): The belongings of a slain enemy fighter, including his weapons, armor, and whatever he has on him.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): The act of securing the spoils after the battle, typically by bringing them into the Muslim camp or abode of Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible for the Imam to grant extra rewards (tanfil) from the spoils of war?
A) Only after the spoils have been secured in the abode of Islam
B) Before the injury (isabah) and before the spoils are secured, or from the khums
C) At any time during or after the battle
D) Only after the battle is completely over

Key Takeaway
The Imam may grant extra rewards to fighters only before the spoils are secured or from the khums, not after the fighters' rights are established.

Ruling on Nafal: Permissibility from Khums After Ikhraz
Explains the permissibility and conditions of nafal for a military detachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafal after ihraz and distinguish between nafal from khums and from general ghanimah.

Core Idea
Nafal after ihraz is only allowed from the khums; the general ghanimah is joint property.

The spoils of war are divided among the participants, but the imam may give extra bounty (nafal) to deserving fighters. However, after the spoils are brought to safety, the rights of all fighters are fixed. This lesson clarifies from which portion the imam may still give nafal, based on the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the permissibility of nafal (extra bounty) for a detachment after the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam (ihraz). The text states it is allowed only from the khums.
The ruling distinguishes between nafal from khums and from the four-fifths.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the hadith 'Man qatala qatilan falahu salabuhu' (Whoever kills an enemy, his spoils are for him). This shows that extra reward is given beyond the regular share, supporting the concept of nafal.
The commentary also analyzes the linguistic derivation of 'tanfil' from 'nafl' meaning increase.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates that a fighter who kills an enemy gets the slain's belongings as extra. This implies that the imam can allocate additional shares beyond the normal distribution, especially from the khums which is not owned by the fighters.
The phrase 'falahu salabuhu' is a direct proof for extra allocation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) allow nafal from the entire ghanimah after ihraz under certain conditions. The Hanafi school restricts it to before ihraz or from the khums.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi, after ihraz, the imam can only use the khums for nafal.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that once spoils are ihraz, they become joint property of the fighters. Voiding their right is unjust. The khums is not part of their share, so it may be used for nafal.
The tarjih authority is Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah, consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

إلى القتل.

The Book of Jihad / Chapter on Spoils and Its Division. 415. Or for a detachment, I gave you the quarter after the fifth, not after taking possession here, except from the fifth. The composition indicates increase. His saying: 'Whoever kills an enemy, his spoils are for him' – he called him a slain person due to his proximity to being killed. 'Or for a detachment, I gave you the quarter after the fifth' means after the fifth is taken out, I gave you a quarter of the remainder, or a third, or the like. 'Not after taking possession here' means in Dar al-Islam, because at that point it became property of the gainers. 'Except from the fifth.' [1] His saying: 'The composition' means the combination of letters of the word 'tanfil' indicates increase, because it comes from 'nafl', which originally means donation and increase. [2] His saying: 'Whoever kills...' repels an expected objection: The killing of the slain is inconceivable legally and rationally, but a man only becomes slain after his death, not before. So how can it be correct to say 'Whoever kills a slain person'? Or the repulsion is that a thing may be named by what it will become, as in His saying: 'Indeed I see myself pressing wine' – he called the juice wine because of its proximity and outcome. Similarly, the fighter is called slain at the time of his killing due to proximity. And it is said there is no metaphor and no need for this interpretation, because the active participle is literally in the present, metaphorical in the past and future when engaged in the action, not at the time of utterance. So the slain, even if not literally slain at this statement, is literally slain at the moment the killing occurs upon him without doubt. [3] His saying: 'Not after taking possession' means the Imam may not give nafal after the ghanimah is taken to Dar al-Islam, nor before it after injury as has passed. (4) His saying: 'It became property' means it became owned in common, so it is not permissible to void their right. [0] His saying: 'Except from the fifth' means it is permissible to give nafal after taking possession from the fifth of the ghanimah, because there is no right of the gainers in it, so the Imam may dispose of it as he wishes. (1) That is, the Imam may not give nafal after taking possession of the ghanimah in Dar al-Islam because the right of others is confirmed by taking possession, and it is permissible to give nafal after taking possession from the fifth except for the wealthy, because the fifth is for the needy. See: 'Majma' al-Anhar' (701). (2) Yusuf: from verse 36.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the allocation of nafal (extra bounty) to a military detachment (sariyyah). The author first states the matn: the imam may give a quarter or a third after the khums is taken out, but not after the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam (ihraz) except from the khums itself. The commentary explains each part: 'after the fifth' means after deducting the khums; 'not after ihraz' because once brought to safety, the spoils become joint property of all participants; 'except from the khums' because the khums is not their property, so the imam may allocate from it even after ihraz. The item also analyzes the phrase 'the composition indicates increase', referring to the word 'tanfil' (from nafl meaning extra). The hadith 'man qatala qatilan falahu salabuhu' is cited as proof that nafal is an increase, and the commentary addresses a potential objection about calling a living person 'qatil' (slain), explaining it is by way of future outcome or by considering the present actuality. The key ruling: the imam may give nafal from the khums after ihraz, but not from the general ghanimah.

Key Terms
التنفيل (al-tanfil): Extra allocation of spoils to fighters beyond their regular share, often from the khums or before ihraz.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Bringing the spoils to safety in Dar al-Islam, after which they become joint property of the army.
الخمس (al-khums): One-fifth of the spoils that belongs to the imam for the categories mentioned in the Quran (e.g., Allah, His Messenger, relatives, orphans, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from which portion of the spoils may the imam give nafal (extra bounty) after the spoils have been brought to Dar al-Islam (ihraz)?
A) From the entire ghanimah
B) Only from the khums (one-fifth)
C) Only from the four-fifths
D) Not allowed from any portion after ihraz

Key Takeaway
The imam may give nafal from the khums even after ihraz; giving nafal from the general ghanimah after ihraz is prohibited because it violates the collective ownership of the participants.

Definition of Salab and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Its Ownership
Defines salab and presents the Hanafi ruling with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of salab and explain why the school holds it belongs to all soldiers unless the imam allocates it as nafal, and contrast this with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The salab belongs to all fighters in common unless the imam explicitly gives it as nafal—the killer has no automatic right.

This lesson examines a key dispute in the fiqh of jihad: what happens to the belongings (salab) of an enemy slain in battle? The Hanafi school maintains that salab goes into the general ghanimah pool, shared by all fighters, unless the imam specifically allocates it to the killer as an extra reward (nafal). This contrasts with the Shafi'i school, which gives the killer automatic ownership based on a famous hadith. We will unpack the definitions, the evidence, and the practical implications of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on salab
Identify the baseline ruling: salab is part of the general ghanimah and belongs to all fighters collectively unless the imam gives it as nafal to the killer.
This is the default; the burden of proof is on any exception.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the killer automatically owns the salab of his slain enemy, provided the killer is eligible for a share and the slain was advancing.
The Shafi'i position is based on a literal reading of the hadith.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'Man qatala qatilan falahu salabuhu' (Whoever kills an enemy, his spoils are for him) as reported in authentic collections from the Battle of Hunayn.
This seems to give a general rule of automatic entitlement.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi scholars argue that this hadith refers to a specific announcement (nafal) made by the Prophet on that occassion, not a universal legislation. The default principle is joint ownership of spoils; exceptions require explicit text of general application.
The Hanafi response preserves the general rules of ghanimah distribution.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under the Hanafi school the killer does not automatically take the enemy's belongings; they are pooled. Under the Shafi'i school, he takes them automatically if conditions are met.
This difference affects how spoils are distributed after battle.

Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. 'And his salab includes what he has, even his mount and what he wears, and it belongs to all if the imam did not give nafal.' [1] 'And his salab includes what he has, even his mount and what he wears, and it belongs to all if the imam did not give nafal', contrary to al-Shafi'i. (1) For with him, the salab belongs to the killer if he is among those eligible for a share and he killed him while advancing, due to his saying: 'Whoever kills an enemy, his spoils are for him.' (2) [0] If it is said: Then this necessitates voiding the right of the poor and others or reducing it. I say: It has passed that they are recipients of the khums, not owners of a necessary right, such that the imam may spend it on one category, and its equivalent has passed in the chapter on disbursement of zakah. [1] His saying: 'And his salab includes what he has' means what the slain has, including his mount, clothes on his body, weapons, ring, belt, and what is on his mount of gold and silver in its trappings and saddle, and what is on his animal in its saddlebag and its middle of his wealth; it does not include his wealth with his servant or on another animal, as in 'al-Nahr' and others. [2] His saying: 'It is for all' means if the imam did not give nafal and did not announce the salab for the killer, then it enters the ghanimah and all fighters share in it as in other properties. [3] His saying: 'If he was...' means the conditions for the salab to be for the killer, whether the imam said 'Whoever kills an enemy, his salab is for him' or not, are two: one: that the killer be among those eligible for a share, i.e., not insane or a child etc., who do not deserve a share of the ghanimah. Second: that the killing occurred while the slain was advancing or facing the fight, so if he killed him while he was fleeing, turning away, sleeping, or occupied with something, he does not deserve the salab. [4] His saying: 'Advancing' is a state of the slain; al-Itqani made a mistake in Ghayat al-Bayan where he made it a state of the killer. [0] His saying: 'Due to his saying' – this saying was uttered by him, Allah [bless him], on the day of Hunayn; it was narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. (1) See: 'al-Ghaya wa al-Taqrib' (p. 321), 'Rawdat al-Talibin' (6:374) etc. (2) In Sahih al-Bukhari (3:1144), and Sahih Muslim (3:1371) etc. (3) 'al-Nahr al-Fa'iq' (3:222).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i concerning the salab (spoils of a slain enemy). The matn states: 'His salab includes what he has, even his mount and what he wears, and it belongs to all if the imam did not give nafal.' The commentary explains that salab includes the slain's mount, clothes, weapons, ring, saddle, and items on his animal, but not wealth with his servant or on other animals. The ruling: if the imam does not allocate it as nafal, it goes into the general ghanimah and all soldiers share equally. The disagreement with al-Shafi'i: al-Shafi'i holds that the salab automatically belongs to the killer, based on the hadith 'Man qatala qatilan falahu salabuhu'. The Hanafi response, detailed in the commentary, argues that the hadith refers to nafal granted by the imam, not an automatic right. The text then addresses an objection: if the salab is given to the killer, does that not reduce the rights of the poor in the khums? The answer: the poor are only recipients of the khums, not owners of a necessary right; the imam may allocate the khums as he sees fit. The commentary also clarifies the conditions for the killer to obtain the salab: (1) he must be eligible for a share in the ghanimah (i.e., adult sane free Muslim), and (2) he must kill the enemy while advancing (muqbil), not fleeing or asleep. It corrects a mistake by al-Itqani who thought 'muqbil' describes the killer.

Key Terms
السلب (al-salab): The belongings of a slain enemy, including his mount, weapons, clothing, and items on his animal, but not wealth with his servant or on other animals.
النفل (al-nafal): Extra allocation given by the imam to a fighter, such as granting the salab of a slain enemy.
مقبل (muqbil): Advancing towards battle; a condition for the killer to receive the salab if the imam allocates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the salab (belongings) of a slain enemy if the imam does not give it as nafal to the killer?
A) It belongs to the killer automatically
B) It is given to the imam alone
C) It becomes part of the general ghanimah shared among all soldiers
D) It is divided only among the poor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that salab is part of the general ghanimah and belongs to all fighters unless the imam specifically allocates it as nafal; the killer does not acquire automatic ownership.

The Status of Salab: Tanfil or Permanent Law?
Clarifying the Hanafi position on salab as tanfil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school interprets the hadith on salab as tanfil (incentive) rather than a permanent legal ruling, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The salab belongs to the state unless the imam grants it; the hadith is an incentive, not a law.

In this lesson, we will examine a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the spoils (salab) taken from a slain enemy. While the Shafi'i school views the Prophet's statement as a permanent legal ruling giving the killer automatic ownership, the Hanafi school interprets it as a one-time incentive (tanfil) that does not create a general right. We will explore the evidence each side presents and see how the Hanafi position aligns with broader principles of state authority over war spoils.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the Prophet's statement 'Whoever kills a fighter has the right to his salab' was a specific incentive for a particular battle, not a permanent law. The killer does not automatically own the salab; the imam may grant it as a bonus.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i interprets the same hadith as a permanent legal ruling (sharīʿah). According to the Shafi'i school, the killer is entitled to the salab as a matter of right, without the need for the imam's permission.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i school relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith and other reports where the Prophet gave salab to killers without conditions.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters that the hadith is ambiguous and can be interpreted as tanfil. They cite a narration from al-Tabarani where Muʿadh ibn Jabal told Habib ibn Maslamah that ‘a person only gets that for which his imam's heart is content,’ indicating that the imam has discretion. Even though the chain is weak, it supports the plausibility of the tanfil interpretation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Shafi'i view, the killer automatically owns the salab. Under the Hanafi view, the salab belongs to the state (bayt al-mal), and the imam may decide to grant it or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نحمل هذا على التنفيل لا على وضع الشريعة. وأصحاب السنن إلا النسائي من حديث أبي قتادة، وورد أنه قال يوم بدر أيضاً، أخرجه ابن مَرْدُوية في «تفسيره»، وسنده ضعيف.
[١]قوله : ونحن ... الخ ؛ حمل الشافعي له هذا القول النبوي على تشريع وحكم، بأنّ السلب يستحقه القاتل بحكم الشريعة، وحمله أصحابنا على التنفيل. ويؤيده ما أخرجه الطبراني في معجمه الكبير» و«الوسط»: «إنّه خرج صاحب قبرص يريد طريق أذربيحان ومعه زمرد وياقوت ولؤلؤ، فخرج إليه حبيب بن مسلمة وقتله وجاء بما معه، فأراد أبو عبيدة الله وهو أمير العسكر أن يخمسه، فقال له ابن مسلمة : لا تحرمني رزقا رزقنيه الله ، فإنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل السلب للقاتل، فقال معاذ له : يا حبيب ؛ إني سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : إنما للمرء ما طابت به نفس إمامه». ومثله أخرجه البيهقي، وإسحاق بن راهويه وغيرهما، وسنده وإن كان ضعيفاً لكنه غير قادح في المقصود، فإنّه ليس الغرض به إثباتُ المذهب، بل ليستأنس به أحد محتملي قول رسول الله : «مَن قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه»، فإنّه يحتمل أن يكون على سبيل التشريع ويحتمل أن يكون على سبيل التنفيل، كذا في «الفتح». [٢] قوله : على التنفيل ؛ يعني كان غرضه الله من هذا القول التنفيل، لا التشريع الدائمي.
And we carry this [hadith] as being about giving extra incentive (tanfil), not as laying down a permanent law (sharīʿah). The authors of the Sunan except al-Nasa'i [narrate] from the hadith of Abu Qatada, and it is reported that he said that on the day of Badr as well; Ibn Mardawayh recorded it in his Tafsir, and its chain is weak.
[1] His statement: 'And we ... etc.': al-Shafiʿi carried this prophetic statement as legislation and ruling, that the slayer deserves the spoils by virtue of permanent law, while our scholars carried it as tanfil (incentive). Supporting this is what al-Tabarani recorded in his al-Muʿjam al-Kabīr and al-Awsaṭ: 'The governor of Cyprus set out heading for the road of Azerbaijan, carrying emeralds, rubies, and pearls. Habib ibn Maslamah went out to him, killed him, and took what he had. Abu ʿUbaydah (may Allah be pleased with him), who was the army commander, wanted to take the khums (one-fifth) of it. Ibn Maslamah said to him: Do not deprive me of a provision that Allah has granted me, for the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) assigned the spoils to the killer. Muʿadh said to him: O Habib, I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: A person only gets that for which his imam's heart is content.' Al-Bayhaqi, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and others recorded something similar. Even though its chain is weak, it does not harm the purpose; for the aim is not to establish the school of thought, but rather to draw comfort from it one of the two possible meanings of the Prophet's statement: 'Whoever kills a fighter, his spoils are for him', for it could be as permanent legislation or as tanfil. This is as stated in al-Fath. [2] His statement: 'as tanfil' means that the Prophet's purpose with this statement was tanfil, not permanent legislation.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafiʿis concerning the legal significance of the Prophet's statement: 'Whoever kills a fighter has the right to his salab (weapons and equipment).' The Shafiʿi school treats this as a permanent ruling (sharīʿah), meaning the killer automatically owns the salab by virtue of the law. The Hanafi school interprets it as tanfil — a special incentive given on a particular occasion — not as a general rule. The commentator supports the Hanafi view by citing a narration from al-Tabarani where a similar incident occurred during the conquest of Cyprus. The commander Abu ʿUbaydah wanted to take khums from the spoils, but Habib ibn Maslamah claimed the salab based on the same hadith. Muʿadh ibn Jabal then informed him that the spoils are only granted if the imam (ruler) is content. This indicates that the grant is discretionary (tanfil), not automatic. The commentator notes that the chain is weak but still serves to support the plausibility of the tanfil interpretation. The key point is that the hadith is ambiguous and can be read either way; the Hanafi school chooses the tanfil interpretation, consistent with their principle that spoils of war are state property unless the imam grants them as an incentive.

Key Terms
التنفيل (al-tanfīl): Giving extra incentive or bonus (nafl) to fighters, particularly allowing them to keep additional spoils beyond the standard shares.
السلب (al-salab): The weapons, armor, and equipment of a killed enemy fighter; the spoils specifically taken from the slain opponent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the killer must pay khums before taking the salab

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith on salab as tanfil (discretionary incentive), meaning the killer does not automatically own the spoils; the imam has authority to grant them or not.

The Condition of Ihraz for Ownership by Disbelievers' Seizure
Commentary on the chapter of seizure by disbelievers, establishing the requirement of ihraz for ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when disbelievers gain ownership of Muslim property and explain the condition of ihraz using the hadith of the Prophet's she-camel.

Core Idea
Mere seizure does not transfer ownership; only securing in their land (ihraz) does.

After establishing the rulings on Muslims seizing enemy property, the author turns to the reverse situation: when disbelievers seize Muslim property. This passage clarifies that Islam protects Muslim property rights even under enemy control—ownership does not transfer automatically. The key distinction is whether the seized property has been brought into enemy territory and secured (ihraz). A well-known hadith about the Prophet's she-camel illustrates this principle in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases in the matn
The matn lists three scenarios: (1) disbelievers capturing each other, (2) a camel that strays to them, and (3) disbelievers overcoming our (Muslim) property and securing it in their land. Note that the third case is conditional on ihraz.
2
Distinguish the rulings for enemy property vs. Muslim property
The commentator explains that enemy property is mubah (permissible) for other enemies, so seizure alone suffices for ownership. But Muslim property is inviolable; hence ihraz is required.
Also note the exception for dhimmis: they remain free and their property is like Muslim property.
3
Locate the condition of ihraz and its equivalence
The author explicitly states that mere seizure does not confer ownership unless the property is secured in the enemy's land. A desert with no Muslim territory beyond is equivalent to dar al-harb for this ruling.
The commentator emphasizes this with 'أشار به إلى أن مجرد الاستيلاء لا يفيد الملك ما لم يوجد الإحراز'.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith from al-Tahawi: polytheists took the Prophet's she-camel to Medina but did not secure it in their land. A Muslim woman recovered it en route, and the Prophet took it back. This shows ownership never transferred to the polytheists.
Contrast with a hypothetical: had they brought it to their land, the ruling would differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب استيلاء الكفار
إذا سبى بعضهم بعضاً وأخذوا مالهم، أو بعيراً ند إليهم، أو غلبوا على مالنا وأحرزوه بدارهم
[1] قوله: باب استيلاء الكفار؛ لما فرغ عن بيان حكم استيلائنا على الكفار شرع في حكم استيلاء الكفار علينا، واستيلاء بعضهم على بعض، وبه ظهر أن إضافة الاستيلاء إضافة إلى فاعله لا إلى مفعوله.
(٢) قوله: بعضهم؛ أي بعض أهل الحرب بعضاً؛ وجه التملك: أن أموال أهل الحرب مباحة، فكان الاستيلاء عليها كالاستيلاء على المباح، كما في الاصطياد والاحتطاب، فإن الاستيلاء على مباح أحد أسباب الملك شرعاً، وكذا ذوات الكفار غير معصومة، فكان استيلاء بعضهم بسبيهم بعضاً مثبتاً للملك.
وذكر الصدر الشهيد في «واقعاته»: أنه لو سبى أهل الحرب أهل الذمة من دارنا لا يملكونه؛ لأنهم أحرار، وحكم أموالهم حكم أموالنا.
[3] قوله: ند؛ - بتشديد الدال المهملة - : ماض من الندود بمعنى الشرود، يعني أخذوا بعيراً شرد وتمرَّد وفرَّ إليهم فأخذوه.
[4] قوله: أو غلبوا على مالنا؛ وإن كان عبداً غير المدبّر وغيره ممن سيجيء استثناؤه.
[0] قوله: وأحرزوه بدارهم؛ أي أحرزوا ما غلبوا عليه من أموالنا بدار الحرب، ومثله الإحرازُ بمفازة ليس وراءها بلد إسلام، وأشار به إلى أن مجرد الاستيلاء لا يفيد الملك ما لم يوجد الإحراز بدارهم.
ويشهد له ما أخرجه الطحاوي: إن المشركين أغاروا على المدينة فذهبوا بناقة رسول الله ﷺ وأسروا امرأة الراعي، فلما كانت ذات ليلة في الطريق قامت المرأة حين غفلتهم، وركبت تلك الناقة وتوجهت إلى المدينة، فأخذ رسول الله ﷺ ناقته، فإنه
Chapter of Seizure by Disbelievers: If some of them take others captive and take their property, or a camel that strays to them, or they overcome our property and secure it in their land.
[1] His statement: Chapter of Seizure by Disbelievers; when he finished explaining the ruling of our seizure of disbelievers, he began on the ruling of disbelievers' seizure of us, and some of them seizing others, and by this it is clear that the addition of 'seizure' is attribution to the doer, not the object.
(2) His statement: 'some of them' i.e. some of the people of war (take) others; the basis for ownership: that the property of the people of war is permissible (mubah), so seizing it is like seizing permissible things, as in hunting and gathering firewood, for seizing a permissible thing is one of the causes of ownership by law, and likewise the persons of disbelievers are not inviolable, so the seizure of some of them by capturing others establishes ownership.
And al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in his Waqi'at that if the people of war capture the people of dhimma from our land, they do not own them because they are free, and the ruling of their property is like the ruling of our property.
[3] His statement: 'nadd' - with a shaddah on the dāl - the past tense of 'nudud' meaning fleeing, meaning they took a camel that bolted, rebelled, and fled to them, so they took it.
[4] His statement: 'or they overcome our property' - even if it is a slave other than a mudabbar and others whose exception will come.
[0] His statement: 'and secure it in their land' - i.e. they secure what they have overcome of our property in the land of war, and like it is securing in a desert that has no Islamic country beyond it, and he indicated by this that mere seizure does not confer ownership unless securing in their land is achieved.
And it is witnessed by what al-Tahawi narrated: That the polytheists raided Medina and took the she-camel of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and captured the wife of the shepherd, and when it was one night on the road, the woman stood up when they were heedless, mounted that she-camel, and headed toward Medina, and the Messenger of Allah ﷺ took his she-camel.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the chapter title and first lines of the matn regarding seizure by disbelievers. The commentator explains the placement of the chapter after the rulings on Muslims' seizure of disbelievers' property, clarifying that 'seizure' here refers to the action of the disbelievers. The ruling states that when one group of warring disbelievers captures another, they own their property and persons because the property and persons of the people of war (ahl al-harb) are not inviolable; seizing them is like seizing permissible things, which is a cause of ownership. An exception is made for dhimmis captured from Muslim lands: they remain free and their property is like Muslim property, so the captors do not own them. The commentator then defines 'nadd' as a camel that strays to the disbelievers, and clarifies that the ruling applies even to slaves (except those with special status). The key point is that mere seizure (استيلاء) does not transfer ownership unless the property is secured (أحرز) in the disbelievers' land or in a desert with no Muslim territory beyond. This is supported by a hadith from al-Tahawi: the Prophet's she-camel was taken by polytheists but a Muslim woman recovered it before it was secured in their land, so the Prophet took it back, confirming that ownership had not transferred to the polytheists.

Key Terms
استيلاء (istila'): Seizure or taking control of property or persons by force.
إحراز (ihraz): Securing or bringing property into the land of war (dar al-harb) or an equivalent place, making it effectively under their control.
ند (nadd): A camel that strays or bolts and runs away to the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when do disbelievers gain ownership of Muslim property they have seized?
A) Immediately upon seizure
B) Only after they have secured it in their land (ihraz)
C) Only if the property is a camel
D) Never, regardless of circumstances

Key Takeaway
For ownership to transfer to disbelievers, Islamic law requires not just seizure but also securing the property in their land (ihraz), as demonstrated by the Prophet's acceptance of his recovered she-camel.

Disbelievers' Ownership of Muslim Property by Conquest
Debate on whether disbelievers own Muslim property by conquest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on disbelievers' ownership of Muslim property by conquest and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Quranic indication and hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Disbelievers own Muslim property by conquest when they secure possession; Shafi'is disagree because it's a prohibited act.

This passage examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the ownership of Muslim property seized by disbelievers in dar al-harb. The Hanafi school holds that such property becomes owned by the captors upon secure possession (ihraz), while the Shafi'i school denies ownership because they consider the act of forcible seizure prohibited. The debate involves Quranic interpretation, hadith evidence, and a fundamental usul principle about prohibited acts not producing legal consequences. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical rulings for recovering property from enemy hands.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate the Hanafi school's ruling: disbelievers own Muslim property by conquest (istila') when they have secure possession (ihraz) in dar al-harb.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that the Shafi'i school holds the opposite view: disbelievers do not own Muslim property by conquest.
The Shafi'i position is based on a usul principle, not on direct textual evidence.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i argument: a prohibited act (muharram) cannot produce a valid legal consequence, so istila' cannot cause ownership.
This principle is applied elsewhere, e.g., zina not establishing the prohibition of affinity by marriage.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafis counter: the Quranic indication (isharat al-nass) from Surah al-Hashr is the primary proof; the hadiths reinforce it; the Shafi'i principle is not absolute and is overridden by textual evidence.
The hadith of 'Aqil is cited as strong evidence because the Prophet validated a sale by a disbeliever who had seized the property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: For Hanafis, the original owner can reclaim property only before distribution in fay'; after distribution, he must pay the price. For Shafi'is, the original owner always retains ownership and can reclaim without payment.
This affects post-conflict property rights rulings.

مَلَكُوه) : هذا عندنا. يدل على أنهم لم يملكوا الناقة بالاستيلاء؛ لعدم وجود الإحراز بدار الحرب، كذا في «فتح القدير». [قوله: مَلَكُوه؛ قد مرَّ وجه تملك مال الكفار وسييجيء وجه تملك مالنا بالاستيلاء، ويشهد له قوله في سورة الحشر عند ذكر مصارف الفيء: لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَارِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ الآية، حيث سمى الصحابة الذين هاجروا من مكة إلى المدينة وأخرجهم الكفار وتسلطوا على دورهم وأموالهم فقراء مع كونهم ذوي أموال وبيوت بمكة. فَعُلِمَ منه بإشارة النص أن الكفار حين استولوا على أموالهم ملكوها فصاروا فقراء مستحقين لأموال الصدقات وغيرها. ويشهد له من الأحاديث ما أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً: «فيما أحرزه العدو فاستنقذه المسلمون منهم إن وجده صاحبه قبل أن يقسم فهو أحق به، وإن وجده بعدما قسم فإن شاء أخذه بالثمن». وأخرج الطبراني عن جابر بن سمرة: «أصاب العدو ناقة رجل من بني سليم، ثم اشتراها رجل من المسلمين فعرفها صاحبها، فأتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فأخبره فأمره أن يأخذها بالثمن الذي اشتراها به صاحبها من العدو، وإلا يخلي بينها وبينه». ومثله أخرجه أبو داود في كتاب «المراسيل». وأخرج الدارقطني وابن عدي وغيرهما مرفوعاً: «من وجد ماله في الفيء قبل أن يقسم فهو له، ومن وجده بعدما قسم فليس له شيء». فهذه الأخبار تثبت ما ذكرنا، فإنه لولا أن الكفار يملكون أموالنا بالاستيلاء والإحراز لما صح بيعهم من آخر، ولما اعتبرت قسمتها بعد غلبتنا. لا يقال: أسانيد هذه الأخبار كلها ضعيفة كما ذكره الشافعي على ما نقله عنه الزيلعي وغيره، فلا تقوم حجة؛ لأنا نقول: كثرة الطريق يجبر الضعف على أنه ليس الغرض منها إثبات حكم حتى يضر الضعف، فإن الحكم ثابت بإشارة نص القرآن، بل الغرض منها استئناس تلك الإشارة وتقويتها فلا يضر حينئذ ضعفها. ويكفي في الباب حديث الشيخين وغيرهما المخرج في «أبواب الحج»، المفيد لما ذكرنا، فإنه يثبت منه أن عقيل بن أبي طالب حين خرج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه من مكة، وكان هو إذ ذاك كافراً باع جميع دور النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأجاز النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذلك البيع حيث قال: «هل ترك لنا عقيل منزلاً». [قوله: مالنا؛ أشار به إلى أنه لا يخالفنا في استيلاء الكفار بعضهم على بعض. [قوله: لما ذكر...الخ؛ استدل الشافعي بأن الاستيلاء محظور شرعاً، والمحظور شرعاً لا يفيد الملك، وهذه قاعدة للشافعية: من أن المحظور الشرعي لا يفيد الأمر الشرعي، وقد فرعوا عليها عدم ثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا، بناءً على أن الزنا محظور شرعاً فلا يفيد حرمة المصاهرة. وأما عند الشافعي: لا يملك الكفار مالنا بالاستيلاء، لما ذكر في أصول الفقه.
('They owned it'): This is our position. It indicates that they did not own the she-camel by conquest; due to the absence of secure possession (ihraz) in dar al-harb, as in Fath al-Qadir. [His saying: 'They owned it'; the aspect of owning the property of disbelievers has passed and the aspect of owning our property by conquest will come, and what testifies to it is His saying in Surah al-Hashr when mentioning the expenditures of fay': 'For the poor emigrants who were driven out of their homes and their properties' – the verse, where He called the Companions who emigrated from Mecca to Medina and the disbelievers expelled them and took control of their homes and properties 'poor' despite them having wealth and homes in Mecca. So it is known from the indication of the text that when the disbelievers seized their properties, they owned them, so they became poor deserving of charity funds and others. And what testifies to it from the hadiths is what al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ibn Abbas marfu'an: 'Regarding what the enemy has seized and then Muslims recover from them: if its owner finds it before distribution, he has more right to it; if he finds it after distribution, then if he wishes, he may take it at the price.' And al-Tabarani narrated from Jabir ibn Samurah: 'The enemy captured a she-camel belonging to a man from Banu Sulaym, then a Muslim man bought it, and its owner recognized it. He came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and informed him, so he ordered him to take it at the price the buyer had paid to the enemy, otherwise he should let it go.' And similar was narrated by Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Marasil. And al-Daraqutni and Ibn Adi and others narrated marfu'an: 'Whoever finds his property in the fay' before distribution, it is his; whoever finds it after distribution, he has nothing.' These reports establish what we mentioned, for if the disbelievers did not own our properties by conquest and secure possession, their sale to another would not be valid, nor would their distribution after our victory be considered. It cannot be said: All the chains of these reports are weak as al-Shafi'i mentioned according to what al-Zaylai and others narrated from him, so they do not establish proof; because we say: The multiplicity of paths compensates for weakness, and moreover the purpose is not to establish a ruling so that weakness would harm, for the ruling is established by the indication of the text of the Quran; rather the purpose is to seek comfort from that indication and strengthen it, so weakness does not harm at that point. And sufficient in this chapter is the hadith of the two Shaykhs and others recorded in the 'Chapters of Hajj', which benefits what we mentioned, for it establishes that 'Aqil ibn Abi Talib, when the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his Companions left Mecca, and he was then a disbeliever, sold all the houses of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, validated that sale when he said: 'Did 'Aqil leave us a house?' [His saying: 'our property'; he indicated by it that he does not disagree with us regarding disbelievers' seizure of each other's property.] [His saying: 'Due to what was mentioned...etc.'; Al-Shafi'i argued that seizure is prohibited by Sharia, and what is prohibited by Sharia does not confer ownership, and this is a principle for the Shafi'is: that a Sharia prohibition does not produce a Sharia consequence. They applied it to the non-establishment of the prohibition of affinity by marriage due to zina, based on the fact that zina is prohibited by Sharia so it does not produce the prohibition of affinity. As for al-Shafi'i: the disbelievers do not own our property by conquest, due to what was mentioned in usul al-fiqh.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether disbelievers (kuffar) can own the property of Muslims by means of military conquest (istila') in dar al-harb. The Hanafi position is that they do own it, while the Shafi'i school denies ownership. The commentary begins by referencing the previous discussion about a specific case involving a she-camel, then proceeds to establish the Hanafi view. The primary Quranic evidence is from Surah al-Hashr (59:8), where Allah calls the Muhajirun 'poor' (fuqara') despite them owning property in Mecca. The implication is that when the disbelievers took control of their homes and wealth, they became the owners, thus rendering the Muhajirun poor and eligible for zakat. This is considered an 'isharat al-nass' (indication of the text) — a Hanafi usul principle. Several hadiths are then cited, including those from al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, al-Tabarani, and Abu Dawud, which show that when Muslims recover property from disbelievers, the original owner has a right to it only before distribution, implying that the disbelievers' ownership was effective. The hadith about 'Aqil ibn Abi Talib selling the Prophet's houses after the Hijra is presented as conclusive proof, since the Prophet (peace be upon him) acknowledged the validity of that sale by asking 'Did 'Aqil leave us a house?' This indicates that the disbelievers owned those houses by conquest. The passage then addresses the Shafi'i objection that such hadiths are weak in chain. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, multiple chains compensate for weakness (a principle of hadith criticism); second, these hadiths are not the primary proof — the Quranic indication is sufficient, and the hadiths merely reinforce it. Then the Shafi'i argument from usul is presented: that seizure (istila') is a prohibited act (mahzur) and a prohibited act cannot be a cause of ownership because a prohibited act cannot produce a valid legal consequence. The Hanafi school does not accept this principle in its absolute form; rather, ownership by conquest is established by explicit textual evidence. The passage also notes that the Shafi'is apply their principle to the case of zina not establishing the prohibition of affinity (hurmat al-musahara). Thus, the Hanafi position is that disbelievers do own Muslim property by conquest, which has practical implications for the validity of sales by disbelievers and the distribution of spoils (fay').

Key Terms
استيلاء (istila'): Military conquest and seizure of property from enemies in dar al-harb.
إحراز (ihraz): The act of secure possession in dar al-harb, i.e., bringing the property into the dominion of the disbelievers such that it is no longer accessible to the original owner.
فيء (fay'): Property taken from disbelievers without warfare (or through peace treaty, etc.), as distinct from ghanimah (war booty).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the Quranic verse in Surah al-Hashr (59:8) indicate that disbelievers own Muslim property by conquest?
A) Because the verse explicitly states that disbelievers become owners.
B) Because the Muhajirun are called 'poor' after their properties were taken, implying the disbelievers became owners.
C) Because the verse was revealed after the Battle of Badr.
D) Because the Muhajirun gave up their properties voluntarily.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that disbelievers own Muslim property by conquest upon secure possession, supported by Quranic indication and hadith, with practical effects on recovery rights.

Disbelievers' Ownership through Seizure – Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on ownership through seizure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that disbelievers own Muslim property by seizure and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of prohibited acts and legal effects.

Core Idea
A prohibited act can still yield legal effects if its ugliness is incidental, not inherent.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi–Shafi'i dispute over whether disbelievers acquire ownership of Muslim property through forcible seizure (istila') during warfare. The Hanafi school argues yes, relying on hadith and the story of 'Aqil's sale of the Prophet's houses. The Shafi'i school argues no, based on the principle that a legally prohibited act cannot produce a legal effect. The debate centers on the nature of the prohibition: is it inherent (qabih li-'aynihi) or incidental (qabih li-ghayrihi)? Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
First, articulate the Hanafi ruling: disbelievers own what they seize from Muslims. Identify the hadith evidence: 'Whoever finds his property in the fay' before division, it is his' and the story of 'Aqil's sale of the Prophet's houses, which the Prophet acknowledged.
Note that the Hanafis use these as proof that ownership transfers to the disbeliever.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its principle
The Shafi'i school objects that seizure is prohibited (haram), and a prohibited act cannot confer ownership. This is a general usul principle: mahzur does not produce legal effects. They analogize to fornication not establishing marital affinity.
This principle is central to the Shafi'i argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: the distinction between inherent and incidental prohibition
The Hanafis respond that the prohibition of seizure is not for the act itself but for the inviolability of Muslim property (qabih li-ghayrihi). Once the property is seized and held, that inviolability ceases, so the prohibition no longer applies in worldly matters. The act's ugliness is incidental, not inherent.
This distinction allows the legal effect of ownership to occur despite the prohibition.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to the Hanafi view, if disbelievers sell the seized property to another, the sale is valid, and when Muslims recapture it, it must be divided as booty. According to Shafi'i, the original owner retains ownership and can reclaim it without division.
This difference has real-world implications for transactions involving recovered property.

وأما عند الشافعي : لا يملك الكفار مالنا بالاستيلاء، لما ذكر في أصول الفقه وأخرج الدارقطني وابنُ عَدي وغيرُهما مرفوعاً : مَن وجد ماله في الفيء قبل أن يقسم فهو له، ومن وجده بعدما قسم فليس له شيء. فهذه الأخبار تثبت ما ذكرنا، فإنه لولا أن الكفار يملكون أموالنا بالاستيلاء والإحراز لما صح بيعُهم من آخر ، ولما اعتبرت قسمتها بعد غلبتنا. لا يقال أسانيد هذه الأخبار كلها ضعيفة كما ذكره الشافعي له على ما نقله عنه الزَّيْلَعِيُّ ، وغيرُه ، فلا تقوم حجّة ; أشار به إلى أنه لا يخالفنا في استيلاء الكفار بعضهم على بعض. قوله : لما ذكر...الخ ; استدل الشافعي له بأن الاستيلاء محظور شرعاً، والمحظور شرعاً لا يفيد الملك، وهذه قاعدة للشافعية : من أنّ المحظور الشرعي لا يفيد الأمر الشرعي، وقد فرّعوا عليها عدم ثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالزني، بناءً على أنّ الزّني محظور شرعاً ، فلا يفيد حرمة المصاهرة التي هي نعمة شرعية، وفرعوا عليها عدم ثبوت ملك الغاصب بالضمان في المغصوب، وعدم الترخّص في قصر الصلاة، وإفطار الصوم للمسافر بسفر المعصية. ونحن نقول : هذه الأسباب إذا وجدت أفادت ترتب أثرها عليها من حيث ذواتها لا من حيث محظوراتها ، ولا ينافي المحظوريّة ترتب المسبب على السبب ; فإنه لا بأس بأن يكون الشيء محظوراً بحسب وصفه مفيداً لشيء هو نعمة بحسب ذاته، ولا يلزم اجتماع المتناقضين لاختلاف الحيثيتين. وقد يقرر الاستدلال بأن الاستيلاء من الأفعال الحسية، والنهي عنها يوجب القبح لعينه، والقبيح لعينه لا يفيد حكماً شرعياً. ونحن نقول : إنه ليس القبح هاهنا لعينه بل لغيره ، فلا يمنع ترتب الملك عليه ، قال الشارح في تنقيح الأصول»: «فإن قيل : النهي عن الحسيات يقتضي القبح لعينه والقبيح لعينه لا يفيد حكماً شرعياً إجماعاً ، فلا تثبت حرمة المصاهرة بالزني، والملك بالغصب واستيلاء الكفّار، والرخصة لسفر المعصية ; فإن المعصية لا توجب النعمة، ولا يلزم أن الطلاق في الحيض يوجب حكماً شرعياً ; لأنه قبيح لغيره، ولا الظهار ; لأن الكلام في حكم مطلوب عن سبب لا في حكم زاجر. قلنا : الزنى لا يوجب ذلك بنفسه بل لأنه سبب الولد، وهو الأصل في إيجاب الحرمة، ثم تتعدّى عنه إلى الأطراف والأسباب : كالوطء، والملك بالغصب لا يثبتُ مقصوداً، بل شرطاً لحكم شرعي وهو الضمان ; لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملكِ شخص واحد، وأمّا الاستيلاء فإنّما نهى لعصمة أموالنا وهي غير ثابتة في زعمهم، أو هي ثابتة ما دام محرزاً ، وقد زال فسقط النهي في حق الدنيا». انتهى قال التفتازاني في «التلويح» عند قوله : وأما الاستيلاء يعني: «إنا لا نُسَلِّم أنه لا دليل على كون الاستيلاء منهيّاً عنه لغيره ، فإنّ الإجماع على ثبوت الملك بالاستيلاء على مباح، وعلى الصيد دليل على أنَّ النهي عنه لغيره : وهو عصمة المحل: أعني كون
As for al-Shafi'i: disbelievers do not own our property by seizure, as mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh. And al-Daraqutni, Ibn 'Adi, and others narrated marfu': 'Whoever finds his property in the fay' before division, it is his; whoever finds it after division, there is nothing for him.' These reports prove what we mentioned, for if disbelievers did not own our property by seizure and possession, their sale to another would not be valid, nor would its division after our victory be considered. It cannot be said that the chains of these reports are all weak, as al-Shafi'i mentioned according to what al-Zayla'i and others transmitted from him, so they do not constitute a proof; for we say abundance of chains compensates for weakness, and the purpose is not to prove a ruling such that weakness would harm, for the ruling is established by an indication of the Qur'anic text; rather the purpose is to seek comfort and strengthen that indication, so weakness does not harm at that time. And sufficient in this chapter is the hadith of the two Shaykhs and others, recorded in 'Abwab al-Hajj', which benefits what we mentioned, for it proves that 'Aqil ibn Abi Talib, when the Prophet ﷺ and his Companions left Mecca, and he was at that time a disbeliever, sold all the houses of the Prophet ﷺ, and the Prophet ﷺ permitted that sale when he said: 'Did 'Aqil leave us any house?' His statement 'our property' indicates that he does not disagree with us regarding disbelievers seizing from each other. His statement: 'as mentioned...' al-Shafi'i argued that seizure is legally prohibited, and what is legally prohibited does not confer ownership, and this is a principle of the Shafi'is: that legal prohibition does not produce a legal effect. And they derived from it that marital affinity is not established by fornication, based on the fact that fornication is legally prohibited, so it does not establish marital affinity which is a legal blessing. And they derived from it that the usurper's ownership by guarantee in the usurped object is not established, and the concession of shortening prayer and breaking fast for the traveler in a sinful journey is not established. We say: These causes, when they occur, produce their effect in terms of their essences, not in terms of their prohibitions, and the occurrence of the effect from the cause does not contradict prohibition; for there is no harm in something being prohibited in its description while being beneficial in its essence, and there is no necessitation of contradiction due to the difference of aspects. And the argument may be formulated that seizure is a physical act, and prohibition of it necessitates ugliness in itself, and what is ugly in itself does not produce a legal ruling. We say: The ugliness here is not in itself but for another reason, so it does not prevent the occurrence of ownership. The commentator said in 'Tanqih al-Usul': 'If it is said: Prohibition of physical things necessitates ugliness in itself, and what is ugly in itself does not produce a legal ruling by consensus, so marital affinity is not established by fornication, nor ownership by usurpation and seizure by disbelievers, nor the concession for a sinful journey; for sin does not necessitate blessing, and it does not necessitate that divorce during menstruation produces a legal ruling because it is ugly for another reason, nor dhihar; because the discussion is about a ruling sought from a cause, not a prohibitive ruling. We say: Fornication does not cause that by itself, but because it is the cause of the child, which is the origin of establishing affinity, then it extends to sides and causes like intercourse; and ownership by usurpation is not established as an end, but as a condition for a legal ruling which is guarantee, lest the substitute and the substituted be united in the ownership of one person; as for seizure, it is prohibited for the inviolability of our property, and that inviolability is not established in their belief, or it is established as long as it is possessed, and it has ceased, so the prohibition falls in worldly matters.' End. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding whether disbelievers (kuffar) own Muslim property that they seize through warfare (istila'). The Hanafi position is that they do acquire ownership, while al-Shafi'i holds that they do not. The author begins by stating the Shafi'i view and then presents Hanafi proofs. First, he cites a hadith reported by al-Daraqutni and others that whoever finds his property in the fay' (booty) before division may take it, but after division it is not his. This implies that the disbelievers owned it, otherwise their sale to another Muslim would not be valid. The author acknowledges that the chains of this hadith are weak, but argues that abundance of chains compensates for weakness, and the purpose is not to establish the ruling independently but to reinforce the Qur'anic indication. He then presents the stronger hadith of 'Aqil ibn Abi Talib, who sold the Prophet's houses when he was still a disbeliever, and the Prophet ﷺ did not invalidate the sale but merely asked if 'Aqil left any house. This proves that the Prophet recognized the disbeliever's ownership through seizure. The author then addresses the Shafi'i principle that a legally prohibited act cannot produce a legal effect (e.g., fornication does not create marital affinity, usurpation does not create ownership). The Hanafi response distinguishes between acts prohibited for their own essence (qabih li-'aynihi) and those prohibited due to an external factor (qabih li-ghayrihi). Seizure by disbelievers is prohibited because of the inviolability of Muslim property, not because the act of seizure itself is inherently evil. Therefore, the prohibition does not prevent the legal consequence of ownership. The author supports this with a quote from 'Tanqih al-Usul' and from al-Taftazani's 'al-Talwih', emphasizing that consensus on seizure of permissible items and hunting shows that the prohibition is for another reason. Thus, the Hanafi position stands: disbelievers own what they seize from Muslims, and this has practical implications for the validity of subsequent sales and division of booty.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء (al-istila'): Forcible seizure and taking possession of property by disbelievers in warfare.
الفيء (al-fay'): Booty or property taken from disbelievers without actual fighting (or before division).
قبيح لعينه / قبيح لغيره (qabih li-'aynihi / qabih li-ghayrihi): An act that is inherently ugly vs. an act that is ugly due to an external factor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the prohibition of seizure (istila') not prevent disbelievers from owning Muslim property?
A) Because the hadith chains are weak and thus the prohibition is not established.
B) Because the prohibition is for an external reason (the inviolability of property), not for the act itself, so ownership still occurs.
C) Because disbelievers are not addressed by Islamic prohibitions.
D) Because the Hanafi school does not recognize seizure as prohibited at all.

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers who seize Muslim property during war acquire ownership over it because the prohibition of seizure is incidental (qabih li-ghayrihi) and does not block the legal effect of ownership in worldly matters.

Prohibition of Sensory Actions and Property Taken by Unbelievers
Debate on prohibition of sensory actions and property seizure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that prohibition of a sensory action indicates inherent evil only when no evidence shows the evil is for something else, to determine the permissibility of retaking property from unbelievers.

Core Idea
Inviolability lost: retaking property from unbelievers is permissible because the prohibition was not inherent.

This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on a key usul principle: does prohibition (nahy) of a sensory action imply inherent evil (qabah li-aynihi) or can it be overridden? The practical issue is whether Muslims may own property retaken from unbelievers who had seized it. The Hanafi school argues that the prohibition was due to the property's inviolability, which lapses once taken into dar al-harb, so retaking is permissible and ownership is established. The Shafi'i school counters that the prohibition is inherent, blocking ownership. Understanding how the Hanafi school uses evidence to override the default rule is crucial for similar cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on retaking property
Identify what the Hanafi school rules: retaking property from unbelievers who have seized it is permissible (mubah) and ownership is established. Note the key condition: the property must have been secured in dar al-harb, losing its inviolability.
This is the mufta bihi ruling.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position and its basis
The Shafi'i school holds that the prohibition of seizure (a sensory action) is inherent, so the action is inherently evil and cannot produce any legal effect like ownership. Thus retaking is prohibited.
The Shafi'i rely on the general principle that nahy of sensory actions implies qabah li-aynihi.
3
Follow the Hanafi response: evidence overrides the default
The Hanafi school responds that the default rule (nahy of sensory actions indicates inherent evil) is overridden by evidence. Here, the evidence is the loss of inviolability (isma) because the property is now in dar al-harb. The evil of the prohibition was for the temporary inviolability, not the act itself.
This is an application of the rule: 'when evidence shows the evil is for something else, the action is qabih li-ghayrihi and legal effects follow.'
4
Analyze the proof: inviolability conditional on security
The Hanafi school proves that inviolability depends on the property being secured (muhraz) by hand or by abode. Once the unbelievers secure it in dar al-harb, the security ceases and inviolability falls. Thus the property becomes like non-inviolable property (e.g., game).
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the nature of isma.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: Hanafi allows Muslims to own the retaken property; Shafi'i prohibits ownership and requires return if possible.
This shows how an usul difference yields a fiqh difference.
