And the inviolability of our property is not established in their belief; because they believe in its permissibility and ownership by seizure, so they are, regarding the addressee with the establishment of the inviolability of our property, like one who has not been reached by the address from among the believers, so their seizure of us is like their seizure of game.
And since there is here a suspicion that it might be said: We do not concede that inviolability is not established in their belief, rather they know that, and they only deny stubbornly, he pointed to another answer: which is that inviolability is only established as long as the property is kept secure by hand actually, or by the abode, and after their seizure and their securing it in the abode of war, the securing which is the cause of inviolability has ceased, so inviolability falls, so seizure is no longer prohibited, and seizure is a continuous action having the ruling of initiation in the state of continuation, so after securing it in the abode of war, it becomes as if he initially seized property that is not inviolable, so he owns it, like a Muslim seizes game. End.
[1] His saying: 'necessitates' ; its apparent meaning is that ugliness is established by prohibition, just as beauty is established by command, and this is the view of the Ash'aris that beauty and ugliness are shar'i, and in our view they are rational, established before the coming of the Sharia, so it is better to say: 'indicates' instead of 'necessitates', which is what the commentator chose in al-Tanqih, and he said in al-Tawdih: Know that prohibition indicates ugliness, and we only chose the word indication because we mentioned that Allah only prohibits a thing due to its ugliness, not that prohibition establishes ugliness. End.

(1)
And in al-Talwih: 'The summary: Prohibition of a sensory action is carried, when unrestricted, upon ugliness in itself: i.e., for its essence or its part, and by means of a context it is carried upon ugliness for something else, so that something else if it is a description subsisting in the prohibited thing, it is like ugliness in itself, and if it is adjacent and separate from it, then no.
And prohibition of a shar'i action is carried, when unrestricted, upon ugliness for something else, and by means of a context upon ugliness in itself, and al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, says the opposite, and the fruit of that is whether rulings are attached to it or not.'

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the ruling on property seized by unbelievers from Muslims and subsequently retaken by Muslims. The discussion begins with the principle that prohibition of a sensory action (fi'l hissi) implies that the action is inherently evil (qabih li-'aynihi). The commentator clarifies that for the Hanafis, this is a matter of rational beauty and ugliness, not a matter of revelation establishing them. The passage then addresses the specific case: when unbelievers occupy Muslim lands and take property, they do not recognize the inviolability (isma) of Muslim property because they believe it is permissible for them to own it by seizure. Therefore, their seizure is like their seizure of game (which is lawful). The commentator then anticipates an objection: the unbelievers might know the truth but deny it stubbornly. The response is that inviolability depends on the property being in a secure place (muhraz) by hand or by abode (dar al-Islam). Once the unbelievers have taken the property into dar al-harb, the security (ihraz) is lost, and hence the inviolability is lost. Consequently, retaking such property is not prohibited, and the Muslim can own it just as one owns game. The passage then dives into the usul principle: the difference between sensory and shar'i actions. For sensory actions (like the physical act of seizure), prohibition at face value indicates inherent evil. For shar'i actions (like a contract of sale), prohibition indicates evil due to something else (qabih li-ghayrihi). The Hanafi position is that for sensory actions, the prohibition implies inherent evil unless there is evidence to the contrary. In this case, there is evidence that the evil of seizure is for something else (the temporary inviolability), so the legal effects (like ownership) follow despite the prohibition. The Shafi'i position is the reverse: for shar'i actions, prohibition implies inherent evil. The commentator concludes that the agreed point is that something that is inherently evil (qabih li-'aynihi) does not produce a shar'i ruling (like ownership). However, ugliness for something else does produce a ruling with dislike (karaha). The Hanafis argue that the prohibition of seizure here is for something else (the lost inviolability), so ownership can be established. The passage is a thorough defense of the Hanafi position using usul principles.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition; a command to abstain from an action. In this passage, it is discussed whether prohibition establishes the ugliness (qabah) of an action or merely indicates it.
القبح لعينه (al-qabah li-'aynihi): Ugliness in itself; an action that is evil by its own essence or by a part of it. Such action does not produce any shar'i ruling (e.g., ownership).
القبح لغيره (al-qabah li-ghayrihi): Ugliness for something else; an action that is evil due to an external factor, such as a temporary prohibition. Such actions can still produce legal effects (like ownership) but with dislike (karaha).
عصمة (isma): Inviolability; the legal protection of property or life. In this context, the inviolability of Muslim property in the hands of Muslims. It ceases when the property is taken by unbelievers into dar al-harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider retaking property from unbelievers after their seizure permissible despite the general rule that prohibition of sensory actions indicates inherent evil?
A) Because unbelievers are not subject to Islamic law.
B) Because the inviolability of the property is lost once it is secured in dar al-harb, providing evidence that the evil is for something else.
C) Because the prohibition of sensory actions only applies to Muslims, not to the act itself.
D) Because the Shafi'i view is incorrect and the Hanafi view is the only valid one.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows ownership of property retaken from unbelievers because inviolability is conditional; once lost, the prohibition of seizure is for something else, not inherent.

Loss of Property Inviolability After Enemy Capture
Explains the basis for loss of ismah in property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that loss of effective possession (ihraz) removes the worldly inviolability of property, and apply it to scenarios of enemy capture.

Core Idea
Property is originally permissible; inviolability depends on owner's ability to benefit. When that is lost, it returns to being mubah.

When disbelievers seize Muslim property, does it become lawful for them? The Hanafi school distinguishes between worldly and Hereafter rulings. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that the key factor is not the enemy's belief but the loss of effective possession (ihraz). Property is originally mubah, and inviolability is a temporary protection to facilitate benefit. Once the owner can no longer benefit due to enemy control, the property reverts to its original permissibility in worldly terms, though sin remains in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the scenario: disbelievers capture Muslim property. The question is whether it becomes lawful for them in worldly terms. The text states the primary ruling that the prohibition falls.
2
Understand the basis of inviolability
Recognize that ismah (inviolability) for property is conditional upon effective possession (ihraz) and the owner's ability to benefit. When the enemy gains control, these conditions cease, and ismah is lost.
Refer to the Quranic principle 'He created for you all that is in the earth' (2:29) indicating original permissibility.
3
Analyze the objection
An objector claims that if the ruling is based on the enemy's belief, then it would also permit taking free Muslims' lives and liberty. The text refutes this by showing the ruling is based on loss of ihraz, not belief.
4
Distinguish property from persons
Understand why the analogy fails: free Muslims have inherent inviolability due to freedom and Islam, which is not lost by capture. Property is originally mubah and only gains protection through ownership and benefit.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding a problematic extension of the ruling.
5
Confirm the evidence
The passage cites the Quranic verse as a proof that property is created for everyone's benefit, implying original permissibility. Inviolability is a secondary ruling for societal benefit, and when that benefit is no longer possible, the original state returns.
The evidence is settled (qati) in transmission but open to interpretation (zanni) in its legal implication.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في زعمهم، وليس لنا ولاية الالتزام، فسقط النهي في حق الدُّنيا، إذ العصمة إنما كانت ثابتة ما دامَ محرزاً بدارنا ؛ لتيقن التمكن من الانتفاع ، فإذا زال الإحرازُ سقطت العصمة.
[1]قوله : في زعمهم ؛ اعترض عليه : بأنّ المدعى كون استيلائهم سبباً للتملك في نفس الأمر، والدليل إنما يدلّ على كونه سبباً في زعمهم، ولو صح هذا لزم تملكهم أحرارنا ، وإباحة دمائنا لهم ؛ لأنهم يعتقدون إباحتهما أيضاً.
وأجيب عنه : بأنّ مدار المسألة ليس اعتقادهم الإباحة ، بل هي العصمة، وهي في الرقاب متأكدة بالحرية المتأكدة بالإسلام فلا يحتمل السقوط، ولا يقاس المال على الذات، فإن المال مباح في الأصل، بخلاف الآدمي فإنّه خُلِقَ مالكاً لأشياء، محترماً معصوماً بنفسه. 
[٢]قوله : وليس لنا ؛ أي ولاية لأهل الإسلام على إلزامهم بكون المال معصوماً. 
[٣]قوله : فسقط النّهي ؛ أي النهي عن الاستيلاء سقط عنهم في حق أحكام الدنيا ؛ لعدم وجود الإلتزام والإلزام وإن بقى ذلك في حق الآخرة.
[٤] قوله : إذ العصمة ؛ هكذا في بعض النسخ : بإذ التعليلية، وفي بعضها : والعصمة ؛ بالواو الابتدائية، وفي بعضها: «أو» ؛ وهو الصحيح الموافق لعبارة «تنقيح» الشارح له، وتقرير صاحب «التلويح» على ما مر ذكره.
[0] قوله : إنما كانت ثابتة...الخ ؛ توضيحه على ما في «الهداية» وشروحها : إن قوله : خَلَقَ لَكُم مَّا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا ، ونحو ذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون المال معصوماً لشخص أصلاً ، بل يكون كلّ شيء بحيث ينتفع به كلّ إنسان. وإنما تثبت العصمة في المال لمن تثبت له مسلماً كان أو كافراً، المنافي لضرورة تمكن المالك من الانتفاع ودفع الحاجة، فإنه لولا ذلك لكان لكل أحد سبيل التعرض إلى كلّ مال، فلا تحصل المصلحة والمنفعة للمالك ، فإذا زال التمكن من الانتفاع باستيلاء الكفار عاد مباحاً كما كان.
مع وجود الدليل
In their claim, and we have no authority to bind them, so the prohibition ceases in worldly matters, since inviolability was only established as long as it was held in our land, due to certainty of ability to benefit. So if holding ceases, inviolability falls. [1] His statement: 'in their claim' – an objection is raised: the claim is that their takeover is a cause for ownership in reality, but the evidence only indicates it as a cause in their claim. If this were correct, it would necessitate their ownership of our free persons and permissibility of our blood for them, because they also believe them permissible. Answer: The basis of the issue is not their belief of permissibility, but rather inviolability. And inviolability in persons is strengthened by freedom strengthened by Islam, so it cannot fall. Wealth cannot be analogized to the self, for wealth is originally permissible, unlike the human who is created as an owner of things, respected and inviolable by himself. [2] His statement: 'and we have no' – meaning no authority for the people of Islam to obligate them that wealth is inviolable. [3] His statement: 'so the prohibition falls' – meaning the prohibition on taking falls regarding worldly rulings, due to lack of obligation and enforcement, though it remains in the Hereafter. [4] His statement: 'since inviolability' – thus in some copies with 'idh' of cause, in some with 'wa al-ismah' with waw of inception, in some 'aw' – and it is correct according to the expression of 'Tanqih' and the author of 'Talwih' as mentioned. [0] His statement: 'was only established...' – clarification according to 'Hidayah' and its commentaries: His saying 'He created for you all that is in the earth' and similar implies that wealth is not inviolable for anyone originally, but everything is such that every person can benefit from it. Inviolability in wealth is only established for the one for whom it is established, Muslim or disbeliever, contrary to the necessity of the owner's ability to benefit and fulfill need. For if not, everyone would have a way to attack every wealth, so benefit and advantage for the owner would not occur. So when the ability to benefit ceases due to disbelievers' takeover, it returns to being permissible as it was. With the existence of the evidence.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn statement that when disbelievers seize Muslim property, the prohibition on their seizure falls in worldly rulings because the property's inviolability (ismah) depends on the owner's ability to benefit from it. The text begins by addressing an objection: if the ruling is based on the disbelievers' belief that it is permissible, then they would also own our free persons and shed our blood, since they believe that too. The answer is that the basis is not their belief but the loss of inviolability due to the loss of effective possession (ihraz). The inviolability of persons, especially free Muslims, is inherent and cannot be lost because it is strengthened by freedom and Islam. Wealth, however, is originally mubah (permissible) and only becomes inviolable to enable the owner to benefit. When the enemy takes control and removes that ability, wealth returns to its original state of being mubah. The commentator then explains variant readings of 'idh al-ismah' and clarifies the reasoning using the Quranic verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth' (al-Baqara 2:29), which indicates that everything is originally permissible for everyone. Inviolability is a concession to facilitate ownership and benefit. Therefore, when the enemy's takeover eliminates the owner's ability to benefit, the property reverts to being permissible for them, though the sin (ithm) remains in the Hereafter because the inviolability by Islam is not lost—only the worldly protection due to the change in dominion.

Key Terms
العصمة (al-ismah): Legal inviolability that protects property and persons from being taken or harmed without right.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Effective possession or control, such that the owner can freely benefit from the property and it is protected from others.
المباح (al-mubah): Permissible; property that is not protected and may be taken by anyone, originally or after loss of ismah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view explained in this passage, what determines whether property taken by disbelievers becomes permissible for them?
A) The disbelievers' belief that it is permissible
B) The owner's loss of ability to benefit due to the enemy's effective possession
C) The religion of the owner (Muslim vs. disbeliever)
D) The type of property (movable vs. immovable)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, property captured by disbelievers becomes mubah for them in worldly terms because inviolability depends on effective possession and ability to benefit, which are lost upon capture.

The Runaway Slave Captured by Disbelievers: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on runaway slave ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding a runaway slave captured by disbelievers, explain the rationale of each position, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A runaway slave's inviolability does not end when captured by disbelievers, according to Abu Hanifa, because the slave's ismah is not solely for the master's benefit.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: if a runaway slave ('abd abiq) is captured by disbelievers, does he become their property? The matn states that such a slave is not owned, but the commentary reveals that Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold conflicting views. Understanding their arguments and the underlying principles of inviolability (ismah) and possession (ihraz) is essential for grasping the Hanafi jurisprudence on the spoils of war and the status of captives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories not owned
First, note that the passage clearly states that free persons, mudabbar, mukatab, and even a runaway slave are not owned by disbelievers. This is the baseline ruling from the matn.
The phrase 'even if they take him' signals an exception for the runaway slave, indicating a dispute exists.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The commentary specifies that Abu Hanifa holds the disbelievers do not own the slave even when captured by force, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say they do own him. This is the core ikhtilaf.
If the slave is taken without force, all agree they do not own him (ittifaqan).
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn argue that the slave's inviolability (ismah) was for the right of the master. Since the master's control is lost, the ismah ceases, and the slave becomes mubah (permissible property).
This is a key talil: ismah is tied to the master's right, not inherent to the slave.
4
Examine the objection and response on possession
An objection arises: if ismah ceases only after possession (ihraz), then the initial seizure was of inviolable property, so how can ownership arise? The response employs the concept of an extended act (fi'l mumtadd): once possession is complete, the seizure is retroactively treated as if it was of non-inviolable wealth from the start.
This illustrates the sophisticated Hanafi reasoning on temporal continuity of acts.
5
Refine based on the slave's religion
The passage adds a further distinction: if the runaway slave is Muslim or dhimmi, Abu Hanifa says not owned; if apostate (murtadd), they are owned. For a dhimmi slave, there are two opinions. This shows that the ruling is not uniform for all slaves.
The apostate slave loses ismah entirely, hence becomes mubah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا حرنا ومدبّرنا ومكاتبنا وعبدنا ابقا وإن أخذوه
(لا حرنا، ومدبّرنا، ومكاتبنا، وعبدنا ابقا وإن أخذوه)، إنما قال: وإن أخذوه؛ لأن الخلاف فيما أخذوه وقهروه وقيدوه ففي هذه الصورة لا يملكونه عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله خلافاً لهما، لكن إن لم يأخذوه قهراً لا يملكونه اتفاقاً. لهما: إن عصمته كانت لحق المولى، وقد زالت، فقد صار مباحاً وقع في أيديهم.
لا يقال: فينبغي أن يكتفي بالاستيلاء من دون حاجة إلى ضم الإحراز؛ لأنا نقول الاستيلاء الكامل هو الذي يكون حالاً ومآلاً، والكفار ما داموا في دار الإسلام وإن اقتدروا على المال حالاً لكن لم يقتدروا عليه مآلاً؛ لأنهم ما داموا في دارنا مقهورون بالذات، ويحتمل استردادنا منهم، فإذا أحرزوا بدراهم اقتدروا عليه اقتداراً كاملاً.
فإن قلت: العصمة إن زالت بإحرازهم لزم أن لا يكون الاستيلاء محظوراً؛ لكونه على مال مباح.
قلت: العصمة المؤثمة باقية؛ لأنها بالإسلام، وإن زالت المقومة؛ لأنها بالدار. فان قلت: لما كان زوال العصمة بالإحراز، وهو بعد الاستيلاء، كان استيلاؤهم على مال معصوم حين الاستيلاء، فكيف يفيد الملك؟ قلت: لما كان الاستيلاء فعلاً ممتداً حكم ابتدائه وبقائه واحد، صار بعد الإحراز كالاستيلاء على غير معصوم من الابتداء.
[١] قوله: لا حرنا...الخ؛ يعني إذا استولى الكفار علينا فسبوا أحرارنا ومدبّرينا وأمهات أولادنا ومكاتبينا لم يملكوهم؛ لأن السبب إنما يفيد الملك في محله، والمحل المال المباح، والحر ليس بمال، وهو معصوم بذاته، وكذا أم الولد والمدبّر والمكاتب؛ لأنه تثبت فيهم الحرية من وجه بخلاف رقاب الكفار، فإن الشرع أسقط عصمتهم جزاء على كفرهم فيملك بالغلبة أحرارهم وأرقاءهم.
[٢] قوله: وعبدنا؛ قال في «الكفاية»: قال أبو اليسر في «عين الفقهاء»: العبد المسلم أو ذمي إذا أبق إلى دار الحرب فأخذه الكفار لا يملكونه عند أبي حنيفة، والعبد المرتد يملكونه، والعبد إذا كان ذمياً ففيه قولان.

Not our free persons, nor our mudabbar, nor our mukatab, nor our runaway slave, even if they take him. (Not our free persons, mudabbar, mukatab, and our runaway slave, even if they take him) – He only said 'even if they take him' because the dispute is concerning one they take, overpower, and bind. In this situation, they do not own him according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to the two. But if they did not take him by force, they do not own him by agreement. For the two: its inviolability was for the right of the master, and it has ceased, so it has become permissible and has fallen into their hands.
It cannot be said: then it should suffice with seizure without need to include possession; because we say complete seizure is that which is in both present and future. The disbelievers, as long as they are in the land of Islam, even if they have power over the wealth in the present, they do not have power over it in the future; because as long as they are in our land they are inherently subjugated, and it is possible for us to recover it from them. So when they take possession with dirhams, they have complete power over it.
If you say: if inviolability ceases by their possession, and possession is after seizure, then their seizure was of inviolable wealth at the time of seizure, so how does it give ownership? I say: since seizure is an extended act, the ruling of its beginning and continuation is one, so after possession it becomes like seizure of something non-inviolable from the beginning.
[1] His statement: 'not our free persons...' – meaning if the disbelievers overpower us and take our free persons, mudabbarun, ummahat awlad, and mukatabun, they do not own them; because the cause only gives ownership in its place, and the place is permissible wealth. The free person is not wealth, and is inviolable by himself. Likewise umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab because freedom is established in them from one aspect, unlike the necks of disbelievers, for the Law has dropped their inviolability as punishment for their disbelief, so by force they own their free persons and slaves.
[2] His statement: 'and our slave' – said in 'Kifayah': Abu al-Yusr said in 'Ayn al-Fuqaha': The Muslim or dhimmi slave who escapes to dar al-harb and the disbelievers take him – they do not own him according to Abu Hanifa. The apostate slave they own. If the slave is a dhimmi, there are two opinions.
[3] His statement: 'for the two...' – summary: the inviolability present in the slave is for the right of the master, because the establishment
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn regarding the status of various categories of Muslims when captured by disbelievers. It first states that free persons (ahrar), mudabbar (slaves whose manumission is delayed after the master's death), mukatab (slaves who have contracted manumission for payment), and runaway slaves ('abd abiq) are not owned by disbelievers even if taken. However, the commentator notes that the phrase 'even if they take him' indicates a dispute regarding the runaway slave: if the disbelievers overpower him, Abu Hanifa holds they do not own him, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold they do own him. If the slave is not taken by force, all agree they do not own him. The Sahibayn argue that the inviolability of the slave was for the master's right, and that right has ceased due to the escape, so the slave becomes mubah. The commentator then addresses an objection: why is mere seizure not enough without possession (ihraz)? The answer is that complete seizure requires both present and future control; as long as the disbelievers are in dar al-Islam, they lack future control because they are subjugated and recovery is possible. Only when they take the captive to dar al-harb (or effectively secure him) does possession become complete. Next, an objection about the timing of inviolability loss: if loss occurs only after possession, then the seizure itself was of inviolable wealth, so how can ownership arise? The answer is that seizure is an extended act; once possession is achieved, the ruling applies retroactively to the beginning of the seizure, making it as if the wealth was non-inviolable from the start. The commentator then explains the rationale for not owning free persons and those with partial freedom: they are not wealth (mal) and have inherent inviolability. The text further distinguishes a runaway slave based on his religion: if Muslim or dhimmi, Abu Hanifa says not owned; if apostate (murtadd), they are owned; if dhimmi, there are two opinions. The passage ends mid-sentence in the Sahibayn's argument about the slave's inviolability being for the master's right.

Key Terms
العبد الآبق (al-'abd al-abiq): A slave who runs away from his master.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is promised after the master's death (tadbir).
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract (kitabah) to earn and purchase his freedom.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master.
الإستيلاء الكامل (al-istila' al-kamil): Complete seizure that includes both present and future control, often requiring possession (ihraz).
الإحراز (al-ihraz): The act of taking possession or securing control over something, often by transporting it to one's own territory.

A) All Hanafi scholars agree they do not own him.
B) Abu Hanifa says they own him; Sahibayn say they do not.
C) The slave becomes free.
D) The ruling depends on whether the slave is a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school internally disagrees on whether a runaway slave captured by disbelievers becomes their property, due to differing views on whether the slave's inviolability is solely for the master's right or is inherent; the practical difference affects the master's right to reclaim the slave without ransom.

Enemy Capture of Muslims and Absconded Slaves
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on capture of Muslims and slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on enemy ownership of captured free persons and absconded slaves, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn including the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A Muslim's inherent human immunity prevents enemy ownership; only when fully nullified does the enemy acquire property.

This lesson examines a critical ruling from the laws of war (ahkam al-jihad): when the enemy captures Muslims or their slaves, do they become owned by the enemy? The Hanafi school, through an intra-madhhab debate, distinguishes between free persons and slaves, and between those taken by force versus those not. We will explore the reasoning behind the positions and the practical consequences for war spoils.

Guided Path
1
State the matn ruling and its trigger
Identify what the matn rules and the condition 'even if they take him' that triggers the dispute.
The dispute only arises when the enemy takes by force; otherwise, all agree.
2
Present the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's position (enemy does not own) and the Sahibayn's position (enemy does own when taken by force).
Note that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the proofs of each side
For the Sahibayn: immunity was due to master's right, now ceased, so slave becomes mubah. For Abu Hanifa: immunity due to humanity emerges, making slave like a free person.
The key is the distinction between immunity that incurs sin (mu'aththima) and immunity that ensures value (muqawwima).
4
Examine the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa's argument that complete seizure (istila') requires both immediate and ultimate control; ihraz is necessary, and mu'aththima remains.
This response also clarifies why ihraz is required for ownership of property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: does the slave remain with his Muslim master or become spoils of war? The commentator favors Abu Hanifa.
Also note exceptions: a renegade slave is owned; a dhimmi slave has two opinions.

[١] قوله : لا حرنا...الخ؛ يعني إذا استولى الكفار علينا فسبوا أحرارنا ومدبّرينا وأمهات أولادنا ومكاتبينا لم يملكوهم؛ لأنّ السبب إنّما يفيد الملك في محله، والمحل المال المباح، والحر ليس بمال، وهو معصوم بذاته، وكذا أم الولد والمدبّر والمكاتب؛ لأنه تثبت فيهم الحرية من وجه بخلاف رقاب الكفار، فإن الشرع أسقط عصمتهم. جزاء على كفرهم فيملك بالغلبة أحرارهم وأرقاءهم. [٣]قوله : لهما إن...الخ؛ حاصله : إنّ العصمة الموجودة في العبد لحق المالك لقيام وله : إن العصمة التي كانت لحق المولى، وما زالَتْ ظَهَرَتْ عصمتُهُ التي قد كانت باعتبار الآدمية، فصار بمنزلة الاحرار فلا يملكونه. ونملك بالغلبة حرّهم، وما هو ملكهم. [1] قوله : وله أن العصمة...الخ؛ حاصله : إن العصمة التي كانت في العبد لحق المولى قد زالت بإباقه، فظهرت يده على نفسه فإن سقوط اعتبارها إنما كان لتحقق يد المولى عليه تمكينا له من الانتفاع، فلما زالت عادت فصار معصوماً بنفسه فلم يبق محلاً للملك. بخلاف العبد الذي أبق من المولى، وهو يتردد في دار الإسلام، فإن يد المولى باقية عليه حكماً، فيملكونه إن أخذوه من دار الإسلام كسائر الأموال، وإنما يملكون البعير وغيره من الدواب إذا نفر إليهم لتحقق الاستيلاء، ولا يد للعجماء لتظهر عند الخروج من دارنا بخلاف العبد، كذا في «الهداية»، وغيرها.
([The matn]:) 'Our free person, our mudabbar, our mukatab, and our slave [who has] absconded, even if they take him.' He [the commentator] said: He [the author] said 'even if they take him' because the disagreement is concerning what they take, overpower, and shackle. In this scenario, they do not own him according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. However, if they do not take him by force, they do not own him by agreement. For the two [the Sahibayn]: His immunity (isma) was due to the master's right, and it has ceased, so he becomes permissible (mubah) property falling into their hands. It may not be said: then they should suffice with mere seizure without need for adding 'taking into their abode' (ihraz). Because we say: complete seizure is that which exists both immediately and ultimately, and the disbelievers as long as they are in the abode of Islam, even if they have power over the property immediately, they do not have power over it ultimately; because as long as they are in our abode, they are intrinsically subdued, and it is possible that we recover it from them. So when they take it into their abode (ihraz), they gain complete power. If you say: if immunity (isma) ceases by their ihraz, then seizure would not be prohibited because it is upon permissible property. I say: the immunity that incurs sin (al-mu'aththima) remains because it is due to Islam, while the immunity that ensures value (al-muqawwima) has ceased because it is due to the abode. If you say: since the cessation of immunity occurs by ihraz, which is after seizure, so their seizure was upon protected property at the time of seizure, how does it confer ownership? I say: since seizure is an extended act, the ruling of its beginning and its continuation are the same, so after ihraz it becomes like seizure upon unprotected property from the beginning. [1] His statement: 'Our free person...etc.' means: when the disbelievers overpower us and capture our free persons, mudabbars, mothers of our children (ummahat awlad), and mukatabs, they do not own them because the cause only confers ownership upon its proper place, and the proper place is permissible property. The free person is not property, and he is protected in himself. Similarly, the mother of a child, the mudabbar, and the mukatab, because freedom is established in them to some extent. Unlike the necks of disbelievers, for the Law has nullified their immunity as a punishment for their disbelief, so their free persons and slaves are owned by conquest. [2] His statement: 'and our slave [who has] absconded'; in al-Kifaya: Abu al-Yusr said in 'Ayn al-Fuqaha': A Muslim slave (whether Muslim or dhimmi) who absconds to the abode of war and is taken by the disbelievers is not owned by them according to Abu Hanifa. A renegade slave is owned by them. As for a dhimmi slave, there are two opinions. [3] His statement: 'for the two...etc.' Its summary: the immunity existing in the slave is due to the right of the master, because [the master's] hand is established over him... And for him [Abu Hanifa]: the immunity that was due to the master's right, and it has ceased, his immunity that was due to humanity emerges, so he becomes like a free person, so they do not own him. And we own by conquest their free persons and what is their property. [1] His statement: 'and for him the immunity...etc.' Its summary: the immunity that was in the slave due to the master's right has ceased by his absconding, so his hand over himself becomes apparent. The fall of its consideration was only due to the establishment of the master's hand over him to enable him to benefit. When it [the master's hand] ceased, it [the slave's hand] returned, so he becomes protected in himself and no longer remains a place for ownership. Contrary to the slave who absconds from his master while he is moving about in the abode of Islam, for the master's hand remains over him legally, so they own him if they take him from the abode of Islam like other properties. They only own the camel and other beasts if they run away to them due to actual seizure, and the beast has no hand to become apparent upon leaving our abode, unlike the slave. This is from al-Hidayah and others.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the status of captured Muslims and slaves by enemy forces. The matn states that the enemy does not own free Muslims, mudabbars, mukatabs, or absconded slaves even if they take them. The commentator explains that the disagreement arises when the enemy takes the slave by force and subdues him. According to Abu Hanifa, the enemy does not own such a slave, while the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that they do. However, if the enemy does not take the slave by force, all agree they do not own him. The Sahibayn argue that the slave's immunity (isma) was due to the master's right, and since that right ceased when the slave absconded, the slave becomes permissible property. The commentator refutes this by explaining the concept of complete seizure (istila') which must be actual both immediately and ultimately. As long as the enemy remains in Dar al-Islam, their power is incomplete, and ihraz (taking into their abode) completes it. The immunity that incurs sin (al-mu'aththima) remains due to Islam, while the immunity that ensures value (al-muqawwima) ceases. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the immunity due to humanity emerges when the master's right ceases, making the slave like a free person. The commentator then distinguishes this from other cases: a slave who absconds within Dar al-Islam is still under the master's hand legally, so the enemy can own him by taking; a runaway animal is owned because it has no hand to appear. The passage also includes an exception for a renegade slave, who is owned by the enemy, and a dhimmi slave regarding whom there are two opinions.

Key Terms
عصمة (isma): Immunity or sanctity that protects a person from being enslaved or harmed; in this context, it is of two types: mu'aththima (due to Islam, remains) and muqawwima (due to abode, ceases).
إحراز (ihraz): The act of taking captured property into the enemy's abode (Dar al-Harb), which completes their possession. Essential for ownership according to Abu Hanifa.
استيلاء (istila'): Seizure or overpowering. Complete seizure requires both immediate control and ultimate control, which is achieved through ihraz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the enemy captures a Muslim slave who has absconded to Dar al-Harb by force, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The enemy owns him.
B) The enemy does not own him.
C) The enemy owns him only if they take him to their abode.
D) The slave becomes free.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds that even in war, a Muslim's inherent human dignity prevents enslavement by the enemy, and this principle extends to slaves who abscond.

Reclaiming Property from Booty Before Division
Ruling with proof by analogy from gift law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reclaiming one's property from spoils before division, explain the proof via analogy with gift revocation, and identify the condition that triggers this right.

Core Idea
A Muslim may reclaim his property from booty without payment before division, as enemy ownership is not absolute.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in the law of ghanima (spoils of war). When Muslims capture spoils that include property originally taken from Muslims by the enemy, the original owner can take it back without compensating the soldiers, provided the spoils have not yet been divided. The author proves this by analogy with the revocation of a gift, showing that the right to reclaim is not necessarily tied to ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Condition
State the ruling: a Muslim who finds his property among the spoils before division may take it without paying anything. Note that the condition is 'before division' (qabl al-qisma).
The ruling applies only before the spoils are distributed to individual soldiers.
2
Understand the Scenario
The property was first seized by disbelievers (ihraz) and brought into their territory. Then Muslims conquered the enemy and captured their property, including the original owner's goods.
This is a two-stage process: enemy capture (ihraz) followed by Muslim recovery (ghanima).
3
Analyze the Proof - Analogy with Gift Revocation
The proof is qiyas: just as a donor can revoke a gift as long as it remains in existence, even though it fully entered the ownership of the donee, so too the original owner can reclaim his property from the spoils despite the enemy's prior ownership.
The shared illa is that the right to retract (ruju') is not incompatible with the transfer of ownership.
4
Examine the Objection and Hanafi Response
An objector might argue that if the owner can reclaim, it implies the enemy never owned the property. The Hanafi response: no, ownership and the right to reclaim can coexist, as shown by the gift case.
This addresses a potential logical inconsistency.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
Once the spoils are divided, individual soldiers become exclusive owners; the original owner then cannot take without payment (or must seek compensation). The ruling protects original owners while spoils are still collective.
The division is a key threshold in the law of ghanima.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونملك بالغلبة حرّهم، وما هو ملكهم. ومَن وَجَدَ منا ماله، أخذه بلا شيء إن لم يقسم. [٢] قوله : ماله : يعني استولى الكفار على أموالنا وأحرزوها بدارهم، ثمّ غَلَبَ عليهم المسلمون فغنموا أموالهم، وفيها تلك الأموال أيضاً، فمن وجد ماله بينها قبل أن تقسم الغنيمة بين الغانمين أخذه بلا إعطاء شيء. لا يقال : هذا يدل على أنه لم يدخل في ملك الكفار بالإحراز، وإلا لم يبق للمالك حق في الاسترداد؛ لأنا نقول : ليس من ضرورة الأخذ قيام الملك، ألا ترى إلى أن للواهب أن يرجع في هبته إذا كانت قائمة بعينها مع دخولها في ملك الموهوب له قطعاً. [3] قوله : أي بين الغانمين؛ هذا التفسير، هو الموافق لما في المعتبرات.
(We own by conquest their free persons and what is their property. And whoever among us finds his property, he takes it without anything if it has not been divided.) [2] His statement: 'his property' means: the disbelievers seized our properties and took them into their abode (ihraz), then the Muslims overcame them and seized their property, and among it were those properties. So whoever finds his property among it before the spoils are divided among the spoilers, he takes it without giving anything. It may not be said: this indicates that it did not enter the ownership of the disbelievers through ihraz, otherwise the owner would have no right to reclaim. Because we say: it is not necessary that taking implies ownership? Do you not see that a donor can revoke his gift if it still exists in kind, even though it has definitively entered the ownership of the donee? [3] His statement: 'among the spoilers'; this interpretation agrees with what is in the reliable texts.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that when Muslims conquer a territory and capture spoils, if a Muslim finds his own property (which had been previously captured and taken to Dar al-Harb by the enemy) among the spoils, he can take it back without any payment, provided the spoils have not yet been divided among the Muslim army. The reason is that although the enemy had seized and brought the property into their abode, the original owner's right is not entirely extinguished. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if the owner can reclaim it, then it implies the enemy never fully owned it. He responds that ownership and the right to retract are not necessarily linked, using the analogy of a gift: the donor can revoke a gift while it remains in existence, even though the donee had full ownership. Thus, the property's entry into enemy ownership does not preclude the original owner's right to reclaim it before division. This ruling relies on the principle that booty is not individual property until division.

Key Terms
غنيمة (ghanima): Spoils of war taken from the enemy after a military victory.
قسمة (qisma): Division of the spoils among the soldiers; before this division, individual rights to specific items may still be recognized.
إحراز (ihraz): The act of the enemy taking property into their territory/abode, which some consider a transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The spoils must have been divided.
B) The property must be found before division.
C) The enemy must have not taken it to their abode.
D) The Muslim must pay khums.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim may reclaim his property from booty without payment before division, based on the principle that enemy ownership does not remove the original owner's right, analogous to gift revocation.

Owning Enemy Persons and Reclaiming Pre-Division Property
Ruling with proof: Muslim's property found in spoils before division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim reclaiming his property found among spoils before division and explain the underlying principle of original ownership priority.

Core Idea
A Muslim's property found among spoils before division is returned to him gratis—original ownership overrides the ghaneemin's right.

In the context of jihad, Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between the general rule of owning enemy persons and property by force of conquest and the special case where a Muslim's own property is recaptured. How does the law balance the rights of the original owner with the soldiers' claim to spoils? This passage from al-Umdah outlines the ruling and its rationale, centered on the concept of original (private) ownership surviving enemy capture.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the two categories: (1) free enemy persons and (2) their property – both become owned by Muslims through force (ghalbah).
This is the baseline; the exception comes next.
2
Recognize the exception
Note the condition: if a Muslim finds his own property (previously taken by the enemy) among the spoils before division among the ghaneemin, he takes it for free.
The condition 'before division' is critical.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentary argues that the original owner's right is superior; the enemy's confinement (ihraz) does not transfer full ownership, analogous to a donor reclaiming a gift.
Reasoning based on qiyas (gift analogy) and the principle that ownership does not follow mere possession.
4
Distinguish the runaway slave case
A slave who runs to the enemy loses his master's ismah; his inherent human ismah revives, so he is not owned. In contrast, a slave captured within Dar al-Islam is owned because the master's hand persists.
This distinction reinforces the rule: ismah determines ownership.
5
Resolve the interpretive dispute
Identify the two meanings of 'qism' (division): the correct one is division among ghaneemin, not among the kuffar. The latter would wrongly forfeit the owner's right.
The author of Majma' al-Bahrayn held the minority view, which is refuted in al-Durar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وله : إن العصمة التي كانت لحق المولى ، وما زالَتْ ظَهَرَتْ عصمتُهُ التي قد كانت باعتبار الآدمية، فصار بمنزلة الاحرار فلا يملكونه. ونملك بالغلبة حرّهم، وما هو ملكهم.
ومَن وَجَدَ منا ماله " ) : أي في يد الغانمين بعدما غلبنا عليهم، ولم يذكر هذا ؛ لأنه يفهم من قوله : أخذَهُ بلا شيء إن لم يقسم) : أي بين الغانمين يده كسائر الأموال، وقد زالت فيملكونه بالاستيلاء كاستيلاء بقيّة الأموال، فصار كما إذا ندَّت الدابة إليهم فأخذوه، وكما لو أخذوا العبد من دار الاسلام، فإنهم يملكونه بالإحراز اتفاقاً.

[3] قوله : أي بين الغانمين ؛ هذا التفسير، هو الموافق لما في المعتبرات، وظنّ مؤلف
(1) (الهداية» (٦ : ٨).

And we own by force their free [people] and what is their property. And whoever among us finds his property, he takes it without anything if it has not been divided. And he [Abu Hanifa?]: That the protection (isma) which was due to the right of the master, when it ceased, his protection that was based on humanity appeared, so he becomes like a free person, so they do not own him. And we own by force their free [people] and what is their property. And whoever among us finds his property: meaning in the hands of the ghaneemin after we have overcome them, and he did not mention this because it is understood from his saying: he takes it without anything if it has not been divided, i.e. among the ghaneemin. His hand is like other properties, and it has ceased, so they own it by seizure just as they own the rest of the properties. So it becomes as if an animal ran away to them and they took it, and as if they took a slave from Dar al-Islam, for they own it by confinement unanimously. [1] His saying: And he has that the protection... etc. Its summary: The protection that was in the slave due to the right of the master has ceased by his running away, so his hand appeared over himself, for the fall of its consideration was only due to the realization of the master's hand over him to enable him to benefit. When it ceased, it returned, so he became protected by himself, so he no longer remains a locus for ownership. Unlike the slave who ran away from his master and roams in Dar al-Islam, for the master's hand remains over him legally, so they own him if they take him from Dar al-Islam like other properties. They only own camels and other beasts if they run to them due to the realization of seizure, and there is no hand for the dumb beast to appear when leaving our territory, unlike the slave. (1) [2] His saying: his property: meaning the kuffar seized our properties and confined them in their territory, then Muslims overcame them and took their spoils, and among them are those properties. So whoever finds his property among them before the spoils are divided among the ghaneemin takes it without giving anything. For we say: The establishment of ownership is not a necessary condition for taking, don't you see that the donor can revoke his gift if it remains in its original form even though it has entered the ownership of the donee definitively. [3] His saying: i.e. among the ghaneemin; this interpretation is in accordance with what is in the authoritative works. And the author of Majma' al-Bahrayn in his commentary opined that it means division among the kuffar, and that is a corrupt assumption, which has been refuted in al-Durar and others.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on what happens when Muslims capture free non-Muslims and their property in war, and specifically addresses the case where a Muslim's property that was taken by the enemy is found among the spoils. The author first states a general rule: Muslims own the persons of free enemy fighters and all their property by force of conquest (ghalbah). However, an exception is made for a Muslim's property: if a Muslim finds his own property among the spoils before the spoils are divided among the Muslim soldiers (ghaneemin), he may take it back without paying anything. The commentary then explains the reasoning behind this exception. It clarifies that the protection (ismah) of a slave who has run away from his Muslim master to the enemy camp is restored when he escapes, because the master's control is lost. Hence, the slave becomes like a free person and cannot be owned by the ghaneemin. The author distinguishes this from a slave who runs away within Dar al-Islam, where the master's hand remains, so the ghaneemin would own him if captured. The passage also addresses a potential objection: since the property was already confined (ihraz) by the kuffar, does that not make them owners, so that the original owner loses his right? The response given is that ownership is not a necessary consequence of confinement, as seen in the analogy of a gift: the donor can reclaim the gifted item as long as it remains in its original form, even though it has entered the ownership of the donee. Finally, a dispute is noted regarding the meaning of 'division' (qism). The correct interpretation, according to the author, is division among the ghaneemin (Muslim soldiers), not division among the kuffar before recapture. The opinion of the author of Majma' al-Bahrayn is rejected as corrupt.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalbah): The force of conquest in war that transfers ownership of enemy persons and property to Muslims.
العصمة (al-ismah): Legal protection of life and property; for a slave, it derives from the master's right; when the master's hand ceases, the slave's inherent human dignity revives the protection.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): The act of taking possession or confinement of property in the enemy's territory, which establishes ownership by the enemy under their law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the property is still owned by the Kuffar.
B) Because the original owner's right is superior to the ghaneemin's right.
C) Because the property never entered the enemy's ownership.
D) Because the Muslim fought bravely and deserves compensation.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who finds his property among the spoils before division may reclaim it without payment because his original ownership right is given priority over the soldiers' claim to the spoils.

Reclaiming Property After Division Among Ghaneemin
Ruling with proof: owner reclaims property by paying value after division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reclaiming property after division among ghaneemin and explain the reasoning based on harm prevention and the distinction between pre- and post-division cases.

Core Idea
After division, the original owner must pay the value to reclaim his property; before division, he may take it for free.

Previously, we saw that if a Muslim finds his property among spoils before division, he may reclaim it without compensation. This lesson extends the discussion to the situation after division, where the spoils have been allocated to individual soldiers (ghaneemin). The ruling changes: the owner must pay the value of the property to the soldier who received it. The passage explains the reasoning behind this distinction and addresses a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Ruling
The passage states the scenario: the original owner finds his property in its original form after the spoils have been divided among the ghaneemin. The ruling: he may take it by paying its value on the day the ghaneem took it. Note that the owner has a choice: to take or leave.
The value is assessed at the time of taking by the ghaneem, not at the time of reclaiming.
2
Distinguish the Two Cases
Contrast this with the pre-division case (earlier in the text). In the pre-division case, the property had not entered the ownership of any specific ghaneem, so the owner could take it without payment. Here, after division, the property belongs to a specific ghaneem, so taking without payment would harm that soldier.
This distinction is key to understanding the 'illa (effective cause) for the different rulings.
3
Analyze the Reasoning: Harm Prevention
The text states the ruling is 'because there is prevention of harm from both sides.' The owner would be harmed if he could not recover his property, but the ghaneem would be harmed if he lost his allotted spoils without compensation. Paying the value balances these interests.
This is a clear application of the legal maxim 'Harm is to be removed' (al-darar yuzal).
4
Examine the Objection and Response
An objector argues that even before division, all ghaneemin had a collective right to the spoils, so taking the property for free would invalidate that right. The response is that the original owner's right is stronger and takes precedence—after all, it was his property from the start.
This shows that the owner's ownership right is not completely extinguished by the spoils; it remains potent.
5
Extract the Conditions and Value Determination
The ruling applies only if the property is found 'in its own form' (i.e., unchanged and identifiable) and after division. The compensation is the value at the time of taking by the ghaneem, not at the time of reclaiming. This prevents the ghaneem from benefiting from any increase in value.
If the property has been transformed or lost, different rulings apply (not covered here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1] قوله : وبالقيمة ؛ يعني إن وجد المالك القديم ماله بعينه بعدما قسم بين الغانمين أخذه بأداء قيمته يوم أخذ الغانم : أي هو مخير بين الأخذ والترك، فإن شاء ترك وإن اختار الأخذ أخذه بالقيمة ؛ لأن فيه دفع الضرر من الجانبين، جانب المالك ، وجانب الغانم، وهذا بخلاف الصورة الأولى، فإنّه لا ضرر فيها في الأخذ بلا شيء ؛ لأنه لم يدخل في ملك غانم. فإن قلت : لا شبهة في أنه تعلّق به حق جميع الغانمين من حين غنموا، لا سيما بعد الإحراز بدارنا ، وإن لم يدخل في ملك واحد منهم ، ففي أخذه بلا شيء إبطال لحقهم. قلت : لا بأس به، فإنّ حقّ المالك القديم مقدم على حقوقهم.
[1] His saying: And by the value – meaning if the original owner finds his property in its own form after it has been divided among the ghaneemin, he takes it by paying its value on the day the ghaneem took it. That is, he has the choice between taking and leaving. If he wishes, he leaves, and if he chooses to take, he takes it by its value, because there is prevention of harm from both sides, the side of the owner and the side of the ghaneem. And this is different from the first case, where there is no harm in taking without anything because it did not enter the ownership of any ghaneem. If you say: There is no doubt that the right of all ghaneemin attached to it from the time they took it as spoils, especially after confinement in our territory, even if it did not enter the ownership of any one of them. So taking it without anything would invalidate their rights. I say: No harm, for the right of the original owner takes precedence over their rights.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a ruling on the scenario where a Muslim finds his property among the spoils after the spoils have already been divided among the ghaneemin (the Muslim soldiers). The rule is that the original owner may reclaim his property by paying its value as assessed on the day the ghaneem took possession of it. The owner has a choice: he may either leave the property with the ghaneem or take it back by paying its value. The reasoning given is to balance the interests—the owner's right to his property and the ghaneem's legitimate claim to the spoils they have been allotted. Unlike the pre-division case, here the property has been assigned to a specific ghaneem, so taking it without compensation would cause him harm. The commentary then addresses an objection: even before division, did not all ghaneemin have a right to the property? The response is that the original owner's right is stronger, so taking the property before division does not harm the ghaneemin because it never belonged to any individual. After division, however, the ghaneem has a specific right, so compensation is required.

Key Terms
القيمة (al-qimah): The market value of the property on the day the ghaneem took it, used as the compensation for reclaiming property after division.
الغانمون (al-ghānimūn): The Muslim soldiers who participated in the battle and are entitled to spoils.
المالك القديم (al-mālik al-qadīm): The original owner of the property before it was taken as spoils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
At what point in time is the value of the property assessed when the original owner reclaims it after division among ghaneemin?
A) At the time the owner was originally deprived of it.
B) At the time the owner reclaims it.
C) At the time the ghaneem took possession of it.
D) At the time of the battle.

Key Takeaway
After division among ghaneemin, the original owner may reclaim his property only by paying its value at the time the ghaneem took it, balancing the rights of both parties.

Reclaiming Property Bought from the Enemy: Conditions and Exceptions
Basic ruling with variations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an owner may reclaim property bought by a merchant from the enemy and identify the exceptions where reclaim is not permitted.

Core Idea
Owner reclaims by paying price only if the purchase was valid; invalid purchase or prohibited goods may block reclaim.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling when a Muslim merchant buys a Muslim's property from enemy hands and brings it to Muslim territory. The original owner has a right to reclaim it, but the details depend on the validity of the merchant's purchase and the nature of the transaction. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the key principles with several nuanced exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic scenario and the owner's right
Focus on the core case: a merchant buys property from the enemy and brings it to Dar al-Islam. The original owner may take it by paying the price the merchant paid.
This assumes a valid (sahih) purchase.
2
Determine the condition for that right
If the purchase contract is defective (fasid), the owner pays the market value, not the price. Likewise if the merchant seized it as booty (intihab).
The key difference is between a valid and invalid acquisition.
3
Explore variations in payment method
If the merchant paid with a commodity ('ard), the owner pays the value of that commodity. If the purchase involved wine or swine (impure items), the owner cannot reclaim at all.
Prohibited subject matters void the right entirely.
4
Examine the effect of credit and matching terms
If the merchant bought for a like amount on credit, or for a matching quantity and quality, there is no benefit to the owner, so no reclaim. But if the amount is less or quality inferior, the owner may reclaim beneficially.
The underlying rationale is whether the owner gains any advantage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2] قوله : وبالثمن ؛ يعني إن اشتراه تاجر من العدو أخرجه بدارنا، فإن شاء المالك أَخْذَهُ أَخَذَهُ. من التاجر بأداء الثمن الذي أداه التاجر إلى العدو، وهذا إذا كان الشراء صحيحاً، فإن كان العقد فاسداً أخذه بالقيمة لا بالثمن. وكذا إن انتهبه التاجر من العدو، ولو اشتراه التاجر بالعرض أخذه المالك بأداء قيمة ذلك العرض إليه، ولو كان الشراء بخمر أو خنزير ليس للمالك أخذه، وكذا لو شراه التاجر بمثله نسيئة، أو بمثله قدراً ووصفاً بعقد صحيح أو فاسد لعدم الفائدة، فلو بأقل قدراً أو أردأ وصفاً فله أخذه مفيد، كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره. [٣] قوله : أي إن فُقِئت ؛ صورتُهُ : إذا أخذ الكفار عبداً ودخـلـوا بـه دارهـم ، فاشترى منهم تاجر وأخرجه إلى دار الإسلام ففقأ رجل عينه هاهنا أو قطع يده، فأخذ أرشه ، فالمالك القديم يأخذ منه بكلِّ الثَّمنِ إن شاء، ولا يُحَطَّ من الثَّمن شيء بإزاء ما أُخِذَ من الأَرْش.
[2] His saying: And by the price – meaning if a merchant bought it from the enemy and brought it out to our territory, then if the owner wishes to take it, he takes it from the merchant by paying the price that the merchant paid to the enemy. This is if the purchase was valid. If the contract was defective, he takes it by the value, not by the price. Likewise if the merchant seized it as booty from the enemy. And if the merchant bought it with a commodity, the owner takes it by paying the value of that commodity to him. And if the purchase was with wine or swine, the owner may not take it. Likewise if the merchant bought it for a like amount on credit, or for a like amount in quantity and quality with a valid or defective contract, due to no benefit. If it was for a lesser amount in quantity or inferior in quality, then he may take it beneficially. So in al-Bahr and others. [3] His saying: 'i.e., if it was gouged' – its scenario: when the kuffar take a slave and enter with him into their territory, then a merchant buys him from them and brings him out to Dar al-Islam, and a man gouges out his eye here or cuts off his hand, and he takes the compensation (arsh). Then the original owner takes him from the merchant by paying the entire price if he wishes, and nothing is deducted from the price in exchange for what was taken as arsh.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage handles the case where a Muslim merchant buys property (e.g., a slave) from the enemy and brings it to Muslim territory. The original Muslim owner has the right to reclaim it by paying to the merchant the exact price the merchant paid to the enemy. However, if the sale contract between the merchant and the enemy was defective (fasid), the owner pays the market value instead of the price. If the merchant obtained the property by plunder (intihab) rather than purchase, the same rule applies: owner pays the value. If the merchant traded a commodity ('ard) for the property, the owner pays the value of that commodity. There are restrictions: if the purchase involved wine or swine (which are impure), the owner cannot reclaim because the transaction is invalid. Similarly, if the merchant bought it on credit for a like amount or with a matched quantity and quality, there is no benefit for the owner to reclaim, so he may not. But if the purchase was for a smaller quantity or inferior quality, the owner may reclaim beneficially. The passage also covers a sub-case where the slave's eye is gouged out or hand cut after the merchant bought him. The owner can still reclaim the slave by paying the full price, and the compensation (arsh) taken by the merchant for the injury does not reduce the price owed. The original owner is not entitled to any reduction for the damage.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The price paid by the merchant to the enemy; the owner pays this amount to reclaim the property.
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation paid for bodily injury to a slave (e.g., loss of eye), which the merchant may have received from the injurer.
القيمة (al-qimah): Market value, paid by the owner when the purchase contract was defective.
العرض (al-'ard): Commodity or merchandise used as payment; owner pays its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a merchant buys a Muslim's slave from the enemy with a defective contract, what must the original owner pay to reclaim him?
A) The price the merchant paid to the enemy.
B) The market value of the slave at the time of purchase.
C) The value of the commodity used in payment.
D) Nothing, because the contract was defective.

Key Takeaway
The owner's right to reclaim property from a merchant is conditional on the validity of the purchase and the nature of the transaction; exceptions apply for invalid or prohibited dealings.

Damage to Reclaimed Slave: No Reduction in Reclamation Price
Sub-case on damage and arsh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that when a merchant receives compensation (arsh) for injury to a slave bought from the enemy, the original owner must still pay the full reclamation price and may not deduct the arsh.

Core Idea
Compensation for damage goes to the merchant; the owner pays the full price without deduction.

This lesson covers a specific sub-case within the broader ruling: a slave bought from the enemy is injured after arrival in Muslim territory, and the merchant receives compensation (arsh). The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the original owner may still reclaim the slave by paying the full price, and the arsh does not reduce the amount owed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The kuffar take a slave, a merchant buys him and brings him to Dar al-Islam. Then a third party injures the slave (e.g., gouges out an eye).
The injury occurs after the merchant's possession.
2
Note the merchant receives compensation (arsh)
The merchant takes arsh from the injurer for the loss of the eye or hand.
Arsh is the fixed compensation for bodily injury to a slave.
3
Determine the owner's right
The original owner may take the slave by paying the entire price paid by the merchant to the enemy.
No deduction is made for the arsh.
4
Understand the rationale
The arsh belongs to the merchant as compensation for his loss; the owner's obligation to pay the price is independent of that compensation.
The owner's right to reclaim is based on the original purchase, not the subsequent injury.

Source Text

Recovering Property from Spoils: Value, Price, and Compensation
Commentary expanding three recovery scenarios with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios for recovering property from spoils and apply the correct payment method (value or price) and determine whether compensation for injury is taken.

Core Idea
Original owner reclaims property by paying value if divided, price if purchased, and never takes compensation for injury.

The spoils of war (ghanima) create complex rights among warriors, traders, and original owners. When an owner finds his property after it has been taken by infidels and then recovered by Muslims, the Hanafi school provides detailed rules to balance the owner's right with the rights of those who acquired it. This commentary expands three key scenarios, showing how value (qimah), price (thaman), and injury compensation (arsh) are handled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of recovery
The passage covers three distinct ways the owner's property comes back into Muslim hands: (a) found after division among warriors, (b) bought by a trader from the enemy, (c) bought by a trader then injured. Determine which scenario applies to the case.
Each scenario has a different payment rule.
2
State the payment rule for each scenario
For (a): owner pays value (qimah) on the day the spoiler took it. For (b): owner pays price (thaman) if purchase valid, otherwise value. For (c): owner pays full price and does not take compensation (arsh) for injury.
The rule for (c) is supported by the principle that descriptions do not affect price in a valid sale.
3
Understand the basis of the rule
The ruling uses istihsan (juristic preference) to repel harm from both sides. In (a), the owner's right precedes the warriors' rights. In (b), the trader's valid purchase must be respected. In (c), valid ownership prevents deduction for lost description.
This is not based on explicit text but on reasoning from the objectives of the law.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objection argues that descriptions are sometimes compensated (e.g., in murabaha, shuf'a). The answer distinguishes that those cases involve ambiguity (shubha) or invalid-like contracts, whereas here the purchase is fully valid.
This shows the Hanafi method of resolving apparent conflicts between rulings.
5
Apply to new cases
Consider a modern scenario: property stolen by a non-Muslim army, then sold to a Muslim trader. Use the rules above to determine what the owner must pay.
The same principles apply to any property (slave, goods) recovered from enemy possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالقيمة إن قسم، وبالثمن إن شراه منهم تاجر وإن أخذ أرش عينه مفقوءة (وبالقيمة إن قسم، وبالثمن إن شراه منهم تاجر وإن أخذ أرش عينه مفقوءة) : أي إن فقئت عينه في يد التاجر، فأخذ أرشه، فالمالك القديم يأخذ منه بكل الثمن إن شاء، ولا يحط من الثمن شيء بإزاء ما أخذ من الأرش. «مجمع البحرين» في شرحه: إن المراد القسمة بين الكفار، وهو ظن فاسد، قد رد عليه في «الدرر» وغيره. [١] قوله: وبالقيمة؛ يعني إن وجد المالك القديم ماله بعينه بعدما قسم بين الغانمين أخذه بأداء قيمته يوم أخذ الغانم: أي هو مخير بين الأخذ والترك، فإن شاء ترك وإن اختار الأخذ أخذه بالقيمة؛ لأن فيه دفع الضرر من الجانبين، جانب المالك، وجانب الغانم، وهذا بخلاف الصورة الأولى، فإنه لا ضرر فيها في الأخذ بلا شيء؛ لأنه لم يدخل في ملك غانم. فإن قلت: لا شبهة في أنه تعلق به حق جميع الغانمين من حين غنموا، لا سيما بعد الإحراز بدارنا، وإن لم يدخل في ملك واحد منهم، ففي أخذه بلا شيء إبطال لحقهم. قلت: لا بأس به، فإن حق المالك القديم مقدم على حقوقهم. [٢] قوله: وبالثمن؛ يعني إن اشتراه تاجر من العدو أخرجه بدارنا، فإن شاء المالك أخذه أخذه من التاجر بأداء الثمن الذي أداه التاجر إلى العدو، وهذا إذا كان الشراء صحيحاً، فإن كان العقد فاسداً أخذه بالقيمة لا بالثمن. وكذا إن انتهبه التاجر من العدو، ولو اشتراه التاجر بالعرض أخذه المالك بأداء قيمة ذلك العرض إليه، ولو كان الشراء بخمر أو خنزير ليس للمالك أخذه، وكذا لو شراه التاجر بمثله نسيئة، أو بمثله قدراً ووصفاً بعقد صحيح أو فاسد لعدم الفائدة، فلو بأقل قدراً أو أردأ وصفاً فله أخذه مفيد، كذا في «البحر» وغيره. [٣] قوله: أي إن فقئت؛ صورته: إذا أخذ الكفار عبداً ودخلوا به دارهم، فاشترى منهم تاجر وأخرجه إلى دار الإسلام ففقأ رجل عينه هاهنا أو قطع يده فأخذ التاجر ديته من الجاني، فإن المولى إن شاء أخذه أخذه بكل الثمن، ولا يأخذ الأرش؛ لأن ملك التاجر فيه صحيح، فلو أخذه أخذه بمثله. فإن الأرش دراهم أو دنانير فلا فائدة فيه، ولا ينقص شيئاً من الثمن بسبب نقصان العضو؛ لأن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن على ما تقرر في «كتاب البيوع». واعترض عليه: بأن الوصف إنما لا يقابله شيء من الثمن إذا لم يكن مقصوداً بالتناول، وإلا فله حظ منه كما لو اشترى عبداً ففقئت عينه، ثم باعه مرابحة، فإنه يحط من الثمن بقدر النقصان، ولو أعورت بآفة سماوية لا يحط. وكذا في الشفعة إذا كان فوات وصف المشفوع بفعل قصدي قوبل ببعض الثمن، كما لو استهلك المشتري بعض بناء الدار المشفوعة، فالشفيع يأخذها بحط بعض الثمن. وأجيب عنه: بأن الوصف إنما يقابله شيء من الثمن إذا كان مقصوداً بالتناول في الشراء الفاسد أو ما يشبهه، وفي موضع اجتناب الشبهة كما في الصورتين المذكورتين، فإن المرابحة مبنية على الأمانة، يجتنب فيها عن شبهة الخيانة، والملك في الشفعة للمشتري يشبه الفاسد حيث يجب تحوّله إلى الصحيح. وأما الشراء الصحيح الذي لا يشبه الفاسد ولا يلزم فيه اجتناب الشبهة كما فيما نحن فيه، فالوصف لا يقابله شيء من الثمن وإن صار مقصوداً بالتناول حتى لو فقأها التاجر بنفسه فالحكم كذلك، كذا في «الفتح» وغيره.
And by value if it was divided, and by price if a trader bought it from them, and if he took compensation for his gouged eye: meaning if his eye was gouged in the hand of the trader, so he took his compensation, then the original owner takes it from him with the full price if he wishes, and nothing is deducted from the price for the part of the compensation taken. ‘Majma‘ al-Bahrayn’ in his commentary: that it means division among the infidels, and it is a false notion, refuted in ‘al-Durar’ and others. [1] His saying: 'by value'; means if the original owner finds his property in kind after it was divided among the spoilers, he takes it by paying its value on the day the spoiler took it: that is, he has a choice between taking and leaving; if he wishes, he leaves, and if he chooses to take, he takes it by value; because there is repelling harm from both sides, the side of the owner and the side of the spoiler. This is contrary to the first case, for there is no harm in taking it for nothing because it did not enter the ownership of any spoiler. If you say: There is no doubt that the right of all the spoilers attached to it from the moment they seized it, especially after bringing it to our land, even if it did not enter the ownership of any single one of them; so in taking it for nothing is nullification of their right. I say: There is no objection, for the right of the original owner takes precedence over their rights. [2] His saying: 'by price'; means if a trader bought it from the enemy and brought it out to our land, then if the owner wishes to take it, he takes it from the trader by paying the price that the trader paid to the enemy. This is if the purchase is valid; if the contract is invalid, he takes it by value, not by price. Likewise, if the trader seized it as booty from the enemy. And if the trader bought it by barter, the owner takes it by paying the value of that barter to him. And if the purchase was with wine or a pig, the owner may not take it. Likewise, if the trader bought it with something similar on credit, or with something similar in quantity and description, with a valid or invalid contract, due to lack of benefit. But if with less in quantity or inferior in description, then he has the benefit of taking it. [3] His saying: 'that is, if it was gouged'; its case: if the infidels took a slave and entered their land with him, then a trader bought him from them and brought him out to the land of Islam, then a man gouged his eye here or cut his hand, so the trader took his blood-money from the offender; then the master, if he wishes to take him, takes him with the full price, and does not take the compensation; because the trader's ownership of him is valid; so if he takes him, he takes him with its equivalent. For the compensation is dirhams or dinars, there is no benefit in it, and nothing is deducted from the price due to the loss of the limb; because descriptions are not compensated by anything of the price as established in the Book of Sales. And an objection was raised against it: that description is only not compensated by anything of the price if it is not intended for consumption; otherwise it has a share, as if he bought a slave and then his eye was gouged, then he sold it for profit, then he deducts from the price the amount of the deficiency; but if it became one-eyed by a heavenly affliction, he does not deduct. Likewise, in preemption, if the loss of description of the preempted property occurs by a deliberate act, it is compensated by some of the price, as if the buyer consumed some of the building of the preempted house, then the preemptor takes it with a deduction of some of the price. And it was answered: that description is only compensated by something of the price if it is intended for consumption in an invalid purchase or what resembles it, or in a place of avoiding ambiguity as in the two mentioned cases; for sale for profit is based on trust, avoiding ambiguity of betrayal; and the ownership in preemption of the buyer resembles an invalid one where its transfer to a valid one is necessary. As for the valid purchase that does not resemble an invalid one nor requires avoidance of ambiguity, as in our case, description is not compensated by anything of the price even if it becomes intended for consumption; even if the trader himself gouged it, the ruling is the same.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a base matn concerning the recovery of property (specifically a slave) that was taken as spoils by infidels and then came into the possession of Muslims. The commentary expands three distinct scenarios with detailed legal reasoning. 

First scenario: If the property is found in kind after it has been divided among the warriors (ghanimin), the original owner can retrieve it by paying its value (qimah) on the day the warriors took it. He is given a choice between taking and leaving. The reason given is the principle of repelling harm from both sides. An objection is raised that the warriors have rights attached to the property, but it is answered that the original owner's right takes precedence. 

Second scenario: If a trader (tajir) bought the property from the enemy and brought it to Muslim territory, the owner can retrieve it by paying the price (thaman) that the trader paid. If the purchase is invalid, he pays value. Various sub-cases are discussed: purchase by barter, with wine/pig, credit, etc. 

Third scenario: If the property was injured (e.g., eye gouged) while in the trader's hands, and the trader took compensation (arsh) from the injurer, the owner can still take the property by paying the full price, but he does not take the compensation. The reasoning is that in a valid sale, descriptions of the property are not compensated by part of the price. An objection is raised citing cases (sale with profit, preemption) where descriptions are compensated. The answer distinguishes that those cases involve invalid-like purchases or avoidance of ambiguity, whereas here the purchase is fully valid. This shows the Hanafi principle that descriptions have no monetary value in a valid contract.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīmah): The market value of the property on the day the spoiler took it.
ثمن (thaman): The price actually paid by the trader to the enemy in a valid transaction.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a slave (e.g., gouged eye), assessed as diyah.
وصف (waṣf): A characteristic or quality of the property (e.g., having both eyes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the original owner pay to retrieve his property if it was found after being divided among the spoilers?
A) The price (thaman) paid by the spoilers
B) The value (qimah) at the time the spoiler took it
C) Nothing, because his right is absolute
D) The compensation (arsh) for any injury

Key Takeaway
When recovering property from spoils, the original owner pays value if divided, price if purchased, and never takes compensation for injury in a valid purchase.
