Rights When a Captive is Sold Multiple Times
Commentary on the right of successive buyers and original owner when a captive is captured and sold multiple times

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the chain of rights when a captive is sold multiple times and apply the ruling to a similar scenario involving successive purchases.

Core Idea
The first buyer reclaims from the second by paying his price; the owner reclaims from the first by paying two prices.

This passage explains a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on the rights of successive buyers and the original owner when a slave is captured and sold multiple times by infidels. The ruling establishes a chain of compensation to ensure fairness. You will learn the specific steps and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of events
The slave is first captured by infidels and sold to a first buyer. Then the slave is captured again from that buyer and sold to a second buyer.
The captures must be by the same or different infidels? The text assumes separate captures.
2
Determine the first buyer's right
The first buyer has the right to take the slave from the second buyer by paying the exact price the second buyer paid to the infidels.
This is because the capture occurred while the slave was owned by the first buyer, not the original owner.
3
Determine the owner's right
The original owner then has the right to take the slave from the first buyer by paying two prices: the price the first buyer originally paid, plus the price the first buyer paid to the second buyer.
The first buyer is fully compensated for both transactions.
4
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The capture affects the current owner's rights, so the first buyer's claim is primary. The owner's right is secondary and requires indemnifying the first buyer.
This prevents unfair loss to the first buyer.

If a slave is captured and sold, then again, then the first buyer takes him from the second buyer by his price, then his master takes him from him by two prices. [1] His saying: 'if he is captured'; meaning the infidels captured a man's slave and sold him to a trader, then they captured him again from the trader and sold him to another. [2] His saying: 'by his price'; meaning by the amount of the price that the second buyer paid to the infidel seller. [3] His saying: 'from him'; meaning from the first buyer, and he does not have the right to take him from the second buyer by the price; because the capture was not upon the ownership of the master, but upon the ownership of the first buyer. [4] His saying: 'by two prices'; because the slave stood the first buyer at two prices: the price he paid first, and the price he paid second when the master took it from him.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a base matn that deals with a specific scenario of a slave being captured and sold multiple times. The ruling establishes a chain of rights: when a slave is first captured by infidels and sold to a buyer (first buyer), then captured again by infidels from that buyer and sold to a second buyer, the first buyer has the right to take the slave from the second buyer by paying the price that the second buyer paid to the infidels. Then the original owner (master) has the right to take the slave from the first buyer by paying two prices: the price the first buyer paid initially, plus the price the first buyer paid to the second buyer (or the price the first buyer paid when taking from the second buyer? Actually the commentary says the slave stands the first buyer at two prices: the price he paid first and the price he paid second when the master took it from him. This ensures that the first buyer is fully compensated. The reason the first buyer takes from the second buyer by his price (the second buyer's price) is that the capture occurred while the slave was owned by the first buyer, not the master. Hence the first buyer has the primary claim. The master then redeems by paying two prices, covering both transactions. This shows the Hanafi principle of protecting successive ownership rights and compensating losses.

Key Terms
أسر (usira): To be taken captive by the enemy (infidels).
مشتري أول (mushtari awwal): The first buyer who purchased the slave from the infidels after the first capture.
مشتري ثاني (mushtari thani): The second buyer who purchased the slave from the infidels after the second capture.

Redeeming a Captured Slave and the Absconding Slave Dispute
Commentary on capture, sale, and redemption of slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on redeeming a slave sold multiple times after capture and apply the concept of yad to determine ownership of an absconding slave captured by the enemy.

Core Idea
The slave's own yad prevents enemy ownership of his person but not of his goods.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya commentates on the rulings of a slave captured by harbis and then sold repeatedly. It explains the chain of redemption: the first buyer takes from the second for the price, then the master takes from the first buyer for two prices. The commentary also addresses whether injuries reduce the price, distinguishing valid sales from murabahah and shuf'ah. Finally, it delves into the intra-Hanafi dispute on an absconding slave captured with goods, where Abu Hanifah's view hinges on the slave's yad over himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of captures and sales
Trace the sequence: slave captured by enemy (first capture) → sold to first buyer → captured again (second capture) → sold to second buyer. Determine who has the right to take the slave and at what cost.
The first buyer takes from the second buyer for the price the second paid; the master takes from the first buyer for two prices.
2
Understand the rule on compensation for injuries
Recognize that in a valid sale, physical qualities (e.g., sight) are not part of the price. Therefore, the master does not deduct arsh when redeeming, even if the merchant injured the slave. Contrast with murabahah and shuf'ah where intentional defects lead to deductions due to suspicion.
The objection from murabahah and shuf'ah is rejected because those involve corrupt or suspicious transactions.
3
Analyze the absconding slave scenario
A slave absconds (yabiqu) with goods, both are captured by the enemy, and a man buys both. According to Abu Hanifah, the master takes the slave for free but pays for the goods. The disciples say he takes both by paying the price or leaves them.
This difference stems from the dispute on whether enemy ownership can override the slave's yad.
4
Apply the yad principle
Abu Hanifah considers the slave's yad over himself as a barrier to enemy ownership. The slave's yad over goods is incomplete due to slavery, so enemy owns the goods. The disciples argue that capture by force gives ownership, regardless of yad.
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the slave's control over himself is inherent and precludes enemy ownership.
5
Note the automatic freedom for Muslim slaves
A Muslim slave captured by the enemy is freed by law (without wala'). A dhimmi slave similarly, but a harbi slave must be sold and cannot be taken into dar al-harb.
This is a separate ruling mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text

The Book of Jihad / Chapter on the Seizure of Disbelievers and Chapter on the Musta'min

If a slave is captured, then sold, then similarly, the first buyer has the right to take him from the second [buyer] for his price, then his master has the right to take him from him for two prices.

[1] (If a slave is captured, then sold, then similarly, the first buyer takes him from the second for his price, then his master takes him from him for two prices.) So the merchant takes his blood money from the assailant. The master, if he wishes to take him, takes him for all the price and does not take the compensation (arsh) because the merchant's ownership of him is valid. So if he takes him, he takes him for its equivalent. If the compensation is dirhams or dinars, there is no benefit in it, and nothing is deducted from the price due to deficiency of the limb because qualities are not compensated by any part of the price, as established in the Book of Sales.

And an objection was raised against it: that the quality is not compensated by any part of the price only if it is not intended by the transaction; otherwise, it has a share in it. For example, if one buys a slave whose eye is gouged out, then sells him on a profit basis (murabahah), he deducts from the price according to the deficiency. But if it becomes blind by a heavenly affliction, he does not deduct. Similarly, in preemption (shuf'ah), if the loss of a quality of the preempted property is due to a deliberate act, it is met with a part of the price, as if the buyer consumes some of the building of the preempted house, then the preemptor takes it with a deduction of some of the price. And the answer was given: that the quality is only compensated by some of the price if it is intended in the transaction in a corrupt sale or something similar, and in a place where avoidance of suspicion is necessary, as in the two mentioned cases. For murabahah is based on trust, and suspicion of betrayal is avoided therein, and ownership in preemption resembles corrupt ownership because it must be transformed into valid ownership. As for a valid sale that does not resemble a corrupt one and does not require avoidance of suspicion, as in our case, the quality is not compensated by any part of the price even if it becomes intended in the transaction, so much so that if the merchant himself gouged out the eye, the ruling is the same. This is as in al-Fath and others. [1] His statement: 'if captured' means the disbelievers captured a man's slave and sold him to a merchant, then captured him again from him and sold him to another. [2] His statement: 'for his price' means the amount of the price that the second buyer paid to the disbelieving seller. [3] His statement: 'from him' means from the first buyer, and he does not have the right to take him from the second buyer for the price because the capture occurred not on the master's property but on the first buyer's property. [4] His statement: 'for two prices' because the slave cost the first buyer two prices: the price he first paid and before the first taking no. If he absconds with goods, and the disbelievers capture them, and a man buys both from them, the slave is taken for free and the other [goods] for the price, and a Muslim slave is freed.

And before the first taking no). A slave was captured from Zayd's hand, so 'Amr bought him for one mina, then he was captured from 'Amr, so Bakr bought him for one hundred. So 'Amr takes him from Bakr for one hundred, then Zayd takes him from 'Amr for two hundred because it cost 'Amr two hundred. And if 'Amr did not take him, Zayd does not have the right to take him from Bakr because Bakr bought a slave who was captured from 'Amr after 'Amr bought him. So if Zayd took him from Bakr, the price that 'Amr paid would be lost. So Zayd does not take him before 'Amr takes him. (If he absconds with goods), and the disbelievers capture them, (and a man buys them from them, the slave is taken for free, and the other for the price) because, as previously mentioned, they do not own the absconding slave. [1] His statement: 'the slave is taken' means the master takes the slave for nothing, and takes the goods from the merchant by paying the price to him. This according to Abu Hanifah is his [view], and the two [disciples] said: he takes both for the price if he wishes, otherwise he leaves them. This difference is based on the difference regarding the absconding slave when they capture him forcibly: does he enter their ownership or not? As its detail previously passed. And an objection was raised against Abu Hanifah's madhhab that he should take the goods also for free because when the slave's hand over himself became apparent in his view, preventing ownership, it became apparent also regarding the goods with him. And it was answered with the contrary, which is slavery: that the slave's hand over himself appeared over himself only, so it was apparent in one respect and not another, so we considered it regarding himself but not regarding his property. And it was objected: that there exists the reality of the slave's control over the property before the disbelievers' control, so the disbelievers do not own it because their ownership is only over that over which no one has control. This is as in al-Binayah. [2] His statement: 'and freed' means this is a freeing without an act of freeing, and similarly in the following cases, it is a legal freeing with no patronage (wala') over him for anyone. This is as in al-Durar. [3] His statement: 'a Muslim slave' and similarly a dhimmi, for a harbi is forced to sell him as well and cannot bring him into dar al-harb. This is as in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings concerning a slave captured by enemy forces and subsequently sold multiple times. The author, a Hanafi scholar, explains the rights of the original buyer and owner to redeem the slave by paying the purchase prices. First, if a slave is captured and sold, then captured again and sold again, the first buyer may take the slave from the second buyer by paying him his purchase price. The original master may then take the slave from the first buyer by paying him both prices: the price the first buyer paid initially and the price he paid to the second buyer. The master does not have to pay compensation for any injury to the slave because in a valid sale, physical qualities are not considered part of the price. An objection is raised regarding cases where defects are intentional, such as in profit-based sales (murabahah) and preemption (shuf'ah), where a deduction is made. The response clarifies that those are exceptional cases involving suspicious transactions; in a valid sale like ours, no deduction is made even if the merchant himself caused the injury. The passage then addresses a scenario where a slave absconds (yabiqu) with goods, both are captured, and a man buys them. According to Imam Abu Hanifah, the slave is taken back without payment because the enemy does not own him, but the goods are taken by paying the price because the slave's hand over his property is not complete due to his status as a slave. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) dissent, stating that the buyer may take both by paying the price. This disagreement stems from whether the enemy can own a runaway slave captured by force. Finally, the passage states that a Muslim slave captured by the enemy is automatically freed by law (without an act of manumission), with no wala' for anyone, and a dhimmi slave is treated similarly, but a harbi slave must be sold and cannot be taken into dar al-harb.

Key Terms
أسر (usira): To be taken captive by enemy forces.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid for the slave in a sale, which must be reimbursed to the buyer upon redemption.
آبق (abiq): A slave who has run away from his master.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for a bodily injury, which in this context the master does not receive because the slave's qualities are not part of the price.
يد (yad): Control or dominion over something, used here to describe the slave's control over himself and his property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, when a runaway slave (آبق) is captured by the enemy along with goods, and a man buys both from the enemy, what can the original owner (المولى) take without payment?
A) The slave only
B) The goods only
C) Both the slave and the goods
D) Neither; he must pay the price for both

Key Takeaway
The key point is that in Hanafi law, a slave's own control (yad) over his person can prevent enemy ownership, but this does not extend to his goods, leading to different redemption rules for the slave and his property.

Recovering Muslim Slaves and Goods from Disbelievers: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership of Muslim slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the recovery of a Muslim slave captured by disbelievers, and apply the ruling to a scenario involving an absconding slave with goods.

Core Idea
A Muslim slave cannot be owned by disbelievers—Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn say he can be owned by possession.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the fate of a Muslim slave who, along with goods, is captured by disbelievers. It also addresses a slave purchased by a musta'min. The core difference revolves around whether disbelievers can acquire ownership over a Muslim slave who has absconded. Understanding this dispute requires tracing the legal reasoning (ta'lil) and its practical consequences (thamarah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers two scenarios: (1) an absconding Muslim slave with goods captured by disbelievers, and (2) a Muslim slave bought by a musta'min and taken to dar al-harb. Identify the key facts in each.
2
State Abu Hanifa's positions
For scenario (1): the master recovers the slave for free, but must pay the price for the goods. For scenario (2): the slave becomes free (itq hukmi) without wala'.
3
State the Sahibayn's positions
For scenario (1): the master may take both slave and goods for the price or leave them. For scenario (2): the slave does not become free; he remains enslaved.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on both issues.
4
Uncover the underlying principle
The disagreement traces to whether disbelievers can own an absconding Muslim slave taken by force. Abu Hanifa says no; the Sahibayn say yes. This principle drives the different rulings.
The text cites earlier discussion ('marra tafsiluhu') on this point.
5
Analyze the objections and responses
Two objections to Abu Hanifa are presented: (1) if the slave's hand prevents ownership of himself, it should also prevent ownership of goods; (2) the slave had actual possession of goods before capture. The commentary provides rebuttals for each. Understand how each refines the doctrine.

وقبل أخذ الأول لا، عبد أُسرَ من يد زيد، فاشتراه عمرو بمئة، ثُمَّ أُسر من عمرو، فاشتراه بكر بمئة، فعمرو يأخذه من بكر بمئة، ثُمَّ يأخذه زيدٌ من عمرو بمئتين؛ لأنه قام على عمرو بمئتين، ولو لم يأخذه عمرو، فليس لزيد أن يأخذه من بكر؛ لأنَّ بكراً اشترى عبداً أُسِرَ من عمرو، بعدما اشتراه عمرو، فلو أخذه زيد من بكر لضاعَ الشَّمنُ الذي أعطاه عمرو، فلا يأخذه زيد قبل أخذ عمرو. فلو أبق بمتاع، فأخذهما الكفّار، فشراهما منهم رجلٌ أُخِذَ العبد مجاناً، وغيره بالثمن؛ لما مر أنهم لا يملكون العبد الآبق. وعتق عبد مسلم إلى البائع الكافر، والثمن الذي أعطاه ثانياً للمشتري الثاني حين أخذه منه. أخذ العبد؛ يعني يأخذ المولى العبد بلا شيء، ويأخذ المتاع عن التاجر بأداء الثمن إليه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، وقالا: يأخذهما بالثمن إن شاء وإلا ترك، وهذا الخلاف مبني على الخلاف في العبد الآبق إذا أخذوه قهراً، هل يدخل في ملكهم أم لا؟ على ما مر تفصيله. واعترض على مذهب أبي حنيفة رحمه الله بأنه ينبغي أن يأخذ المتاع أيضاً بغير شيء؛ لأنه لَمَّا ظهرت يد العبد على نفسه عنده حتى منعت من التملك ظهرت في حق المتاع الذي معه أيضاً. وأجيب عنه: بأن يد العبد على نفسه ظهرت على نفسه فكانت ظاهرة من وجه دون وجه فاعتبرناها في حق نفسه دون ماله. ورد عليه: بأنه وجد حقيقة استيلاء العبد على المال قبل استيلاء الكفار فلا يملكهم الكفار؛ لأن ملكهم إنما هو فيما لم تكن يد أحد عليه. وعتق؛ هذا عتق بلا إعتاق، وكذا في الصور الآتية فهو عتق حكمي لا ولاء فيه لأحد عليه. شراه مستأمن هاهنا وأدخله دارهم، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يعتق؛ لأنَّ الواجب أن يُجبَرَ في دارنا على بيعه، وقد زال إذ لا يدلنا عليهم، فبقي عبداً في أيديهم. قلنا: إذا زالت ولاية الجبر، أقيم الاعتاق مقامه تخليصاً للمسلم عن أيدي الكفار. شراه مستأمن؛ أي حربي دخل دارنا بأمان، أما لو أسره الحربي وأدخله داره لا يعتق اتفاقاً؛ لوجود المانع عنده من عمل المقتضى، وهو حق استرداد المولى.
And before the taking of the first one, no. A slave was captured from the hand of Zayd, then Amr bought him for one hundred, then he was captured from Amr, then Bakr bought him for one hundred, so Amr takes him from Bakr for one hundred, then Zayd takes him from Amr for two hundred; because he cost Amr two hundred. And if Amr did not take him, Zayd cannot take him from Bakr; because Bakr bought a slave captured from Amr, after Amr had bought him, so if Zayd took him from Bakr, the price that Amr gave would be wasted, so Zayd does not take him before Amr takes him. If he (the slave) absconds with goods, and the disbelievers capture both, and a man buys both from them, the slave is taken for free, and other than him (i.e., the goods) for the price; because as previously mentioned they do not own an absconding slave. And a Muslim slave is freed to the disbelieving seller, and the price that he gave secondly (goes) to the second buyer when he takes him from him. Taking the slave: meaning the master takes the slave with nothing, and takes the goods from the merchant by paying the price to him. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and the two (disciples) said: He takes both for the price if he wishes, otherwise he leaves. This disagreement is based on the disagreement regarding the absconding slave when they take him by force: does he enter their ownership or not? As per its detail previously passed. And it was objected to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he should take the goods also for nothing; because when the slave's hand over himself became apparent according to him, such that it prevented ownership, it becomes apparent regarding the goods that are with him as well. And it was answered: that the slave's hand over himself appeared over himself, so it was apparent in one aspect and not in another, so we considered it regarding himself but not regarding his property. And it was rebutted: that the reality of the slave's seizure of the property existed before the disbelievers' seizure, so the disbelievers do not own it; because their ownership is only regarding what no one had a hand over. And (his) freedom: this is freedom without manumission, and likewise in the following cases, it is a legal freedom with no patronage (wala') for anyone over him. A musta'min bought him here and took him into their abode, this according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two he is not freed; because the obligation is that he be compelled in our abode to sell him, and that has ceased since we have no authority over them, so he remains a slave in their hands. We said: When the authority of compulsion ceased, manumission is established in its place to rescue the Muslim from the hands of the disbelievers. A musta'min bought him: i.e., a harbi who entered our abode with aman, but if a harbi captured him and took him into his abode, he is not freed by agreement; because there exists an impediment to the operation of the effective cause, namely the master's right of recovery.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute commentary from the sharh of Al-Wiqayah. It addresses two scenarios: first, the recovery of a slave who has been captured and sold multiple times among Muslims, and second, the purchase of a Muslim slave by a musta'min who then takes him to dar al-harb. In the first scenario, the original owner Zayd recovers his slave from Amr for the price Amr paid (200), but only after Amr first recovers from Bakr (the last buyer) for the price Bakr paid (100). This prevents loss to intermediate buyers. If a slave absconds with goods and both are captured by disbelievers, the master recovers the slave for free because disbelievers cannot own a Muslim slave (as per earlier ruling). The goods, however, must be paid for because the disbelievers had valid ownership over them. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the slave (who cannot be owned) and the goods (which can). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the buyer may either take both for the price or leave them, treating the slave and goods alike. The dispute hinges on whether disbelievers can own an absconding slave taken by force. Abu Hanifa says no; the two say yes. An objection to Abu Hanifa argues that the slave's hand over the goods should also prevent ownership, but the answer is that the slave's hand over himself is partial, so it protects himself but not his property. Another rebuttal claims the slave had actual possession before capture, so disbelievers never owned the goods either. In the second scenario, if a musta'min (enemy with aman) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, Abu Hanifa rules the slave becomes free (legal freedom without patronage), while the two say he remains enslaved because the compulsion to sell him in dar al-Islam is no longer possible. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that when judicial compulsion fails, manumission substitutes to rescue the slave from unbelief. If the slave was originally captured and taken by force, all agree he is not freed because the master's right of recovery (istirdad) persists.

Key Terms
آبق (ābiq): A slave who has run away from his master.
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi (enemy combatant) who enters dar al-Islam with a pledge of safety (aman).
عتق حكمي (itq hukmī): Legal freedom without actual manumission; no patronage (wala') for anyone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The slave remains enslaved.
C) The slave becomes free with wala' for the musta'min.
D) The slave is returned to dar al-Islam.

Key Takeaway
The core dispute is whether disbelievers can own an absconding Muslim slave; Abu Hanifa's position leads to free recovery of the slave, while the Sahibayn allow ownership by possession.

Musta'man's Purchase of a Muslim Slave and Entry into Dar al-Harb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation of slave taken to enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the emancipation of a Muslim slave bought by a musta'man and taken to dar al-harb, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
When a musta'man buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, Abu Hanifa frees him; the Sahibayn keep him enslaved.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the fate of a Muslim slave who falls into the hands of a non-Muslim with safe conduct (musta'man) and is then taken to enemy territory. The ruling hinges on whether the judge's authority to compel sale (wilayat al-jabr) extends across borders, and whether emancipation can substitute when that authority is lost. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of istihsan and the limits of judicial power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Scenario
The scenario: a musta'man (a non-Muslim with aman) buys a Muslim slave in dar al-Islam and then takes him to dar al-harb. Determine what legal issue arises.
The musta'man's ownership is valid by purchase, but the slave is Muslim, so the judge can compel sale to prevent subjugation.
2
State the Two Positions
Abu Hanifa holds the slave is freed; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold he is not freed. Note that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The disagreement is on the effect of the inability to enforce the compulsory sale.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's Reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the authority of compulsion (wilayat al-jabr) ceases in dar al-harb, so emancipation is substituted by istihsan to rescue the Muslim. The condition of entering dar al-harb stands in place of the cause (emancipation by the judge).
This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on the principle of necessity.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's Reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that since the obligation to compel sale has ceased, the slave remains in the ownership of the musta'man. No new cause for emancipation arises.
They adhere strictly to the absence of the cause.
5
Identify the Practical Difference (Thamarah)
The thamarah al-khilaf: If the slave returns to dar al-Islam, Abu Hanifa considers him free; the Sahibayn consider him still a slave, so the original master can reclaim him or his value.
This affects how the courts treat returning slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراه مستأمن هاهنا وأدخله دارهم، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما لا يعتق ؛ لأنَّ الواجب " أن يُجبَرَ في دارنا على بيعه، وقد زال إذ لا يدلنا عليهم، فبقي عبداً في أيديهم. قلنا : إذا زالت ولاية الجبر ، أقيم الاعتاق مقامه تخليصاً للمسلم عن أيدي الكفار.

[1] قوله : شراه مستأمن ؛ أي حربي دخل دارنـا بأمان، أمـا لـو أسـره الحربي وأدخله داره لا يعتق اتفاقاً ؛ لوجود المانع عنده من عمل المقتضى، وهو حق استرداد المولى، كذا في «النهر»). [2] قوله : لأن الواجب...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه يجب على القاضي أن يُجبر الكافر إذا اشترى عبداً مسلماً أو ذميّاً أن يبيعه ولا يخرجه إلى دار الإسلام حذراً من ذلّة الإسلام، فإن الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى ، فإذا أخرجه إلى دارهم انقطعت ولاية الجبر عليه، فيبقى في يده عبداً. (3) قوله : قلنا ؛ جواب من قبل أبي حنيفة ، قال العيني في «البناية) بيانه : «إنَّ الحربي المستأمن في دارنا يُزالُ ملكه بالبيع، فإذا دخل دار الحرب انتهت الحرمة بانتهاء الأمان وسقطت عصمة المال، وقد عجز القاضي عن إعتاقه عليه ؛ إذ لا ينفذ قضاؤه على من في دار الحرب، فقامَ شرط زوال عصمة ماله، وهو دخول دار الحرب مقام علّته، وهو إعتاق القاضي». انتهى.

[4]قوله : أقيم...الخ ؛ قال في «الكفاية» (3) : لا يقال : الإحرار بدار الحرب سبب لإثبات الملك فيما لم يكن مالكاً له، ألا ترى أنهم إذا أخذوا عبداً مسلماً في دارنا ملكوه إذا أحرزوه بدارهم، فيستحيل أن يزول ملكه به ؛ لأنّ الإحراز لَمّا كان سبباً لإثبات الملك ابتداء فأولى أن يبقى الملك الثابت بقاء. لأنا نقول : ليس هذا كما أخذوا عبداً بدارنا ؛ لأنهم لا يملكونه بالأخذ حتى تجبَ الإزالة عليهم، وإنما يملكونه بالإحراز بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنهم ملكوه بالشراء، فاستحق الإزالة عليهم بإقامة شرط الزوال مقام السبب.

[ 1 ] قوله : كعبد ؛ أي عُتِق عبد أسلم فجاءنا في دار الإسلام أو لحق عسكرنا بدار الحرب، أو غلبنا على دارهم ؛ وذلك لأنه أحرز نفسه بالخروج إلينا مراغماً لمولاه في الصورة الأولى والثانية ، أو بالالتحاق بمنعة المسلمين في الثالثة. والأصل فيه : أنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لَمَّا حاصر الطائف خرج إليهم من عبيدهم أبو بكرة الثقفي له وغيره مسلمين ، فقال النبي : «هم عتقاء الله ) ، أخرجه البيهقي وغيره.

[2] قوله : ثمة ؛ أي في دار الحرب، وهذا قيد اتفاقي ، فإنه لو خرج مراغماً لمولاه فأسلم في دارنا، فالحكم كذلك بخلاف ما إذا خرج بإذن مولاه أو لحاجته بأمره فأسلم عندنا، فإن حكمه أن يبيعه الإمام ويحفظ ثمنه لمولاه الحربي، كذا في «البحر»).
He [the musta'man] bought him here and entered him into their land. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is not emancipated, because the obligation is to compel him to sell in our land, and that has ceased since we have no authority over them, so he remains a slave in their hands. We (Abu Hanifa) say: When the authority of compulsion ceases, emancipation is established in its place to rescue the Muslim from the hands of the disbelievers.

[1] His saying: He bought him as a musta'man; i.e., a harbi who entered our land with aman. But if the harbi captured him and entered him into his land, he is not emancipated by agreement, because there is a hindrance to the operation of the effective cause, which is the master's right of recovery, as in al-Nahr.
[2] His saying: because the obligation... etc.; its summary: It is obligatory upon the judge to compel the disbeliever, if he buys a Muslim slave or a dhimmi, to sell him and not take him out to Dar al-Islam, cautious of the humiliation of Islam, for Islam is high and nothing is above it. So if he takes him out to their land, the authority of compulsion is cut off, and he remains a slave in his hand.
(3) His saying: We (Abu Hanifa) say; a response from Abu Hanifa. al-Ayni in al-Binaya explained: 'Indeed, the harbi musta'man in our land, his ownership is removed by sale. When he enters Dar al-Harb, the inviolability ends with the end of aman and the inviolability of property falls. The judge is unable to emancipate him because his judgment does not execute over those in Dar al-Harb. So the condition of the removal of inviolability of his property, which is entering Dar al-Harb, stands in place of its cause, which is the emancipation by the judge.' End.

[4] His saying: is established... etc.; He said in al-Kifaya: It cannot be said: Emancipation in Dar al-Harb is a cause for establishing ownership over what one did not own. Do you not see that if they capture a Muslim slave in our land, they own him when they take possession of him in their land? So it is impossible that his ownership is removed by it, because taking possession, since it was a cause for establishing ownership initially, it is more appropriate that the established ownership remains. For we say: This is not like their capturing a slave in our land, because they do not own him by capture until the removal becomes obligatory upon them; rather, they own him by taking possession. Unlike our case, they owned him by purchase, so the removal is deserved upon them by establishing the condition of removal in place of the cause.

[1] His saying: like a slave; i.e., a slave who converted to Islam then came to us in Dar al-Islam, or joined our army in Dar al-Harb, or we overcame their land. That is because he took possession of himself by coming out to us, being disloyal to his master in the first and second scenarios, or by joining the protection of the Muslims in the third. The basis for this: When the Prophet (peace be upon him) besieged Ta'if, some of their slaves came out to him, including Abu Bakra al-Thaqafi, and others, Muslim, and the Prophet said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' Recorded by al-Bayhaqi and others.

[2] His saying: there; i.e., in Dar al-Harb. This is an agreed-upon restriction. For if he came out being disloyal to his master and converted to Islam in our land, the ruling is likewise. But if he came out with his master's permission or for his need by his command and converted in our land, then the ruling is that the Imam sells him and keeps his price for his harbi master, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of a Muslim slave bought by a musta'man (a harbi who enters Muslim lands with safe conduct) and taken to dar al-harb. The matn states the ruling: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave is emancipated; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), he is not. The commentary explains the reasoning. The obligation in Islamic law is that a disbeliever who buys a Muslim slave must be compelled by the judge to sell him, to prevent the humiliation of a Muslim being enslaved. This obligation (wilayat al-jabr) exists only in dar al-Islam. Once the slave is taken to dar al-harb, that obligation ceases. Abu Hanifa argues that since the judge cannot enforce the sale, the slave is freed instead, as a means to rescue him from the disbelievers. This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference). The Sahibayn argue that since the obligation has ceased, the slave remains in the ownership of the musta'man. Abu Hanifa's argument is further supported by al-Ayni: entering dar al-harb removes the inviolability of the musta'man's property, and since the judge cannot act, the condition of entering dar al-harb substitutes for the cause of emancipation by the judge. The commentary also addresses a possible objection: that freeing the slave in dar al-harb would establish ownership where there was none, but this is refuted because the musta'man acquired the slave through purchase (which is a valid contract), not through capture (which requires taking possession). Therefore, removal of ownership is appropriate. The passage then gives a parallel case: a slave belonging to harbis who converts to Islam and comes to dar al-Islam (either by flight, joining Muslim army, or conquest) is emancipated, based on the hadith of Abu Bakra during the siege of Ta'if. However, if the slave leaves with his master's permission and then converts, he is not freed but sold by the Imam and the price returned to the harbi master.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): A harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory) who enters Muslim lands with temporary safe conduct (aman).
إعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave, making him free. In this context, the legal act of freeing performed by the judge or automatically by operation of law.
ولاية الجبر (wilayat al-jabr): The authority of the judge to compel a disbeliever who buys a Muslim slave to sell him, to prevent subjugation of a Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is freed because the authority of compulsion ceases.
B) The slave remains a slave because the authority of compulsion ceases and no substitute cause exists.
C) The slave is freed only if he converts to Islam.
D) The musta'man must sell the slave to a Muslim within dar al-Islam.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to free a Muslim slave taken to dar al-harb by a musta'man, while the Sahibayn hold the slave remains enslaved because the judge's authority to compel sale is lost.

The Musta'min Merchant and the Prohibition of Treachery
Explaining protections and limits for a merchant with aman in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general prohibition on a musta'min merchant harming those who granted him aman, and identify the exception when the enemy king betrays him.

Core Idea
A musta'min is bound by aman: harming hosts is treachery unless they first betray.

This passage from al-Wiqayah establishes a key rule of Islamic international law: a Muslim who enters enemy territory with a safe conduct (aman) must honour that pact. The ruling prevents treachery (ghadr), which is forbidden by primary texts. However, the pact is reciprocal; if the enemy king breaks it first by imprisoning the merchant or taking his property, the merchant may retaliate.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the basic prohibition: the merchant must not harm the blood or wealth of those who granted him aman.
2
Identify the exception
Locate the phrase 'إلا إذا أخذ ملكهم ماله، أو حبسه، أو غيره بعلمه' which allows retaliation when the enemy king betrays with his knowledge.
3
Understand the rationale
Recognize that aman creates mutual trust; betrayal by one side nullifies the pact, permitting self-help.
4
Contrast with the captive
Note that a Muslim captive in dar al-harb has no aman, so the prohibition does not apply to him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن
لا يتعرض تاجرنا لدمائهم وأموالهم إلا إذا أخذ ملكهم ماله، أو حبسه، أو غيره بعلمه، وما أخرجه ملكه ملكاً حراماً، فيتصدق به، فإن أدانه حربي، أو أدان حربياً، أو غصب أحدهما الآخر، وجاءا هاهنا لم يقض لأحدهما بشيء.
Chapter of the Musta'min
Our merchant shall not be allowed to their blood and wealth except if their king takes his wealth, or imprisons him, or other such things with his knowledge. And what he takes out, he owns it as a forbidden ownership, so he must give it in charity. If a harbi gave him credit, or he gave a harbi credit, or one of them usurps from the other, and they come here, the judge shall not decide anything for either of them.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage from the matn of al-Wiqayah, commented upon by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, establishes the ruling for a Muslim merchant who has entered enemy territory (dar al-harb) under a safe conduct (aman). The passage type is a ruling statement with explanation. The basic rule is that it is absolutely prohibited for our merchant to harm the persons, property, or honor of the people of dar al-harb who have given him aman, because this would be treachery (ghadr), which is forbidden by the primary texts. However, an exception is made if the enemy king or his agents first betray the merchant by imprisoning him, taking his property, or similar acts with the king's knowledge. In that case, the merchant may retaliate because the breach of trust has come from them. This contrasts with the situation of a Muslim captive in dar al-harb, for whom treachery is not prohibited because there is no mutual pact.

Regarding property that the merchant takes from them in such a situation, he validly acquires ownership because it is enemy property, but the ownership is legally 'forbidden' (haram) due to the treachery. He is required to give it in charity as a means of purifying his conscience, not for reward, since Allah is Good and accepts only what is good. If he takes goods but has not removed them from the enemy territory, he must return them.

The final part of the passage deals with debts (din) and mutual wrongs that occurred while the merchant was in dar al-harb. If a harbi gave credit to (adana) the merchant, or the merchant gave credit to a harbi, or one wrongfully took the other's property, and then both come to dar al-Islam, the Muslim judge does not adjudicate between them at all. The reason is that the Muslim judge has no jurisdiction (wilayah) over the harbi for actions that took place in dar al-harb, and the musta'min merchant is considered to have not submitted to Islamic law for past actions, only for future ones. This is unlike the case of a valid or corrupt sale where legal rights are established.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person (Muslim or harbi) who enters enemy territory with a safe conduct (aman).
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; violation of a pact or trust.
إدانة (idānah): To give credit; a transaction creating a debt in the counterparty's dhimmah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what circumstances may a musta'min merchant harm those who granted him aman?
A) Whenever he wishes, because their property is permissible.
B) If the enemy king betrays him by imprisoning him or taking his property with his knowledge.
C) If he is a captive in dar al-harb.
D) Never, under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The aman creates a binding pact; harming the musta'min's hosts is generally haram, but retaliation becomes permissible if the enemy first commits treachery with the king's knowledge.

Property Taken Through Treachery: Forbidden Ownership and Charity
Explaining protections and limits for a merchant with aman in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of 'forbidden ownership' (milk haram) and the obligation to give charity for property taken from harbis after a breach of aman.

Core Idea
Property taken from the enemy after their betrayal is owned but forbidden; the owner must purify it by charity.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage: 'وما أخرجه ملكه ملكاً حراماً، فيتصدق به.' Even when the musta'min merchant takes property in retaliation, he does not gain pure ownership. The property is considered haram upon him, and he must give its value in charity. This teaches that Islam does not reward treachery even when it is retaliatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the ruling applies when the merchant takes property from the enemy after a breach of aman by the king.
2
Note the ownership status
The phrase 'ملكاً حراماً' indicates that he owns it technically, but it is forbidden.
Ownership (milk) is recognized for legal purposes, but consumption is prohibited.
3
Understand the consequence
He must give it in charity (يتصدق به) to purify the property, not for reward.
If the property is still in dar al-harb, he must return it; if removed, charity is wajib.
4
Contrast with regular booty
In lawful warfare without a prior aman, booty is halal. Here, because of the aman and the improper method, it becomes haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن
لا يتعرض تاجرنا لدمائهم وأموالهم إلا إذا أخذ ملكهم ماله، أو حبسه، أو غيره بعلمه، وما أخرجه ملكه ملكاً حراماً، فيتصدق به، فإن أدانه حربي، أو أدان حربياً، أو غصب أحدهما الآخر، وجاءا هاهنا لم يقض لأحدهما بشيء.
Chapter of the Musta'min
Our merchant shall not be allowed to their blood and wealth except if their king takes his wealth, or imprisons him, or other such things with his knowledge. And what he takes out, he owns it as a forbidden ownership, so he must give it in charity. If a harbi gave him credit, or he gave a harbi credit, or one of them usurps from the other, and they come here, the judge shall not decide anything for either of them.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage from the matn of al-Wiqayah, commented upon by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, establishes the ruling for a Muslim merchant who has entered enemy territory (dar al-harb) under a safe conduct (aman). The passage type is a ruling statement with explanation. The basic rule is that it is absolutely prohibited for our merchant to harm the persons, property, or honor of the people of dar al-harb who have given him aman, because this would be treachery (ghadr), which is forbidden by the primary texts. However, an exception is made if the enemy king or his agents first betray the merchant by imprisoning him, taking his property, or similar acts with the king's knowledge. In that case, the merchant may retaliate because the breach of trust has come from them. This contrasts with the situation of a Muslim captive in dar al-harb, for whom treachery is not prohibited because there is no mutual pact.

Regarding property that the merchant takes from them in such a situation, he validly acquires ownership because it is enemy property, but the ownership is legally 'forbidden' (haram) due to the treachery. He is required to give it in charity as a means of purifying his conscience, not for reward, since Allah is Good and accepts only what is good. If he takes goods but has not removed them from the enemy territory, he must return them.

The final part of the passage deals with debts (din) and mutual wrongs that occurred while the merchant was in dar al-harb. If a harbi gave credit to (adana) the merchant, or the merchant gave credit to a harbi, or one wrongfully took the other's property, and then both come to dar al-Islam, the Muslim judge does not adjudicate between them at all. The reason is that the Muslim judge has no jurisdiction (wilayah) over the harbi for actions that took place in dar al-harb, and the musta'min merchant is considered to have not submitted to Islamic law for past actions, only for future ones. This is unlike the case of a valid or corrupt sale where legal rights are established.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person (Muslim or harbi) who enters enemy territory with a safe conduct (aman).
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; violation of a pact or trust.
إدانة (idānah): To give credit; a transaction creating a debt in the counterparty's dhimmah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Halal booty.
B) Haram to use, must give in charity.
C) Must be returned to the enemy.
D) Permissible to keep if the enemy started first.

Key Takeaway
Even in retaliation, a musta'min does not gain clean ownership; the property is haram and requires charity.

No Jurisdiction Over Past Debts and Wrongs in Dar al-Harb
Explaining protections and limits for a merchant with aman in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a Muslim judge does not adjudicate disputes arising from debts or usurpation that occurred between a musta'min merchant and a harbi in dar al-harb.

Core Idea
The Muslim judge has no authority over actions in dar al-harb between musta'min and harbi; he cannot enforce rights.

The final sentence of the passage addresses financial matters. If a harbi gave credit to (adana) the musta'min or vice versa, or if one usurped property from the other, and then both come to dar al-Islam, the judge does not decide anything for either. This is because the judge's jurisdiction (wilāyah) does not extend to past acts in dar al-harb concerning harbis, and the musta'min is considered not to have submitted to Islamic rule for those acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of transactions
The passage mentions credit (idānah) and usurpation (ghasb) as between musta'min and harbi.
2
State the judge's ruling
The judge 'لم يقض لأحدهما بشيء' — he does not decide anything for either party.
3
Understand the reason
The judge has no wilayah (authority) over a harbi for acts in dar al-harb, and the musta'min is considered not to have submitted to Islamic law for past actions.
4
Contrast with valid sales
For a valid or corrupt sale (not credit), the rights are established and may be recognized, but the judge will not enforce the debt.
This distinction is important: ownership rights from sales are established, but personal obligations like debts are not enforced.

Ruling on Harbis' Acts in Dar al-Harb When Entering Dar al-Islam
Explaining that ghasb in dar al-harb causes ownership but is not actionable between musta'mins or Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between ghasb and loan in dar al-harb and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument regarding Muslim litigants.

Core Idea
Ghasb in dar al-harb causes ownership; a loan does not—consent and inviolability determine enforceability.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of acts committed by warring disbelievers (harbis) in dar al-harb after they enter dar al-Islam. The central issue is whether a qadi should adjudicate claims of usurpation (ghasb) and loans when both parties become Muslims or musta'mins. The ruling hinges on the presence of consent and inviolability at the time of the act, leading to a nuanced debate between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on ghasb vs loan
Identify the foundational ruling: ghasb in dar al-harb causes ownership, while a loan requires mutual consent and thus remains enforceable in dar al-Islam.
This distinction is central to the entire discussion.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that when the harbis become Muslims, the qadi should at least judge the debt against the Muslim because he has committed to Islamic law.
This introduces the dimension of changed legal status.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response on equality
The text responds that if judgment is prevented for the musta'min, it must also be prevented for the Muslim to maintain equality between litigants.
Equality in court procedure is the principle invoked.
4
Examine the rebuttal about equality's scope
A rebuttal is raised: equality is about treatment in court (e.g., seating), not about nullifying one's right. An example: retaliation for father vs. son.
The rebuttal challenges the application of equality to substantive rights.
5
Determine the final resolution and thamarah al-khilaf
The gloss of al-Hidayah resolves that the limitation on equality applies only when the judge's authority is incomplete, as with a musta'min. Since for two Muslims the judge has full authority, the lack of equality is not an issue; but the act itself (ghasb) is still not adjudged due to the nature of the act in dar al-harb.
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, ghasb is never actionable; under Abu Yusuf, it might be actionable for Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / باب استيلاء الكفار وباب المستأمن
وكذا لو فعل ذلك حربيان وجاءا مستأمنين، فإن جاءا مسلمين قضي بينهما بالدين لا الغصب
[1] (وكذا لو فعل ذلك حربيان وجاءا مستأمنين)؛ لأنه لا ولاية لنا عليهما، (فإن جاء مسلمين قضي بينهما بالدين لا الغصب؛ لأنَّ الإدانة وقعت صحيحةً لتراضيهما بخلاف الغصب؛ لأنه لا تراضي ولا عصمة. والغصب في دار الحرب سبب يفيد الملك؛ لأنه استيلاء على مال مباح غير معصوم، فصار كالادانة، وقال أبو يوسف: يقضي بالدين على المسلم دون الغصب؛ لأنه التزم أحكام الإسلام حيث كان. وأجيب عنه: بأنه إذا امتنع في حق المستأمن امتنع في حق المسلم أيضاً تحقيقاً للتسوية بينهما. ورد عليه: بأن وجوب التسوية بين الخصمين إنّما هو في الإقبال عليهما والإقامة والإجلاس، وغيرها لا في أن يبطل حق أحدهما بلا موجب لموجب إبطال حق الآخر، ألا ترى أنه يقضى بالقصاص للأب على الابن، ولا يقضي به للابن على الأب. إلا أن يقال: انعدام التسوية بين الخصمين بهذا الوجه إنما يمنع إذا كان لقصور ولاية القاضي على أحد الخصمين، كما في مسألة المستأمن مع المسلم، وأما إذا كان بمعنى في أحد المتخاصمين مع كمال ولاية القاضي عليهما فلا يمنع، كذا في حواشي «الهداية»(۱). [١] قوله: ذلك؛ أي الغصب، أو المعاملة بالدين، أو القرض، أو غير ذلك مما يجب فيه شيء لأحدهما على الآخر. [٢] قوله: فإن جاءا؛ يعني إن فعل ذلك كافران في دار الحرب، ثم دخلا دار الإسلام مسلمين وتحاكما إلى القاضي قضى بينهما لتحقق ولايته عليهما حال القضاء. [٣]قوله: بخلاف الغصب؛ يعني إن الغصب لم يوجد فيه التراضي ولا عصمةً لمال الحربي، فإذا غصبه آخر ملكه لوجودِ الاستيلاء على مال مباح، ولا خبث في ملك الحربي حتى يؤمر بالرد، كذا في «الهداية (۳). (۱) «العناية» و«الهداية (٥ : ٢٦٧). (۲) «الهداية» (۲) : ١٥٣).
The Book of Jihad / Section on the Seizure of Disbelievers and Section on the Musta’min
And likewise if two warring [disbelievers] did that and came as musta’mins, then if they come as Muslims, judgment is given between them concerning the debt, not the usurpation. [1] (And likewise if two warring [disbelievers] did that and came as musta’mins) because we have no authority over them. (If they come as Muslims, judgment is given between them concerning the debt, not the usurpation, because the loan occurred validly due to mutual consent, unlike usurpation because there is no consent and no inviolability. Usurpation in dar al-harb is a cause that confers ownership, because it is taking possession of permissible property that is not inviolable, so it became like a loan. Abu Yusuf said: judgment is given concerning the debt upon the Muslim, not the usurpation, because he undertook the laws of Islam where he was. It was answered about him: that if it is prevented with respect to the musta’min, it is prevented with respect to the Muslim as well, to achieve equality between them. And it was objected to him: that the obligation of equality between the litigants is only in approaching them, seating them, etc., not in nullifying the right of one without cause for a cause nullifying the right of the other. Do you not see that retaliation is adjudged for the father against the son, but not for the son against the father? Unless it is said: the lack of equality between litigants in this manner is only prevented if it is due to the judge’s limited authority over one of the litigants, as in the case of the musta’min with the Muslim. But if it is due to a meaning in one of the litigants while the judge has full authority over both, it is not prevented. This is in the glosses of al-Hidayah. [1] His saying: “that”; i.e., usurpation, or dealing with debt, or loan, or other than that in which something is owed by one to the other. [2] His saying: “if they come”; meaning if two disbelievers do that in dar al-harb, then enter dar al-Islam as Muslims and litigate before the judge, he judges between them because his authority is established over them at the time of judgment. [3] His saying: “unlike usurpation”; meaning that in usurpation there is no mutual consent nor inviolability for the property of the harbi, so if another usurps it, he owns it due to taking possession of permissible property, and there is no impurity in the ownership of the harbi such that he would be ordered to return it. This is in al-Hidayah. (1) Al-‘Inayah and al-Hidayah (5:267). (2) Al-Hidayah (2:153).
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school, primarily between Abu Hanifa (represented by the text) and Abu Yusuf. The text begins by stating the ruling: If two harbi (warring disbelievers) commit an act like ghasb or a loan in dar al-harb and then come to dar al-Islam as musta’mins (protected persons) or as Muslims, the qadi will judge between them regarding the debt (loan) but not regarding ghasb. The reason given is that the loan was valid due to mutual consent, whereas ghasb lacks consent and the property of a harbi has no inviolability. In dar al-harb, ghasb causes ownership because it is taking possession of property that is permissible and unprotected. This is analogous to a loan. Abu Yusuf disagrees: when the harbis become Muslims, the qadi should judge the debt against the Muslim (the defendant) but not ghasb, because the Muslim has committed to Islamic law. The author answers that if the qadi cannot judge ghasb against a musta’min, he also cannot judge it against a Muslim, to maintain equality between litigants. A rebuttal is then given: the requirement of equality is about treatment in court (like seating), not about nullifying one’s right to preserve equality. An example is given: retaliation is adjudged for the father against the son but not vice versa, even though they are equal in other respects. The final resolution: the lack of equality in this case is only problematic if due to the judge’s limited authority over one party, as with a musta’min; but if the judge has full authority over both, as with two Muslims, it is not an issue. Thus, the ruling for musta’mins differs from that for Muslims. This is supported by glosses on al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta’min): A non-Muslim who enters dar al-Islam with a safe conduct (aman) from a Muslim authority.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation or wrongful taking of property without consent.
إدانة (idānah): A loan or debt transaction with mutual consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is ghasb (usurpation) not adjudged between two former harbis who become Muslims, while a loan is adjudged?
A) Because ghasb is a sin, while a loan is permissible.
B) Because ghasb causes ownership in dar al-harb due to lack of consent and inviolability, while a loan requires consent.
C) Because the qadi has no jurisdiction over ghasb at all.
D) Because ghasb is a criminal act while a loan is civil.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that ghasb in dar al-harb transfers ownership, making it unenforceable in dar al-Islam, while consensual loans remain actionable.

Killing a Musta'min in Dar al-Harb: Diya, Not Qisas
Ruling with proof: killing a musta'min in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim who kills a musta'min in dar al-harb, explain why qisas is dropped due to shubha, and apply the rule to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Intentional killing of a musta'min in dar al-harb requires diya, not qisas, due to execution impossibility creating shubha.

This passage addresses a unique scenario: a Muslim kills a musta'min (protected non-Muslim) in dar al-harb. The Hanafi school rules that even if intentional, the killer pays diya from his own wealth and does not face retaliation. The key reason is that immediate execution of qisas is impossible in dar al-harb, creating a legal doubt (shubha) that averts the penalty. Let's examine the ruling, its reasoning, and the Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a Muslim killing a musta'min in dar al-harb, either intentionally or accidentally. The ruling is that the killer must pay diya from his own wealth in both cases, and additionally pay kaffarah (expiation) if the killing was accidental.
The ruling applies specifically to musta'min victims, not to Muslim or dhimmi victims in dar al-harb.
2
Understand the core reasoning for dropping qisas
Qisas (retaliation) is not obligatory for intentional killing because its execution is impossible at the time of the killing. Dar al-harb lacks a Muslim authority and military power to enforce qisas. This impossibility creates a legal doubt (shubha).
The principle _al-qisas yudra'u bish-shubah_ (retaliation is averted by doubts) is invoked.
3
Address the potential objection: future possibility
One might argue that even if immediate execution is impossible, qisas could be executed later when the killer enters dar al-Islam. The Hanafi response is that the impossibility at the time of killing creates a shubha that drops qisas entirely; it does not merely postpone it.
The shubha arises from the difficulty at the moment of the act, not from a hypothetical future scenario.
4
Identify the proof and analogical reasoning
The passage cites Quran 4:92, which prescribes diya and kaffarah for accidental killing of a believer. The Hanafi school analogizes the musta'min killing to this verse: in accidental killing, diya is the original obligation; in intentional killing, diya substitutes when qisas is averted. The verse also requires kaffarah for accidental killing.
The verse directly supports the accidental case; the intentional case is covered by the general principle of substituting diya when qisas falls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل مسلم مستأمن مثلَهُ ثَمَّةَ عمداً، أو خطأ، ودي من ماله، وكفر للخطأ)؛ لأنه لم يجب القصاص وقت القتل؛ لتعذُّرِ الاستيفاء؛ لأنَّه بالمنعة، فتجب الدية) [1]قوله: مثله؛ أي مسلماً مستأمنا في دار الحرب. [٢] قوله: عمداً أو خطاً؛ قد مرَّ الفرق بينهما في آخر «باب قطع الطريق» قبيل «كتاب الجهاد»، ومرَّ أيضاً: أن العمد موجبة الإثم والقصاص، والخطأ موجبه الدية على العاقلة والكفارة، وهي المذكورة في القرآن في سورة النساء: من عتق رقبة مؤمنة، وإن لم يقدر عليه فصيام شهرين متتابعين). [٣]قوله: ودي؛ ماض من الديّة: أي أدَّى دية المقتول في الصورتين من ماله، ولا يجب القصاص في صورة العمد، ولا الديّة على العاقلة في صورة الخطأ. [٤] قوله: لأنه بالمنعة؛ أي استيفاء القصاص يكون بالمنعة والقوة، ولا منعة بدون جماعة المسلمين،والإمام، ولم يوجد ذلك في دار الحرب. فإن قلت: القصاص وإن تعدَّرَ استيفاؤه عند القتل لكنه ممكن مآلاً عند دخول القاتل في دار الإسلام، فينبغي أن يجب استيفاؤه؛ لأنه وإن لم يمكن حالاً فقد أمكن مآلاً. قلت: لَمَّا تعذر استيفاؤه عند القتل، وهو الموجب للقصاص أورث ذلك شبهةً، والقصاص مما يندرئ بالشبهة فسقط بالكلية. [0]قوله: فتجب الدية؛ لأنه لَمَّا سقط القصاص في العمد، فلو لم تجب الدية لزم ضياع نفس المقتول من غير عوض، وأما في الخطأ فالدية هو الواجب الأصلي، وكذا في شبه العمد. (1) كما في قوله: وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِنٍ أَن يَقْتُلَ مُؤْمِنًا إِلَّا خَطَا وَمَن قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَا فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَن يَصَدَّقُوا فَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ عَدُوّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِن فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤمِنَةً وَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُم مِّشَقٌ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ. وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٌ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ تَوْبَةً مِّنَ اللَّهِ وَكَانَ اللَّهُ عَلِيمًا حَكِيمًا } [النساء : ١٩٢]
If a Muslim kills a musta’min like him there intentionally or accidentally, he pays the diya from his own wealth, and [pays] kaffarah for accidental. [This is] because retaliation was not obligatory at the time of the killing due to difficulty of execution because it [the execution] requires power, so diya becomes obligatory. [1] His saying: “like him”; i.e., a Muslim musta’min in dar al-harb. [2] His saying: “intentionally or accidentally”; the difference between them has passed in the “Chapter on Highway Robbery” before the “Book of Jihad”, and it also passed that intentional [killing] necessitates sin and retaliation, and accidental requires diya upon the ‘aqila and kaffarah, which is mentioned in the Quran in Surat al-Nisa: freeing a believing slave, and if unable, fasting two consecutive months. [3] His saying: “he pays diya”; past tense of diya: i.e., he pays the diya of the slain in both cases from his own wealth, and retaliation is not obligatory in the case of intentional, nor is diya upon the ‘aqila in the case of accidental. [4] His saying: “because it is by power”; i.e., exacting retaliation is by power and strength, and there is no power without the group of Muslims and the Imam, and that is not found in dar al-harb. If you said: Retaliation, although its execution was difficult at the time of killing, is possible ultimately upon the killer’s entry into dar al-Islam, so it should be obligatory because even if not possible currently, it is possible ultimately. I say: Since its execution was difficult at the time of killing, that which necessitates retaliation (the killing) gives rise to a doubt (shubha), and retaliation is warded off by doubts, so it falls entirely. [0] His saying: “so diya becomes obligatory”; because when retaliation falls in intentional [killing], if diya were not obligatory, the life of the slain would be wasted without compensation. As for accidental, diya is the original obligation, and likewise in quasi-intentional. (1) As in His saying: “It is not for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake. And whoever kills a believer by mistake must free a believing slave and pay blood money to his family unless they remit it. If the deceased belonged to a people at war with you and he was a believer, then [only] the freeing of a believing slave. If he belonged to a people with whom you have a treaty, then blood money paid to his family and the freeing of a believing slave. And whoever does not find [the means] must fast two consecutive months as repentance to Allah. And Allah is All-Knowing, All-Wise.” [al-Nisa 4:92]
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning a Muslim who kills a musta’min (a protected non-Muslim with safe conduct) in dar al-harb. The text states that whether the killing is intentional or accidental, the Muslim must pay the diya (blood money) from his own personal wealth. For accidental killing, he also pays kaffarah (expiation) as per the Quranic verse (freeing a slave or fasting). The reason given is that retaliation (qisas) is not obligatory at the time of the killing because it is impossible to execute in dar al-harb, which lacks a Muslim government and military power to enforce it. The text then addresses a potential objection: although immediate execution is impossible, it might become possible later when the killer enters dar al-Islam, so shouldn’t qisas be obligatory eventually? The answer is that the difficulty at the time of the killing creates a legal doubt (shubha), and the principle is that qisas is averted by doubts. Therefore, qisas is dropped entirely. When qisas is dropped, diya becomes necessary to prevent the wasteful loss of life. For accidental killing, diya is the original obligation anyway. The proof is strengthened by reference to the Quranic verse on accidental killing, which prescribes diya and kaffarah. This ruling applies specifically to musta’mins in dar al-harb; if the victim were a Muslim, the rules would differ, but the text focuses on the musta’min.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta’min): A non-Muslim with safe conduct (aman) to enter or reside in dar al-Islam temporarily.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents application of certain fixed penalties (hudud, qisas).
دية (diya): Blood money, a monetary compensation for homicide or injury, paid to the victim’s heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qisas (retaliation) not required for a Muslim who intentionally kills a musta'min in dar al-harb, according to this passage?
A) Because the killer is a Muslim and the victim is a non-Muslim, so qisas is never applicable.
B) Because immediate execution of qisas is impossible in dar al-harb, creating a legal doubt that prevents qisas.
C) Because the victim’s family has forgiven the killer.
D) Because the musta’min’s life is not protected in dar al-harb.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who kills a musta'min in dar al-harb pays diya from his own wealth and, if accidental, expiates; qisas is dropped due to impossibility of execution creating a shubha.

Killing a Musta'min in Dar al-Harb: Qisas Repelled by Shubhah
Ruling and rationale for killing a musta'min in Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for killing a musta'min in dar al-harb and explain why qisas is repelled by shubhah.

Core Idea
Qisas drops entirely due to shubhah; diyah is wajib from the killer's own wealth.

This lesson examines a specific case from the law of siyar (international law): a Muslim kills a musta'min (a person with safe conduct) in enemy territory. The Ḥanafī ruling is that qisas is not imposed, even for intentional killing, because execution was impossible at the time, creating a legal doubt (shubhah) that repels the punishment. Instead, the killer must pay diyah from his own wealth, and if the killing was mistaken, he must also perform kaffarah. We will unpack the reasoning and the principle of dar' al-shubuhat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing a Muslim musta'min in dar al-harb, whether intentional or mistaken. The ruling: no qisas; diyah wajib from the killer's wealth; plus kaffarah in case of mistake.
The passage specifies that the killer and victim are both Muslim musta'mins in enemy territory.
2
Locate the dalil and its application
The commentator cites Quran 4:92 for the general obligation of diyah and kaffarah in mistaken killing. For intentional killing, the public interest (iḥtiyāṭ li-dam al-Muslim) and the principle that blood must not go unavenged require diyah when qisas is impossible.
The verse directly covers mistaken killing; the ruling for intentional killing is extended by reasoning.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: qisas might be possible later when the killer returns to dār al-Islām. The response: impossibility at the time creates a shubhah, and qisas is repelled by shubuhat; it does not revive later.
This is a classic application of the maxim 'al-ḥudūd tudraʾu bi al-shubahāt'.
4
Understand why diyah is from the killer's wealth, not the ʿāqila
In mistaken killing, the ʿāqila usually pays diyah, but here because the killing occurred in dār al-ḥarb where the ʿāqila has no authority or connection, the obligation shifts to the killer alone.
This is a special rule for cross-dār cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل مسلم مستأمن مثله عمداً أو خطأ، ودي من ماله، وكفر للخطأ)؛ لأنه لم يجب القصاص وقت القتل؛ لتعذُّرِ الاستيفاء؛ لأنَّه بالمنعة، فتجب الدية) [1]قوله : مثله؛ أي مسلماً مستأمنا في دار الحرب. [2] قوله : عمداً أو خطاً؛ قد مرَّ الفرق بينهما في آخر «باب قطع الطريق» قبيل «كتاب الجهاد»، ومرَّ أيضاً: أن العمد موجب للإثم والقصاص، والخطأ موجبه الدية على العاقلة والكفارة، وهي المذكورة في القرآن في سورة النساء: من عتق رقبة مؤمنة، وإن لم يقدر عليه فصيام شهرين متتابعين). [4] قوله : لأنه بالمنعة؛ أي استيفاء القصاص يكون بالمنعة والقوة، ولا منعة بدون جماعة المسلمين، والإمام، ولم يوجد ذلك في دار الحرب. فإن قلت : القصاص وإن تعدَّرَ استيفاؤه عند القتل لكنه ممكن مآلاً عند دخول القاتل في دار الإسلام، فينبغي أن يجب استيفاؤه؛ لأنه وإن لم يمكن حالاً فقد أمكن [؟] قلت : لَمَّا تعذر استيفاؤه عند القتل، وهو الموجب للقصاص أورث ذلك شبهةً، والقصاص مما يندرئ بالشبهة فسقط بالكلية. [0]قوله : فتجب الدية؛ لأنه لَمَّا سقط القصاص في العمد، فلو لم تجب الدية لزم ضياع نفس المقتول من غير عوض، وأما في الخطأ فالدية هو الواجب الأصلي، وكذا في شبه العمد. (1) كما في قوله : وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِنٍ أَن يَقْتُلَ مُؤْمِنًا إِلَّا خَطَا وَمَن قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَا فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَن يَصَدَّقُوا فَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ عَدُوّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِن فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤمِنَةً وَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُم مِّشَقٌ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ. وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٌ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ تَوْبَةً مِّنَ اللَّهِ وَكَانَ اللَّهُ عَلِيمًا حَكِيمًا } [النساء : ١٩٢]
If a Muslim kills a musta'min like himself intentionally or mistakenly, he pays diyah from his wealth and expiates for mistake; because qisas was not obligatory at the time of killing due to difficulty of execution since it requires power; so diyah is obligatory. [1] He said: 'like himself' i.e., a Muslim musta'min in dar al-harb. [2] He said: 'intentionally or mistakenly' — the difference between the two has passed at the end of the chapter on Highway Robbery before the Book of Jihad, and also that intentional killing necessitates sin and qisas, while mistaken killing necessitates diyah on the 'aqilah and expiation, which is mentioned in the Quran in Surah al-Nisa': freeing a believing slave, and if unable, fasting two consecutive months. [3] He said: 'diya' is past tense of diyah: i.e., he pays the diyah of the slain in both cases from his wealth, and qisas is not required in case of intentional killing, nor diyah on the 'aqilah in case of mistake. [4] He said: 'because it requires power' — i.e., execution of qisas requires power and strength, and there is no power without the community of Muslims and the Imam, and that does not exist in dar al-harb. If you say: Although execution of qisas is difficult at the time of killing, it is possible eventually when the killer enters dar al-Islam, so it should be obligatory; because even if not possible immediately, it is possible later. I reply: Since its execution at the time of killing was difficult, which is the cause for qisas, this creates a doubt, and qisas is repelled by doubts, so it falls entirely. [0] He said: 'so diyah becomes obligatory' — because when qisas falls in intentional killing, if diyah were not obligatory, the life of the slain would be wasted without compensation. As for mistaken killing, diyah is the original obligation, and similarly in quasi-intentional killing. (1) As in His saying: 'And it is not for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake, and whoever kills a believer by mistake then freeing a believing slave and a compensation (diyah) delivered to his family unless they give charity. But if he was from a people hostile to you and he is a believer, then freeing a believing slave. And if he was from a people between whom and you is a treaty, then a compensation (diyah) delivered to his family and freeing a believing slave. And whoever does not find [the means] then fasting two consecutive months [as] repentance from Allah. And Allah is ever Knowing and Wise.' [Al-Nisa 4:92]
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the case of a Muslim who kills a musta'min (a person granted aman) in dar al-harb. The ruling states that whether the killing is intentional ('amd) or mistaken (khata'), the killer must pay the diyah from his own wealth, and in the case of mistake, he must also perform kaffarah (expiation). No qisas is imposed for intentional killing. The commentator explains each phrase: 'like himself' means a Muslim musta'min in the enemy territory; 'intentionally or mistakenly' distinguishes the two categories of killing; 'diya' means he pays the diyah from his own wealth—not from the 'aqilah (kinsmen) even in mistake. The reason qisas is not required is that execution of qisas requires power and authority, which do not exist in dar al-harb without the Muslim community and Imam. The commentator anticipates an objection: even if immediate execution is impossible, it might become possible later when the killer returns to dar al-Islam. The response is that the inability at the time of killing creates a legal doubt (shubhah), and the principle is that punishments (hudud) and qisas are repelled by doubts. Therefore qisas drops entirely. Without qisas, diyah becomes mandatory to prevent the life from being wasted, as per the principle that the blood of a Muslim must not go unavenged. The passage also references the Quranic verse on diyah and kaffarah for mistaken killing (al-Nisa 4:92), which serves as the foundational text for the obligation of diyah in such cases. The commentary follows the Hanafi position that diyah in dar al-harb is from the killer's wealth, not on the 'aqilah, because the 'aqilah's obligation is based on their connection and ability to provide protection, which is absent across different dar.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person (Muslim or non-Muslim) who enters dar al-harb with an aman (safe conduct).
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of unlawful killing or injury.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of a fixed punishment (hadd) or qisas.
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of paternal relatives (or the state treasury/divan) who are obligated to pay diyah in cases of mistaken killing.
منعة (man'ah): Power, authority, or protection; the ability to enforce judicial punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does diyah in this case come from the killer's wealth rather than the 'aqilah?
A) Because the killing was intentional
B) Because the 'aqilah is not present in dar al-harb
C) Because the killer is not liable otherwise
D) Because the victim was a musta'min

Key Takeaway
When qisas cannot be executed due to lack of authority at the time of killing, the resulting shubhah repels qisas permanently and diyah becomes wajib from the killer's own wealth.
